# Igros Kodesh

## Volume 1

## 1

21 Adar, 5698
Riga

To the honorable Rav, famous for his name and praise, expert in the chambers of Torah, a sealed treasure and a storehouse of wisdom, prince of Torah, judge and teacher, the captain in the sea of the Talmud and guide to those who carry its vessels, etc.,
Moreinu HaRav R’ Y. Rozin shlita,

Peace and blessing!

Your letter has reached me. Regarding the science of astronomy, we find much in the *Yad HaChazakah* of the Rambam z”l concerning this field of knowledge. It appears that the Rambam z”l also relied upon the books of the scholars of the nations of the world in this regard, as he writes concerning the calculations in *Hilchos Kiddush HaChodesh*, chapter 17, halacha 25. Furthermore, what he writes in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*, chapter 3, is also found in the books of the Greek scholars, specifically the books of Aristotle and his students and commentators.

I turn to your honor to clarify the approach of our Sages z”l in this science, particularly in reconciling the most contradictory passages, such as *Berachos 58a*, *Pesachim 94a*—where there is a dispute between the Sages of Israel and the scholars of the nations of the world, and [the Gemara concludes] "their words appear [more correct] than ours," etc.—as well as *Chagigah 12a*, *Yoma 20b*, *Bava Basra 25a*, and *Bava Basra 74a*.

Most of all, I ask and request that you explain, as much as can be put into writing, the approach of our Sages z”l regarding astronomy as brought in the Shas, Tosefta, etc., concerning the spheres, the movements of the sun and the moon, the four elements, and the fifth element from which the spheres, stars, etc., are formed.

I remain, seeking your welfare and honoring you according to your lofty stature,

**Menachem Schneerson**

Enclosed is a stamp for the reply.

**My address:**
M. Schneersohn
Riga, Poste Restante

## 2

B"H, 28 Teves, 5689
Riga

To my dear and esteemed relative, the G-d-fearing man, one who engages in communal needs with faith, etc.,
R' Zelik, may he live.

Greetings and abundant peace!

Your letter of blessing and congratulations has reached me. I also recently received the wedding gift, the two-volume set of the book *Yismach Moshe*. I thank you from the depths of my heart, and for your blessings and the warmth of your heart.

And as in water, face reflects face, may you and your household, may they live, be blessed with an abundance of life, blessing, and peace, and may you succeed in all your endeavors. May only goodness and kindness pursue you, in body and soul, with tranquility, happiness, and wealth. As the blessing of your relative who seeks your welfare and honors you,

**M. Schneersohn**

My wife, may she live, sends her regards and conveys her thanks for your blessings.

#### ***2***

Published in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 20, p. 573. A facsimile of the original manuscript is printed in *Migdal Oz*, p. 25.

**R' Zelik:** Slonim.

**Congratulations:** Regarding the wedding of the Rebbe shlita and the Rabbanit shlita on 14 Kislev.

**Wedding gift:** (Yiddish: *droshe geshank*)

3

B"H, 21 Adar Rishon, 5692

To the Rebbe, my master and teacher, Shlita!

...In the handwritten notebook of the Tzemach Tzedek on Mishnayos, which I received as a gift from the Rebbe Shlita, it is noted in the indices that the tractate of Makkos states: "He is not liable unless he removes it with a razor," yet inside the text, nothing is found regarding this Mishnah. It appears to be in the second notebook on Mishnayos, or perhaps in the printed volume on Mishnayos. I have been unable to obtain it. Perhaps it is found there in the *chiddushim* [novellae] that pertain to all of the above.

From the proofs of the Tzemach Tzedek in his *Responsa*, it is evident that he holds that even with scissors (and even not in the manner of a razor), it is forbidden by Torah law, and not only due to the prohibition of "a man shall not wear," but also because of the *pe'os* of the beard. However, because of the prohibition of "a man shall not wear," one is also liable for lashes.

I found in the *Drush* "Ta'ar HaSechira," section 6, that he writes regarding a *Nazir*: "And it is more severe than shaving the beard of any person, which is not forbidden by Torah law except with a razor, unlike a *Nazir*..." I initially thought to equate the two places, assuming his intent in the *Drush* "Ta'ar HaSechira" was regarding the prohibition of Torah-level lashes, that a *Nazir* is liable even for scissors, similar to a razor, unlike a beard. But this is not clear from his language, where he writes "which is not forbidden by Torah law," etc. Perhaps there is an earlier and later version here, and which one did the Rebbe revise in his later years?

In any case, even according to what is written in the aforementioned *Drush* of *Chassidus*—that the prohibition of destroying the *pe'os* of the beard by Torah law is only specifically with a razor—since it is forbidden by Rabbinic law at the very least, it is therefore not the way of men to remove it. Consequently, the prohibition of "a man shall not wear" applies to one who removes his beard with scissors (even not in the manner of a razor), as the text there discusses only the prohibition of shaving hair and the sanctity of hair.

In the index of the printed *Derech Mitzvosecha*, Vol. 2, it is recorded that the *Drush* "Ta'ar HaSechira" is from the year 5617, but I do not know when the *Responsa* were written, especially this particular *Responsa*. Certainly, there are other places where the prohibition of shaving the beard not with a razor is discussed, and it is impossible that there is no *chiddush* there. I am very eager to know all of this, along with the content of the matter.

I ask for your forgiveness for the delay in my letter, as obtaining the books here requires time. There are books I hoped to obtain, but in the end, I could not find them as of yet, and because of this, I have been delayed this much.

I take my leave of the Rebbe with a request for forgiveness once again, and I remain your son-in-law, seeking your blessing, and blessed by the Rebbe.

**Menachem**

**Notes, tentatively, for myself and those like me, on the Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek, Yoreh Deah, Section 93:**

**Whether one must forbid shaving the beard with scissors in the manner of a razor, and even not in the manner of a razor.**

**Section 1, at the beginning.** It should be explained: The law of the hair of the armpit and the pubic area is different, as it is permitted to remove them with scissors in the manner of a razor—according to Rav (Nazir 58b)—and [only] plucking white hairs from among black ones is forbidden. This is because plucking white hairs on one’s head or beard is in a place that is noticeable, unlike the armpit and pubic area.

Seemingly, this is difficult. If so, even with a razor it should be permitted to remove hair from the armpit and pubic area, since it is not noticeable, which is not comparable to plucking white hairs. It is even more difficult if we say that the fact that even with plucking and a *rehitni* (a tool), it is forbidden for Rav to remove hair from the armpit—according to the *Shitah Mekubetzes* cited later—is a prohibition by Torah law (and from the language of the Tzemach Tzedek later, in the section beginning "And again," where he lists the law of plucking and a *rehitni* in a separate paragraph and does not mix it with the law of a razor, it implies that the matter is not clear to him). If so, the law of the armpit and pubic area is equal to the law of plucking white hairs. What, then, is the reason for the Tzemach Tzedek’s question and his answer that the armpit is not noticeable? Furthermore, to distinguish based on the reasoning of the *Tosafos* cited in this section—that Rav permits it because it is done for pain and not for beauty—for this reason, even with a razor it should be permitted to remove it, as is written in *Tosafos*.

It is possible to say: The reason for the distinction between scissors and a razor, even though the prohibition is due to "a man shall not wear"—and especially according to the *Tosafos* (later, Section 2) that scissors in the manner of a razor is close to the skin, but does not uproot the hair from its source like a razor—is because it is not considered "women's adornment," as it was not the way of women to remove hair from the armpit and pubic area with scissors (whether because the trace of the hairs is somewhat noticeable to one standing close by, at least—see later, Section 10, in the section "And another reason"—or for some other reason), and they would only remove it with a chemical or a razor. As it is found in *Moed Katan* (9b and elsewhere, cited later in Section 9), "She passes a chemical (*srak*) over her face below," and in the *Rif* it is written, "Some say they pass a knife over the face below." Likewise in the *Yerushalmi Moed Katan* (Chapter 1, Halacha 7), "And she passes a clay tool over her face below." All of this is removal with a razor.

The Tzemach Tzedek holds that in matters of Torah law, where it is possible to distinguish between what is done in the way of women and what is not, it is only in a place that is not noticeable. In a place that is not noticeable, the prohibition of "wearing a woman's garment" (which leads to abomination) does not apply, since it is not noticeable. Also, one is not liable for "adornment in women's adornments," because it is not women's adornment to remove it with scissors. But if it is a place that is noticeable, what difference does it make in what manner he removed the hair? Since, in the end, a woman's garment and women's adornments are upon him (and it leads to abomination). And it is not comparable to a place that is not noticeable, where the prohibition is because he adorns himself with women's adornments, and therefore Rav only forbade it with a razor, as that is truly women's adornment, and not with scissors.

It is possible to say that this is also what is precise in the language of the *Rambam* (*Hilchos Avodah Zarah*, Chapter 12, Halacha 7), who wrote regarding the armpit and pubic area, "that he should not adorn himself with women's adornment," but regarding plucking white hairs, he gave the reason, "he is liable, because he wears women's adornments." For in the armpit, it is not applicable to say "he wears women's adornments," since it is a place that is not noticeable (except that the *Rambam* follows his own view, that for armpit hair there is no prohibition except by Rabbinic law, and the Sages forbade it in any manner, even with scissors not in the manner of a razor).

However, from all the above, we arrive at a new law: that removing hair from the armpit and pubic area with a chemical has the same status as removal with a razor. This requires further study.

See later, at the end of Section 8, where he writes according to the *Rambam* that armpit and pubic hair is only forbidden by Rabbinic law (even with a razor) because it is a hidden place. But plucking on his head or beard, which is in public, is forbidden by Torah law. He refers to his words here.

**Ibid.** The distinction of *Tosafos* in *Yevamos* (48a), in the section "No," and *Tosafos* in the section "He asks" in *Nazir* (59b).

Explained in *Shabbos* (50b).

**Section 1.** According to Rav, one is liable by Torah law for removing hair from the armpit and pubic area with a razor.

This is also written at the end of Section 9. This is also the superficial implication of the *Shas* passage. But according to the conclusion of the *Shas* that answers—according to the first explanation of Rabbi Yehuda—that one is liable by Rabbinic law, and *Tosafos* explains in the section "Rav said" (*Nazir* 58b) that it is easier for him to answer so that the *braisos* do not contradict each other. If so, it is possible to say that even according to Rav, one is only liable by Rabbinic law. This is also written later in the "Way to say" (Section 6), and from the language of *Tosafos* (in the section "Rav said" mentioned above), it seems that Rabbi Yehuda disagrees with Rav. It appears he holds that in both he disagrees with Rav: that he forbids it even with scissors, and that he holds one liable with a razor by Torah law.

**Ibid., in the section "And again."** Rather, the explanation—in the *Responsa of the Geonim*—is that he shaves all the hair of his head except for the *pe'os*, etc.

It must also be said that the Tzemach Tzedek does not come to explain the view of the *Geonim* according to *Tosafos*, but according to his own view (even though it is forced to settle this in his language here and in *Piskei Dinim Yoreh Deah*, Section 82). For if the explanation of *Tosafos* in the *Responsa of the Geonim* is that he shaves also the *pe'os*, and as the Tzemach Tzedek proves, since he wants to say in *Tosafos* that it discusses a *metzora*, and a *metzora* shaves also the *pe'os*, it must be that it discusses one who shaves also the *pe'os*. This is also proven from what *Tosafos* concludes there: "And know that it did not escape," etc., for there is no proof from this, except for one who shaves all his hair and also the *pe'os*. One should not raise a difficulty according to this explanation: "But (to everyone) it is forbidden to shave the *pe'os*?" For it is possible to say that in *Tosafos* itself he is careful about this, and he thought to answer that it refers to a *metzora*, who overrides the prohibition of "you shall not do" and "you shall not do," and he pushes and concludes—to answer this difficulty—that it is one who shaves for medical reasons (in such a sick person who overrides the negative commandment), and regarding this the *Geonim* wrote that even though the doctors did not command him to shave the armpit and pubic hair because of his illness, it is not forbidden to remove it because it is a disfigurement for him, since he removed all his hair and also the *pe'os* (because of his illness). And even without the proof of *Tosafos*, the Tzemach Tzedek is not comfortable explaining it like the *Shach* in the *Responsa of the Geonim*. Firstly, the language "his entire body from his head to his feet" implies that his head is also included. And secondly, so that the difficulty of the *Yam Shel Shlomo*, the *Bach*, and the *Sedei Chemed* that he brought at the end of this section would not arise.

**Section 2.** "And so it is ruled in *Shulchan Aruch Even HaEzer*, Section 156, Paragraph 13, 'so that they are removed with scissors' is the great measure of all of them."

A printing error; it should be "so that they bend their heads to their roots," etc.

**Section 2, at the end.** And so it is implied further in *Tosafos*, in the section "We see."

I have not merited to understand his proof, for the language "We see" for Rabbi Yehuda, who says "he has none," implies that he has no hairs at all—and also from what he answers, that they fell out due to old age, it implies that they fell out completely—and if so, he could not have answered that he removed them with scissors not in the manner of a razor, for then some length of the hairs would have remained, and he saw that he had none at all, and so the *Rosh* explains. If so, where does *Tosafos* get the proof from this that even with scissors it is forbidden? Rather, it must be that *Tosafos* only comes to prove from scissors in the manner of a razor, and they follow their view that scissors in the manner of a razor is close to the skin itself (and not like the *Rambam*. See also *Rashi Makkos* 21a, in the section "The Torah said"). If so, it is not noticeable if he removed it with a razor or with scissors in the manner of a razor (just as Rav says regarding scissors in the manner of a razor, he is lenient with a razor). And therefore he should have answered that he removed it with scissors in the manner of a razor, and since he did not say so, *Tosafos* proved that even with scissors in the manner of a razor it is forbidden. This requires further study.

Also, the proof of *Tosafos* is difficult, for he could have answered with something better (that even with scissors in the manner of a razor, the trace of the hairs is noticeable to one standing close by, as mentioned above). Furthermore, he answers him according to the truth, that they fell out due to old age (except that regarding this, it is possible to say that the proof is from the fact that he thought to raise a difficulty). And it is not in the implication of the language of the Tzemach Tzedek, who brings a proof from the silence of the language of *Tosafos* in the section "We see," which did not explain "with scissors in the manner of a razor," and in the *Piskei Dinim* he did not bring a proof, except from *Tosafos* in the section "That one" and *Tosafos* in the section "He asks."

**Section 3.** And behold, *Rashi* and *Tosafos* in *Nazir* there explain that Rabbi Yehuda forbids even with scissors, as stated above. It must be that he holds not like the explanation of *Rashi* and *Tosafos* of *Nazir*, but that Rabbi Yehuda only forbids the removal of hair with a razor, etc.

This requires study, for it is not proven from *Rashi* at all, and one should not bring a proof from the explanation of *Rashi* except that **for the one who forbids** with scissors—even if not in the manner of a razor it is forbidden, as mentioned above in Section 2, or—that Rabbi Yehuda forbids even with scissors.

**Section 6.** And in our *Bahag* it is written, "And did not Rav Yehuda say in the name of Rav," etc. From this it is proven like this version, except when Rav says, etc., that according to this, in any case he permitted it, and therefore he asks, etc.

This requires study in the *Bahag*, for according to this version, what does he answer? "He is liable by Rabbinic law"? But Rav completely permitted it with scissors in any case! And it is forced to say that his intent is to answer that in any case there will not be a far-fetched disagreement (that according to Rav it would be permitted even by Rabbinic law, and according to Rabbi Yehuda—it is forbidden by Torah law). See above at the end of Section 3, and later in Section 9.

**Beginning of Section 7.** And if so, the same applies to the beard, not like the *Responsa Noda B'Yehuda*, Second Edition, end of Chapter 1, and see *Lechem Mishneh*, Chapter 5 of *Hilchos Avodah Zarah*, Chapter 12, etc.

See *Pachad Yitzchak*, entry "Pe'os," and *Responsa Chaim Sha'al*—by the *Chida*—Part 1, Section 50, who permit it even with scissors in the manner of a razor, but with a chemical it is better. And the difficulty of the *Lechem Mishneh*, in searching for a source for the law of the *Rambam*, regarding the distinction between a chemical and other removers, the *Rambam* answered in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, Positive Commandment 92 (cited later, Section 215 [16]), that this distinction is explained in the *Mechilta*. And in *Zayis Ra'anan*—on the *Yalkut Shimoni*—he explains the reason that it is lenient with a chemical because the hairs do not fall out immediately. He also answered the difficulty of the *Lechem Mishneh* in the *Radbaz*, *Sefer Batei Kehunah*, Part "Beis Va'ad," *Orach Mishor* at the end of Tractate *Nazir*, *Pnei Moshe* in the *Yerushalmi Nazir* (apparently his intent is *Mareh HaPanim*, Chapter 6, Halacha 3, in the section "All"), and in the book *Merkheves HaMishneh* [Responsa *Chaim Sha'al*, Part 1, Section 50].

**Section 7.** But it is difficult, for if so, Rav should have taught us a greater novelty, etc.

He follows his own view, for according to the reasoning of the *Yam Shel Shlomo*, it is the opposite. End of this section.

**Ibid.** This is not so, for the *Rambam* wrote that even the destruction of the *pe'os* of the beard is forbidden because the idolaters do so, etc.

And later at the end of Section 9, that it is so in the *Midrash*, *Toras Kohanim*. And behold, besides the fact that seemingly the distinction is correct that in the prohibition of "a man shall not wear," which is forbidden because he wears a woman's garment, if so, what does it matter if he wore it in his way—with a razor, or with scissors—it is forbidden, in the end, it is a garment on him. And the *Tanna Kamma* of Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira will prove it, for he holds him liable only when he goes and sits among the women afterward, and if so, there is no practical difference. For in both cases it is an abomination if he sits afterward among the women, but in the destruction of the *pe'os* of the beard, for even according to the *Rambam*, the prohibition is in the destruction of the beard, as he wrote: "The way of the idolaters was to destroy their beards, therefore the Torah forbade destroying the beard," etc. And since he did not destroy—for there is no destruction and shaving except with a razor—he is exempt. And besides all this, I have not merited to understand: do we equate the reason the *Rambam* gave—or also the *Midrashic* exegesis—that we should learn the details of the law through it, that it is not forbidden except specifically where the reason is applicable, to that which "a man shall not wear a woman's garment," which is the essence of the prohibition, that he should be like a woman, and it is not a reason to forbid, but an explanation and clarification of the prohibition? But in the *pe'os* of the beard, the prohibition is explained in the verse: "destruction of the *pe'os* of the beard," and only (the *Rambam* gave) a reason for this, because it is the way of the priests, etc. And the *Rambam* himself will prove it, for he permitted it in the *pe'os* of the head with scissors (*Hilchos Avodah Zarah*, Chapter 12, Halacha 8), even though also in the *pe'os* of the head he explained the reason, because of the way of the idolaters (beginning of Chapter 12 there), and in the hair of the armpit and pubic area it is forbidden even with scissors—see the content of the *Responsa*, Section 7.

And an example of the matter that one does not rely on the reason for the commandment, and even if it is an exegesis of the *Shas*. In *Shulchan Aruch*, Section 183, there is no distinction between an unmarried woman and a married woman regarding the prohibition of a *niddah*. And in *Niddah* 66: "It was taught, Rabbi Meir said, why did the Torah say..." (and one should study *Turei Even* and *Minchas Chinuch* there).

And in the *Darkei Moshe* and *Taz*, Section 181, they explained that in this, the *Tur* disagrees with the *Rambam*, that we should not expound to say that the Torah only forbade it in the way that the idolaters did, since the reason is not explicit in the verse. But in truth, it is not necessary to say that the *Rambam* would hold so. And it has already been explained in the *Beis Yosef*, *Bach*, etc., the words of the *Rambam* in a different way. And even according to the explanation of the *Darkei Moshe* and *Taz*, it is possible to say that the *Tur* does not come to say anything except that we should not rule so based on the words of the *Rambam*, but it is not clear to him that this is the view of the *Yad* (Rambam).

**Section 8.** Except that here one should not mention this prohibition, etc.

He answers with this that which is not mentioned in the Mishnah of *Makkos* (20a), also the negative commandment of "a man shall not wear." And see the end of this section. And it requires study in his language here, and later where he wrote, "And if so, according to this," etc., that he is not liable by Torah law because of the negative commandment of the *pe'os* of the beard, one should hold him liable, etc., which is like a **branch** of *Chassidus* by Torah law, etc. And this requires study.

**End of Section 8.** And according to this, it must be said what is written in the Mishnah, etc.

And so it must be answered also the *Toras Kohanim*, Parshas *Metzora*, cited later at the beginning of Section 108 [19].

And for the first answer, he could have said in the *Tosefta* of *Makkos* (Chapter 4, Halacha 4) also regarding the hairs of the beard, there is one who plucks the armpit and transgresses because of four, etc., and excludes a *Nazir* and includes "a man shall not wear."

**Section 9.** However, it is possible to say that the removal of hair is also applicable to a woman, etc. Know, etc.

Seemingly, it is not a complete proof from the law of plucking, for plucking white hairs from among black ones is for one reason, both in a woman and in a man, on the head and also in the beard, and it is so that he should appear young, unlike the removal of hair, for in a woman, where we find the removal of hair in the armpit and pubic area, it is because it is beauty for her, but in a man, in the removal of the hair of the beard, it is so that he should appear young, and not because of beauty, for on the contrary, it is beauty for him when he has a beard, as it is found in *Bava Metzia* (84a). And later in Section 10, he grasped the essence as the second reason below, and furthermore, since in any case it is for the woman's beauty, etc.

**Section 9.** For in *Tosafos* it is not so, from what he wrote that at the very least it is forbidden with scissors by Rabbinic law, etc.

Apparently, his proof is only from the silence of the language of *Tosafos*, for otherwise it is possible to say that *Tosafos* only says that one cannot bring a proof from the *Shas* except that there is a prohibition also with scissors, but one cannot prove that it is forbidden by Torah law, and also the language "at the very least" proves it somewhat.

**Section 10.** And not like the *Responsa Maharam Alashkar*, Section 35, etc.

See—at length regarding this—the *Responsa* of the author in *Choshen Mishpat*, Section 39.

**End of Section 10.** And without this, in the matter of the *pe'os* of the beard, etc., in the *pe'os* of the beard, etc.

A printing error. It should be in both places "the *pe'os* of the head," etc.

**Section 14 [15].** Seemingly, the proofs he brought in this section from the verses of the elders of Israel, that of Rabbi Avraham ibn Ezra in *Berachos* (12b and 28a), in *Bereishis Rabbah*, end of Chapter 58, and *Bava Metzia* (87a), do not deal with anything except the importance of the **whiteness** of the hairs, and not the importance of the hairs themselves. And it is like a proof to the contrary. And in *Chiddushei Aggados* in *Bava Metzia* there, he wrote that Yitzchak was also with a beard, etc., and therefore Avraham asked for mercy, etc., for the whiteness of the hair, etc., and if so, Avraham had a beard even before the Holy One said to him, "Wear garments," and it is forced that it refers to garments **for the whiteness** of the hairs.

Also in the verse "And the Ancient of Days," upon which the *Me'or Einayim* relies in his explanation in *Bereishis Rabbah* there (and it is in *Midrash Tanchuma*), a beard is not mentioned at all, and from the *Me'or Einayim* it implies that he explains "Wear garments," that just as the Holy One is called "Ancient of Days," so it is said about Avraham, "an old man, advanced in days." And also for those who have the versions and additions in *Midrash Tanchuma* at the end of this chapter and the beginning of the next chapter, which brings also the end of the verse "the Ancient of Days did sit," it must be that their intent is only for the appearance of the whiteness of "his garments," etc., and "the hair of his head," etc.

Likewise, the proof of this section that "if so, the commandment of 'before the hoary head' is nullified," etc., I have not merited to understand. For according to your reasoning, the commandment of "you shall honor the face of an elder" refers specifically to one who has acquired wisdom, and this is not noticeable in the exterior, except that it must be said that it refers to those who know that he is a sage, and if so, the beginning of the verse "before the hoary head," etc., is also established in this way.

One cannot answer that he brings a proof only from the simplicity of the Scripture, and according to its simple meaning, "face of an elder" is also in years. For this is not so, for according to Rabbi Yehuda the Galilean (*Kiddushin* 32b), whose law we follow, the "elder" of the verse means "a young sage," as he concludes in *Tosafos* in the section "What" (32b)—and not like *Rashi* in the section "And Rabbi Yehuda the Galilean"—and also according to the *Tanna Kamma* who disagrees with Rabbi Yehuda the Galilean, in any case we need two things: an elder in years and also in wisdom, and if so, even according to him, we are dealing with those who know him, and if so, we will explain "hoary head" in this way too.

And it is a great strain to say that his proof is according to the simple meaning, that regarding an elder it is written "you shall honor," which means showing favor and respect, and this is in his hand to fulfill, after he reaches him and asks him if he is a Torah scholar—see *Nedarim* (62a), "Rabbi said, it is permitted for a person to make himself known," etc., and the *Rosh* explains, so that they do not belittle the honor of his Torah and be punished—but "before the hoary head you shall rise," which is at a distance of four cubits, it must be that it needs to be noticeable from afar, and it is possible only through the beard.

And perhaps it is possible to say that since a Torah scholar must be noticeable in his clothing and his walk (*Rambam*, *Hilchos De'os*, Chapter 5), even if one does not know him, he will know if a Torah scholar is coming toward him (see *Chullin* 91a, "He appears to him like a Torah scholar").

**Note.**

**A.** It should be noted, from the view of those who say (*Sefer Chassidim*, Section 578, and *Responsa Beis Yehuda*, Yoreh Deah, Section 26. And not like *Halachos Ketanos*, Part 1, 159, who wrote that it is reasonable that it is not necessary) that it is a commandment to rise also before an old woman. The books are not at hand to study them, for where do we have to distinguish between an old man and an old woman? I am astonished.

**B.** In the *Sefer HaMitzvos* of the *Rambam*, he wrote: "Commandment 209 is that He commanded us to honor the sages and to rise before them and to exalt them, and it is His saying, may He be blessed, 'before the hoary head,' etc., 'honor the face of an elder,' etc." And he did not mention a simple elder at all, even though he elaborated there on the details of the honor of a Torah scholar. Likewise, at the beginning of *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, he wrote: "Included in them are two positive commandments: 1) to learn Torah, 2) to honor its teachers and those who know it," and inside there, Chapter 6, Halacha 1, he wrote: "Every Torah scholar, it is a commandment to honor him," etc., but in Halacha 8: "One who is an elder," etc., "they rise before him," etc., and he did not mention the word "commandment" (and see the *Kesef Mishneh* there. And in *Shulchan Aruch*, Yoreh Deah, Section 244: "**It is a positive commandment** to rise before every sage," etc., and likewise "**it is a commandment** to rise before the hoary head," etc. And see the *Ramban* on the Torah, *Vayikra* 19:32). And it implies that the essence of the positive commandment—even according to Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira—is specifically for a Torah scholar (and this requires study, the language of the *Shas*: "hoary head," every hoary head is implied). And if so, the proof from above is gone. And as a sort of support to strengthen that the *Rambam* holds that the commandment in its essence is only the honor of a Torah scholar, is what he wrote in *Moreh Nevuchim*, Part 3, Chapter 36: "The commandments," etc., "which we have counted in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*," etc., "and the honor of the sages of the Torah," etc., "and included in this commandment is also to conduct oneself with modesty and humility," etc., "they said, 'before the hoary head you shall rise'," etc.

**C.** It requires study in *Likutei Torah*, *Shir HaShirim* (the discourse on "I am black," end of Section 67), where he wrote: "And this is what we hold in *Kiddushin* 32b, that 'before the hoary head you shall rise' means an elder **and a sage**."

**D.** Regarding the praise and virtue of the elder, it should be noted from *Shabbos* (154a), *Bava Metzia* (84a), *Bereishis Rabbah*, Chapter 11. *Ta'anis* (15a), *Chullin* (24b), *Maseches Sofrim* (Chapter 14, Halacha 17, and the version of the *Gra* there).

**Section 19 [20].** And see the *Ran* in *Sanhedrin*, etc., "a decree lest the scissors break."

Were I not afraid, I would say that all this is printed in this section by mistake, and its place is above in Section 17 [18] at the beginning, for there he proved that the haircut of "you shall clip" is with scissors.

**Section 20 [21].** And what is written in *Tur Orach Chaim*, etc., "and they shave their beards," etc.

So is also the version of the *Rosh* at the end of Tractate *Rosh Hashanah*. And in the book *Binah Le'itim*, Discourse 2, he brings the version of the *Yerushalmi*, "and they shave their hair," etc.

In the *Sedei Chemed*, he raised a difficulty further on the prohibition of shaving the beard even with scissors, from *Rashi* in *Kerisus* (5a), in the section "Scissors," who explains in another language that he removes it with a razor. And also regarding this, it is possible to say that this is excluding the five *pe'os*, and so that the scissors, in the way of their movement, would not cause the drops to rise, etc. Except that it requires study, according to the explanation of the Tzemach Tzedek that the shaven-bearded ones of Yirmiyahu (II Samuel 10) also refers to not in the place of the *pe'os*, and it is a sign of mourning, if so, why did Aharon remove it with a razor? (And from the end of the content of the *Responsa*, it appears that the Tzemach Tzedek holds that the custom has not changed from then until now). And perhaps it is possible to distinguish, that here it is with scissors and here it is with a razor.

**Content of the Responsa at the end.** And the *Radak* there wrote, etc., according to his words, it is a custom that our ancestors practiced from the time of the receiving of the Torah, etc.

It requires study, for according to the *Radak*, mourners would shave their beards even then. Also, the entire land of Italy and the cities of Edom would shave their beards,

## 4

B"H, 23 Adar Rishon, 5692

To my revered father-in-law, the Admur, shlita,

In addition to my letter regarding the prohibition of shaving the beard...

In my previous letter, I wrote—referring to section 14 [15] of the Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek—that there are authors who cite the Midrash Tanchuma at the end of the parsha and the beginning of the following parsha, and who also include the end of the verse, "the Ancient of Days did sit." This was an error on my part, as I wrote it based on memory. In fact, the version of the Midrash Tanchuma is that they do include the end of the aforementioned verse. And regarding this, one could say, in my humble opinion, what I wrote in my letter.

With this, I remain your son-in-law, seeking your blessing, and blessed by you,

**Menachem**

## 5

B”H, 3rd of Menachem Av, 5692

To the honorable, the Gaon, famous for his name and praise, expert in the chambers of Torah, master of the harvest, who arranges and analyzes [the laws], greater than his teacher,
Moreinu HaRav R’ Y. shlita,

Blessings and peace!

May our teacher instruct us regarding the law of *hefker* (abandoned property) in the *Yovel* year: does it depart from the hand of the one who acquired it from the *hefker* and return to the hand of the original owner, etc.?

Also, what is the reason for the Rambam in his Commentary on the Mishnah, Niddah (4:2), stating that the years of the female for the season of vows are less than the years of the male because their lives are shorter, etc., and not like the reasoning of the *Shas* there? And furthermore, what is the reason that their lives are shorter, etc.?

Respecting him according to the greatness and height of his stature,

**M. Schneerson**

#### ***5***

Printed in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 17, p. 521. *Kovetz Yagdil Torah* (Jerusalem), Issue 9, p. 10 (and in the footnotes there, see the notes transcribed below), and proofread according to a photocopy of the original holy handwriting.

**Moreinu HaRav R’ Y.:** Rozin, the Rogatchover Gaon. Another letter to him — above, 1.

**Hefker in Yovel:** See in the book *Rachash Levav* [by R’ Chaim Shmuel Birnbaum, son-in-law of R’ Akiva Eiger], Jerusalem 5719, in siman 9-10, what is written regarding this.

**Because their lives are shorter:** This is also explained by the Ibn Ezra (on the verse in Vayikra 21:4), and the reason for mentioning the mother before the father is because the male lives longer than the female, etc.

**What is the reason:** In a similar vein, a question is raised in the book *Maaseh Rokeach* on the Rambam, Hilchos Nedarim, Chapter 11. He writes there to explain that the Rambam’s intention must be to provide a reason for the words of R’ Chaim, that the reason the Holy One, Blessed be He, gave the woman extra *binah* is because their lives are shorter. See also the *Chiddushei Chasam Sofer* on the *Shas* there and the sources cited therein.

B.H., 3rd of 12 Kislev, 5698
Paris

To the honorable Rabbi, Chassid, G-d-fearing, wise, and understanding, etc.,
Rabbi Y. Dzikobsohn, may he live:

Blessings and much peace!

I waited, at the time, for your letter informing me of your safe journey and arrival home. Subsequently, I learned that such information had reached my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita. I am now writing to you with a request, the content of which is as follows: At the beginning of the month of Tammuz 5697, a check for two hundred dollars was received here—sent to my address—from D. Potashnik in Baltimore, for my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita.

Now, the bank upon which the check was drawn has gone bankrupt, and its heirs have declared insolvency and are currently involved in legal proceedings and litigation.

In order to strengthen our claims here, we require either the check itself (which is either at Potashnik’s bank, or has already been returned to Potashnik and is in his possession) or, at the very least, if the aforementioned is not possible, a photograph of the check **on both sides**. The sooner this is done, the better.

Therefore, I request that you get in touch, either personally or through an attorney, with Potashnik, and endeavor to have him send the check here as soon as possible, or at the very least the photograph, as well as notification of the date the check was cashed in the United States.

I am not writing directly to D. Potashnik because I do not know him.

To expedite all of this, I am writing from here, rather than through the office in Otwock.

I rely on your understanding to handle this matter in the best possible way, and I thank you in advance.

I will take this opportunity to remind you regarding the *maamarim* of the past *Chag HaShavuos* that you borrowed from me, and surely you will return them to me now. I would also be grateful if you could send me a few copies of the *maamar* given to you in Perchtoldsdorf (which you surely have in print, as you mentioned in Vienna).

You promised me in Vienna that upon arriving home safely, you would endeavor to obtain the transcripts—or a copy of them—of *"Ashkavta DeRebbe,"* and also to look into whether there are any transcripts of *sichos* and *maamarim* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita, from his time in the United States. I am certain you will fulfill this promise as well.

I will conclude with wishes for all the best, and I remain awaiting your letter in response to the above.

**M. Schneerson**

11

B”H, 19 Shevat, 5709
Paris

To the honorable Rabbi, the G-d-fearing and scholarly Chossid, etc., etc.,
Rabbi Y. [Binyaminson], may he live.

Peace and blessing!

Your letter, along with the twenty-eight, arrived in due time.

Regarding what you write concerning the subject of Tzimtzum—that one of your acquaintances claims that all the various approaches to this matter lead to the same place—I am greatly astonished at such a superficial thought, especially since you describe this person in your letter as someone who studies the works of the Mekubalim. It is obvious that this is not the case at all. Furthermore, even in the first generation after the Arizal, who revealed to us the secret of Tzimtzum, there were diametrically opposed opinions among the great scholars regarding the nature of Tzimtzum, as is evident in their works, and this disagreement continued thereafter.

This disagreement centers on two primary issues: whether Tzimtzum is literal—meaning a removal or concealment—and whether the Tzimtzum applies only to the Ohr (the light) or also to the Maor (the source of the light).

One can categorize this into four approaches:
1) Tzimtzum is literal, and it applies even to the Atzmus (the Essence). Those who hold this view argue: how can it be said that the King is present in a place of filth, Heaven forbid?
2) Tzimtzum is literal, but it applies only to the Ohr.
3) Tzimtzum is not literal, but it applies also to the Maor.
4) Tzimtzum is not literal, and it applies only to the Ohr.

The opponents of the Alter Rebbe in his time held to the first approach mentioned above, as is well known. They interpreted the statement "there is no place void of Him" as referring only to His providence. They claimed that the view that the Atzmus is present everywhere contradicts the laws regarding filthy alleyways, etc., as stated in the proclamations and notices published during the time of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe. See also Shaar HaYichud VeHaEmunah, Chapter 7, and Iggeres HaKodesh, end of Epistle 25. I believe a letter from the Alter Rebbe discussing this is also printed in *Beis Rebbe*.

The approach of the author of *Nefesh HaChaim*, which you mention in your letter, corresponds to the third approach mentioned above. He differed on this point from his teacher, the Gra. In general, it appears that Rabbi Chaim of Volozhin saw the works of Chabad, specifically the Tanya, and was influenced by them, though I do not know this for certain.

However, we hold only to the fourth approach mentioned above: that Tzimtzum is not literal, and it does not apply to the Maor but only to the Ohr, and specifically only to the lowest level within the Ohr that precedes the Tzimtzum, as is explained in the books and writings of Chabad.

In our times, having merited the light, the tractate of Tzimtzum has been explained at length, proportionately, and in many details in the published and manuscript works of Chabad Chassidus. Therefore, anyone who wishes to know the subject of Tzimtzum—at least to a certain degree, with understanding and comprehension—has no other path but to study the aforementioned sources. To be convinced of this, it suffices to compare what is said on this subject in other books—many of which, for various reasons, seemingly did not wish to elaborate on the details—with what is explained in the works of Chabad.

I do not know which books and manuscripts you have at your disposal to which I might refer you.

Regarding the subject of Tzimtzum, you should look into: *Torah Ohr*, discourse *Pasach Eliyahu* (there are also the *Hagahos* of the Rebbe Rashab on this, published in a separate booklet); *Likutei Torah*, *Hosafos* to Vayikra, discourse *Lehavin Mah Shene'emar B'Sefer Etz Chaim*; *Shaar HaYichud*; *Derech Mitzvosecha*, *Mitzvas Emunas Elokus* and *Shoresh Mitzvas HaTefillah* (beginning from Chapter 34); and the *Siman* for Parshas *Zecher Rav Tuvcha*. Among the discourses of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that have been printed: *Al Yipater Adam*, Chapter 9; *Dirshu Hashem*, p. 291; *Al Kein Yomru*, p. 291; *Shavuos*, p. 296; *Tov Li*, p. 296.

You surely also have the *Hemshech* of 5666, where this is also explained, beginning from the discourse *Vayolech Hashem Es HaYam* and the discourse *Anochi Hashem Elokecha*.

Also relevant to the subject of Tzimtzum is what is explained in Tanya, Chapters 46 and 47, which is explained in greater detail in the discourses *Yavi'u Levush Malchus* in *Torah Ohr* and *Shaarei Orah*.

I have also noted sources that are surely known to you, for the sake of completeness.

I will conclude with respect and best wishes.

**M. Schneerson**

## 14

B”H, 28 Menachem Av, 5709
Paris

To the honorable Rabbi, the Chassid, the wise and learned, etc.,
Rabbi Y. (may he live),

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter. As is evident from your phrasing, my letter regarding the concept of *Tzimtzum* arrived in due time.

Regarding what you wrote concerning the statement in *Tanya*, *Shaar HaYichud VeHaEmunah*, Chapter 1, that the souls of the nations of the world are from the *kelipos* that contain no good at all—"If so, how can it be said of them that 'everything they do, they do for their own sake,' etc.? And in general, how does the concept of the Seven Mitzvos they were commanded apply to them, and how can they be held accountable for them when they have no *bechirah* (free choice) for good?"

To be honest, these words are not sufficiently clear to me, and I do not know precisely what you are finding difficult in this.

In *Tanya*, it is written that the *nefesh habehamis* in a Jew is from the *kelipos* that contain good, and from it come the good character traits **by nature**, such as compassion, etc. This is a natural matter for a Jew, just like the nature of fire is to separate from the wick, and the like. This is not the case for the nations of the world, who are from the *kelipos* that contain no good; it is not possible for there to be an element of good within them, for it is only the nature of the Good to bestow good. It concludes that the fact we see them doing good is not because their intention is to do good, but rather for their own sake—to be glorified, etc. This is like the case of Nevuchadnetzar, who gave charity; his intention was not that one should be obligated to give charity, but rather that his kingdom should be prolonged. When he was told that the counsel for this was charity, he gave charity. (See also Rashi, *Bava Basra* 10a, s.v. *Ka’an*—"And he said, 'He is regretful'"). The matter is natural, in that the desire here is for the continuation of his kingdom—for his own sake.

Similarly, the commandment of the Seven Mitzvos, their fulfillment, and the punishment for them—seemingly, this has no connection to the good and evil in the nature of the soul. These matters relate to the descriptions of a *Chassid* and one who rules over his soul, according to the explanation of the Rambam in Chapter 6 of *Shemonah Perakim*. See also the commandment of remembering the deed of Amalek, Section 2, in the *Sefer HaMitzvos* of the Tzemach Tzedek—that even their fulfillment due to fear of punishment is sufficient. In *Yad*, *Hilchos Teshuvah* Chapter 10, he writes that even among Jews, this is the *avodah* until their *da’as* increases. And fulfillment can also come from fear of the Place (Hashem)—see *Sotah* 31a.

Regarding the *yiras Hashem* mentioned regarding Iyov, etc., and in Rashi’s commentary there: In *Bava Basra* 15b, some say Iyov was from the nations of the world, and this is also mentioned in *Sotah* 11a. This is not a *yiras Hashem* because His awe is great and imposed upon the creatures from the category of Good specifically (it is merely the opposite of the *chutzpah* of Amalek, unlike the Seven Nations). Rather, it is the nature of man that when he sees many princes bowing to one person, fear falls upon him, etc., as it is written, "Who would not fear You, O King of the nations?" It is simple that all of this is still an external fear, as explained in the note to *Tikkun Chatzos* in the *Siddur* and in *Tanya*, Chapter 42, Section 2. See also the version in Rashi in *Sotah* 31a.

In a more subtle sense, and according to the way of *Chassidus*, what one might ponder here is: regardless of the intention of the non-Jew who does good or fulfills one of the Seven Mitzvos, ultimately he is doing good and fulfilling a *mitzvah*. How is he capable of doing this, given that there is no good in him at all? This is even more difficult regarding the *Sitra Achra* and the *kelipah* itself, which are messengers of the Place and His command guards them, etc., as is discussed at length in *Tanya*, Chapter 7, and in the *Kuntres Uma’ayon* from the Rebbe Rashab. How is this possible if they are evil without good? However, this is explained in the *Maamar* "Padeh VeShalom" of 5667 (and similarly in the *Maamar* "Nachamu" of 5667), which states: "What is written in *Tanya* that in the *kelipos* there is no good at all, the intention is not that there is no spark at all—for it is impossible for anything to exist without a spark from the *Makkif*—rather, it is necessary that there be some spark within them, as mentioned above. It is just that the spark is so distanced and darkened that it is like evil, etc. This means that there is no *Elokus* feeling in it at all, etc." End quote, relevant to our discussion. Perhaps this is also the intention in *Kuntres Uma’ayon*, Section 4, which adds to the language of *Tanya*: "And even though he does not see—**meaning, without their feeling it.**" See also the *Maamar* on the *mitzvah* of the oneness of Hashem in the *Sefer HaMaamarim* of the Tzemach Tzedek, where he writes: "The *Sitra Achra* has its essence in the world of *Asiyah*, and all the souls of the nations of the world are influenced from there and rooted there, and there is nothing there except that thin illumination from the light of holiness that was given there from the days of the kings of old, **and therefore they are obligated to believe in the Creator**." End quote.

It is worth noting the matter of "willful sins becoming like merits," and the matter of trials according to what is written in the *Maamar* "Acharei" and its explanation printed at the end of *Derech Mitzvosecha*.

- It is still difficult to understand, for the Seven Mitzvos of the Bnei Noach are a matter of *birur* (refinement). If so, it must be through Yaakov specifically... and see what is printed in *Ohr HaTorah*, *Maamar* "Re’eh," *Reiach*, footnote 6. Perhaps it can be said that this is the spirituality and inwardness of the *halacha*—*Yad*, *Hilchos Melachim*, Chapter 8, Law 11 and 10: "Moshe Rabbeinu commanded from the mouth of the Almighty to compel all who come into the world to accept the *mitzvos* that the Bnei Noach were commanded, etc. And he must accept them upon himself before three of his peers, etc., and it must be that he accepts them and performs them because the Holy One commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moshe that the Bnei Noach were commanded in them beforehand, etc."

- While speaking of this *halacha*, I will mention its novelty: it implies, here and in *Hilchos Milah*, Chapter 1, Law 6, that for the mere non-acceptance of the Seven Mitzvos—even if he did not actually transgress—a *ben Noach* is liable for death. From where is this derived?

**I sign with honor and best wishes.**

15

[Pre-1940]

Regarding your question concerning the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, Hilchos Nesias Kapayim, section 28, paragraph 44, which cites the Gemara in Megillah (24b) that one who has blemishes on his face or hands should not perform Nesias Kapayim: The Alter Rebbe writes, for example, if his hands are bent or crooked to their sides. This follows Rashi in Megillah on the words "crooked" and "twisted" (*akumos, akushos*) in the Baraisa. The Ran, however, explains *akumos* as a hand bent backward, and *akushos* as an inability to separate the fingers. You suggest that this constitutes a disagreement between Rashi and the Ran regarding the law itself, and that the Rema—who cites the Shulchan Aruch of the Mechaber, section 28, paragraph 10—also disagrees, as he cites both Rashi’s explanation and the Ran’s explanation, whereas the Alter Rebbe cites only Rashi. From this, you infer that since according to Rashi—and the ruling of the Alter Rebbe—one performs Nesias Kapayim even with the *akushos* described by the Ran, it must be that the Ran does not mean he is entirely unable to separate his fingers (for that would certainly be a blemish, and there would be no reason for Rashi and the Alter Rebbe to disagree). Rather, the Ran means he cannot keep his fingers separated—in the manner of the Kohanim to create five "windows"—for the entire duration of Nesias Kapayim. Since this is not a literal blemish, Rashi and the Alter Rebbe rule that he performs Nesias Kapayim.

By way of introduction: The Rema is not the source for these terms here. The terms *bohakniyos*, *akumos*, and *akushos* appearing in the Shulchan Aruch, as well as the terms *astis* and *puah* in paragraph 31, are not from the Rema—as is evident from the fact that the Magen Avraham and the Taz explain these terms.

There are several responses to this matter:

1) It is not found anywhere that this constitutes a disagreement between Rashi and the Ran regarding the law—not even regarding the interpretation of the Sages—just as it is not found that the authors of the two interpretations of the word *bohakniyos* disagree on the law. Especially since the definition of a type of white skin lesion is found in the Gemara (Niddah 19a).

2) The fact that Kohanim separate their fingers is merely a custom, as the Alter Rebbe writes in section 28, paragraph 20, and as stated in the Responsa of the Geonim (cited in the Aruch, entry *Chalon*), that this has no basis in the Talmud. If so, not only is it not forbidden to perform Nesias Kapayim if one cannot keep his fingers spread for the entire duration, but even if one cannot separate his fingers at all, there is no reason to say he is disqualified by law. The prohibition exists because people will stare at his blemish, causing them to lose focus (*hesach hada’as*). In this case, they will not stare at him, as he is no different from the other Kohanim, who also do not separate their fingers—according to the law of the Baraisa.

3) The language of the Mechaber is: "One who has a blemish on his face or hands, such as... *akushos*..." It is simple that *akushos* refers to a condition that is a blemish. The Magen Avraham cites the Ran’s explanation there. It is extremely forced to say that an inability to keep one's fingers spread for the duration of Nesias Kapayim is considered a blemish. Where did the Ran get such a novelty, and why would he be forced to explain it that way? One cannot raise a difficulty from *bohakniyos*, as that is also not a blemish—for one recites the blessing "He who varies the creatures" (*meshaneh habriyos*) upon seeing it (Berachos 58b). One should also note the discussion in Bechoros (45b).

4) In Chullin (56a), there is a version—cited in Rashi on Devarim 32:5 and Mishlei 4:24 (see also Dikdukei Sofrim)—that reads *akumos* and *akushos* (of a weasel). According to the inquirer’s explanation of the Ran, how would one interpret the word *akushos* there?

5) And this is the main point: *Akushos* is a synonym for *akumos* (though *akum* is not a term used in Torah, whereas *akash* and its derivatives appear many times in Tanach). The word *akash* is often translated as *akum* (crooked). What does an inability to keep fingers spread have to do with crookedness?

In my humble opinion, it seems simple that Rashi in Megillah intends to explain the **distinction** between *akumos* and *akushos*, not to innovate the definitions of the words themselves. As he writes: "*Akumos*—bent; *akushos*—to the sides." It was known that both are the opposite of straight and flat. The same applies to the Ran: his intent is to explain the **distinction** between *akumos* and *akushos*, not to define the words where they are synonymous, as both mean "crooked," as is evident from the Targum on several verses. The Ran comes to say that the distinction is: *akumos* means bent backward, and *akushos* means bent in such a way that he cannot separate his fingers (perhaps specifically if they are bent to the sides in a way that leaves no room to spread the fingers). This resolves the matter simply, as stated above. It also explains why the Alter Rebbe did not bring the Ran’s explanation in his Shulchan Aruch: both of the Ran’s explanations are included in the types of crookedness described by Rashi; "a hundred is included in two hundred."

Regarding the core question of a Kohen who cannot keep his fingers separated for the entire duration of Nesias Kapayim: Seemingly, one could argue that while it is true that if the Kohanim did not have the custom of separating their fingers there would be no reason to forbid him, now that all Kohanim create five "windows"—even if only due to custom—if he does not do so, he is different from his peers. People will stare at him, causing *hesach hada’as*. Therefore, he should be forbidden. For this very reason, it would not help if he tied his fingers with the *tzitzis* of his tallis—as the inquirer asked—since he would still be different from his peers and people would stare at him.

However, it seems to me that this logic is also flawed. If any difference in a person causes people to stare to the point of *hesach hada’as*, and the examples of *bohakniyos* etc., were merely illustrative—similar to what is written in the Commentary on the Mishnah (Megillah) that *astis* and *puah* are not exhaustive (the fact that Maimonides did not write them in Mishneh Torah is known according to his general rules, as he usually only writes what is explicitly explained)—it is a great wonder that this novelty, that *any* difference disqualifies a Kohen, is not found in the Poskim. Furthermore, the language of the Gemara and Poskim does not imply this. Even in the Alter Rebbe’s Shulchan Aruch (section 46—which comes from the Beis Yosef), he explains that it does not help for a Kohen with blemishes on his face to drape his tallis where his peers do not, because since he is different, they will stare at him. He should have said: "This is forbidden even for a Kohen without blemishes, because he is changing the custom and people will stare." Furthermore, he writes later: "Even if all the Kohanim wanted to drape their tallis, it should not be permitted, because since they are not accustomed to it... the people recognize that it is because he has a blemish, and they come to stare at him." He specifically says "because he has a blemish." Seemingly, he should have just said "because of that." Also, his conclusion "and they come to stare at him"—people stare at all the Kohanim because they changed from the norm! Rather, it is certain that we require a significant difference that causes excessive staring to the point of *hesach hada’as*. Therefore, the change of draping the tallis alone, without a blemish, is not sufficient. That is why he wrote: "But if **this one, who has a blemish**, wants to drape his tallis—it is forbidden." Regarding this logic that not all differences are equal, we also find in the Lechem Mishneh (Hilchos Nesias Kapayim 15:2) that he explains Maimonides' view that in the case of a difference like *akumos*, covering it does not help, unlike blindness (though this requires study of the Yerushalmi, Megillah 4:8, cited in Tosafos, which says it does help even with *akumos*). See also the Beis Yosef, that the Gemara discusses blindness in one eye because one might have thought that only blindness in both eyes disqualifies one from Nesias Kapayim. Consequently, in the current case, since it is not found in the Shulchan Aruch that this is considered a blemish that forbids Nesias Kapayim, there is no basis to say that this alone is sufficient to forbid him. He is obligated to perform Nesias Kapayim. For this very reason, even one who cannot separate his fingers at all performs Nesias Kapayim. Especially since in our times the custom of the congregation is to cover their faces with the tallis, one can use the reasoning of the Taz (cited in paragraph 46) as a supporting argument, even though this is not the custom for one who has a blemish.

Regarding the inquirer's question about tying his fingers with his *tzitzis* to fulfill the custom of the five "windows": he is uncertain if one should do this, as it would be an *chatzitzah* (interposition) between his fingers, blocking the Divine Presence. The points to note are: 1) From what the Alter Rebbe writes at the end of section 36, that merely looking at the Kohanim is permitted but one is accustomed to be careful, it is evident that he rules like those who say that nowadays it is only a remembrance of the Temple, when there was an indwelling of the Divine Presence. Therefore, he writes the reason of *hesach hada’as* throughout the section (see Rashi, Megillah 24b; Tosafos, Chagigah 17a; Magen Avraham 28:38). 2) It must be that we are not concerned with such an interposition, like the interposition of *tzitzis*, since it is not mentioned anywhere that where one performs Nesias Kapayim on a weekday, one must remove the *tefillin* strap from the finger before Nesias Kapayim. The Mechaber specifically discusses places where Nesias Kapayim is performed on weekdays (see section 129, and in section 27 he mentions wrapping the strap on the finger). Although one could distinguish that *tefillin* straps are in their place, unlike the *tzitzis* here—similar to Negaim 13:10 and Mikvaos 1:1—and thus it is more of an interposition. Also, according to what is written in section 36 that it is a proper custom that the Kohen's tallis should not be an interposition between his hand and the people, it is obvious that a draped tallis is not comparable to the interposition of *tzitzis* threads and *tefillin* straps. See Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim 574:7, Rema. 3) The Midrash cited in section 20 states that the Divine Presence peers through the lattices of their fingers. If so, there is no interposition between the Divine Presence and the people. Also, regarding the Kohanim, there is no interposition, as the *tzitzis* and *tefillin* are nullified in relation to it, as written in section 36 regarding the tallis. (This requires further study from 574:7 mentioned above).

There is another possibility in this case, based on what is written in section 20, that between one word and the next, one is permitted to bring the fingers together. Certainly, during the time of reciting the word, he can keep his fingers separated as required.

It requires study which of the two options is preferable: whether to tie his fingers with the *tzitzis*, even though he will be different from his fellow Kohanim (and even though, as stated, this does not cause staring that leads to *hesach hada’as* even if they did not drape their tallis, as even with mere looking one is accustomed to be careful), or whether he should separate his fingers before reciting each word, even though one might fear distraction. Also, the words "and so too the other fingers" in section 20 are in parentheses. In *She'eris Yehudah* (cited in *Sha'ar HaKollel* 9:2), it is written that every doubt in the Shulchan Aruch was placed in parentheses.

The inquirer also asked: The Alter Rebbe writes in section 36 that the proper custom is for the Kohanim's hands to be outside their tallis, but in Poland they do not follow this custom. How should one act?

Similarly, the Magen Avraham (19) writes in the name of the Zohar to separate all the fingers from one another, and concludes that this is not the custom. The Alter Rebbe does not mention this at all. In the book *Minchas Yom Tov* on the *Kitzur Shulchan Aruch*, he also cites the Zohar, and in the name of the book *Heichal HaBrachah*, that one should not let one finger touch another, and if not, he is called a transgressor. How should one act?

Certainly, in both details, there is a fixed custom passed down through tradition among the Kohanim of *Anash*, and you should go and see.

Regarding the inquirer's statement that creating the five "windows" is to fulfill "peering from the windows": this is apparently a slip of the pen, as it is "peering from the lattices" (*meitzitz min hachrakim*), as the Alter Rebbe writes in section 20, and so it is in the Tur, etc. (See Tanchuma, Parshas Nasso, in the commentary *Etz Yosef*, and *Aruch HaShalem*, entry *Chalon*, for the citation; this is unlike R. S. Buber, who maintains the reversed version).

For the distinction between "windows" (*chalonos*) and "lattices" (*chrakim*), see *Likkutei Torah*, discourse on the meaning of the Second Day of Yom Tov in the Diaspora, and elsewhere. See also the additions to *Biurei HaZohar*, end of Parshas Bechukosai.

From various printings of the Shulchan Aruch, one must clarify who is the author of the explanations of the foreign words in the Shulchan Aruch.

One should check the book *Ketzos HaShulchan* (by the author from Jerusalem) to see if he commented on the above; I could not obtain it here.

One should search in the Sephardic Shulchan Aruch to see if they mention the removal of *tefillin* straps during Nesias Kapayim. Here, I only obtained the *Knesses HaGedolah* and *Shiyurei Knesses HaGedolah*.

16

[28 Tishrei 5700]

Paris, 11/X 1939

Dear Rabbi,

I thank you for your letters of the 22nd and 25th of last month. We have no news regarding our parents. From a letter from Mr. Liberman, we learned that they were unable to leave Warsaw, and we are very worried about them.

I would have very gladly provided you with all the dates and places of birth for our family, as you requested, to facilitate your efforts in obtaining American permits. Unfortunately, I do not know them all, except for the following: Mendel Horenstein, born 1905 in Januszpol; Cheyna Horenstein, born 1904 in Lubawiecy; Haya Moussia Schneerson, born August 13, 1901, in Smolensk, daughter of Joseph and Dina; Mendel Schneerson, born March 1, 1895, son of Leyvik and Chana Janowska, in Nicolaev. I have written to Mr. Liberman asking him to provide you with all the necessary information, but I believe it would be very useful for you, on your part, to address him or Mr. Dubin directly and without delay so that they may provide you with the exact dates.

I also have a personal favor to ask of you: It concerns an intimate friend of our family, Mr. Alexandre Rainin, who lives in Paris; he is requesting an affidavit. He and his wife, Sylvia, are quite wealthy and will not require any financial assistance.

My cordial thanks to Mr. Haskind for his letter.

I would be happy to hear from you soon, and I remain, dear Rabbi, yours most sincerely,

M. Schneerson

***

16

The following four letters were copied from the original manuscripts and translated into Hebrew by Rabbi Menachem Mendel Pevsner. At that time, the Rebbe Rayatz was besieged in Warsaw. His secretary was Rabbi Chaim Lieberman. The Rebbe was in Paris, and Rabbi Israel Jacobson was in the United States, from where he worked on the rescue of our headquarters, based on information he received from Riga and Paris. These four letters all deal with the information received from our headquarters in Warsaw and the efforts for rescue. See the introduction to Igros Kodesh of the Rebbe Rayatz, Vol. 5, pp. 5–9. Due to the war, it was impossible to write the letters in Hebrew, so they were written in French and in code.

Our parents: This refers to the Rebbe Rayatz and his household. Dates and places of birth: These are the dates written in their official documents, which differ slightly from the actual dates of birth.

Mr. Dubin: Rabbi Mordechai, a representative in the Latvian Saeima.

Alexandre Rainin: See the letters of the Rebbe Rayatz to him in Vol. 1, pp. 204, 241, 286. Haskind: Rabbi Dovber.

17

[6 Cheshvan 5700]

Paris, October 19, 1939

Dear Rabbi,

I acknowledge receipt of your esteemed letter of the 8th inst.

Mr. Liberman has surely informed you that yesterday he received news—I do not yet know the details—from our father and the family that they are in good health.

I am certain that it is unnecessary to ask you to expedite the efforts to obtain American visas for our father and the family, and that you are doing everything in your power.

The salary set for our father will surely be sufficient, and because of this, there should be no impediment to obtaining his permit as soon as possible.

Hoping that you have received my last letter, I would be very grateful if you would keep me informed of your progress.

Please accept, dear Rabbi, my thanks and my most devoted sentiments.

M. Schneerson

[27 MarCheshvan 5700]

Paris, November 9, 1939

Dear Rabbi,

I have just received your esteemed letter of the 30th ultimo.

I thank you warmly for your detailed letter, given that we have remained without any news from our entire family since October 19th, and we are very worried about them.

I ask you, dear Rabbi, to please inform us when you have any news regarding our father and the family.

We are well, and we hope to hear from you soon.

Please accept, dear Rabbi, my cordial regards,

M. Schneerson

[Free Translation]

Paris, 9/11/1939

Dear Rabbi!

I have just received your heartfelt regards from the 30th of last month.

I wish to thank you warmly for your detailed letter, as we have been without any news from the entire family since October 19th, and we are very worried about their well-being.

I ask you, dear Rabbi, to inform me as soon as you receive any news regarding our father and the family.

We are doing well and hope to receive news from you soon.

Please accept, honorable Rabbi, my expressions of esteem,

M. Schneerson

## 19

Paris, 17/XI/1939

My dear Rabbi!

I received a letter today from Mr. Liberman, and I am hastening to inform you of what he writes to us.

The Szmotkin building in Warsaw, where my father and the family had been living, has been completely destroyed by fire. Thank G-d, they are all safe and sound.

In the fire, they lost all their belongings, such that, it appears, they have nothing with which to cover themselves.

(They had the courage to save the manuscripts. The library remained in Otwock.)

Since then, they have been staying with Mr. Hirz Gurarie.

I am certain, my dear Rabbi, that you are doing everything in your power to come to their aid, and especially to get them out as soon as possible, given their situation.

Please accept, my dear Rabbi, my most distinguished regards.

M. Schneerson

***

#### 19

See the note to Letter 16. Regarding what follows, see the Introduction to Igros Kodesh, Vol. 5, p. 8. **The manuscripts; the library:** See ibid., pp. 15–16.

23 Iyar, 5702

To our esteemed friend, the distinguished Torah scholar and G-d-fearing man, R. Moshe Pinchas Katz, may he live, of Newark.

Peace and blessing!

We thank you for the notes you wrote to us regarding the *shmuessen*. With Hashem’s help, we will correct the points pertaining to your first note in future issues.

Regarding note 2: It is impossible to use the term *niftar* when speaking to children, as they would not understand what it means. This is how it was translated in *cheder* in the past as well. (Especially regarding the statement that Yaakov Avinu did not die; see the note below).

Regarding your third note: The primary intent was to describe the design of the *keilim* so that people would be aware of them, not to marvel at the gold plating; therefore, we did not write about the plating of the boards.

In general, we were pleased to see that you read the *shmuessen* with such care. If you have further notes in the future, please do inform us, and we will certainly look into them.

On this occasion, we would like to request that you endeavor to distribute the *shmuessen* in your area, as you surely recognize their great value and benefit for young readers. We would be happy to hear what you might be able to do in this regard.

With all respect, and with the blessing of *le'alter l'geulah*,

**Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch**

***

**Regarding the matter of Yaakov Avinu not dying:** See *Chiddushei Aggados* which explains that "he did not die" refers to his *nefesh*, whereas his body did die. Seemingly, the *Tosafos* implies otherwise. It could be said that the words of the *Tosafos* reflect the initial assumption (*kas d'a*), and not the conclusion reached after the question, "Did the eulogizers eulogize in vain?" etc.

See also the *Ramban* on the Torah, at the end of *Bereishis*, who writes: "Yaakov Avinu did not die... and Yosef’s brothers saw that their father had died, for **to them** he was dead" (though he explains it in another way there as well). In *Zohar Chadash* (248b): "Yaakov did not die... the sun did not die, but rather **it was gathered from the world** and went to the moon." See also *Biurei HaZohar*, *Parshas Vayechi*, s.v. *Tach chazei mitzrai*.

In the *Alshich* (*Bereishis* 47:29), he writes that specifically Yaakov did not die, but death did exist among the rest of Yisrael.

This would sweeten the change of names in the verse, "Fear not, worm of Yaakov, [you] men of Yisrael," according to the words of our Sages in *Bereishis Rabbah* (ch. 81), and the commentary of the *Maharzu* that *mei-tei* refers to death (*misah*). However, this requires further study in light of the words of our Sages (*Zohar Chadash* 50c, 70a; *Rashi* on *Taanis* 30b, s.v. *Af*) that "Yisrael saw Yisrael Sava." And in *Bereishis Rabbah* (ch. 96): "And you will not die, as it is said, 'And the days of Yisrael drew near,'" etc.

Seemingly, it is simple that only Yaakov did not die*, unlike Avraham, etc., regarding whom death is explicitly mentioned. This is also implied in *Torah Or*, s.v. *Lehavin inyan ha'avos*, and *Likutei Torah*, s.v. *Vesamti kadkod*, second discourse, end of section 3.

The *Ramban* (ibid.), who writes, "The matter of this Midrash, etc.," implies that he holds this concept applies to all *tzaddikim*. As he concludes there, "This matter will be understood in *Maseches Shabbos* and *Maseches Kesuvos*"—it seems he refers to *Shabbos* (152b) and *Kesuvos* (103a). See also *Bava Basra* (58a).

Perhaps the distinction is that for all other *tzaddikim*, this occurs only at specific times, as per the language of the *Ramban*, whereas with Yaakov, it hovered over him all day. The *Alshich* distinguishes that with Yaakov, even his *ruach* and *neshamah* did not depart, whereas with other *tzaddikim*, only the *nefesh* remained. See *Tosafos* s.v. *Venishmaso*, *Shabbos* (153a), which implies that it is impossible for it to be all day if his *neshamah* is hidden under the Throne of Glory. This is a higher level than if it only descends at times.

It could be said that this applies specifically to those who experienced death and whose connection between the *neshamah* and body was severed, unlike Yaakov Avinu, who did not die; on the contrary, the elevation is specifically the *neshamah* within the body. This still requires further study.

**Note added later:**
*) See *Toras Levi Yitzchak* on the *Shas*, *Maseches Taanis* (ibid., p. 6 and onwards), that this is because Yaakov’s attribute is *Tiferes*—"Give truth to Yaakov" (*titen emes l'Yaakov*). (And there, p. 7: *emes l'Yaakov* consists of the letters of *Yaakov* *lo meis*—did not die). See there at length. Also see the *sicha* of 20 Menachem Av, 5731.

B"H, 27th of Menachem Av, 5702
Brooklyn

My dear friend, the eminent Torah scholar, the renowned Chassid,
G-d-fearing and wise, etc., etc.,
Rabbi Shlomo Aharon Kazarnovsky, may he live,

Shalom u’Bracha!

In accordance with our discussion, I am sending you a copy of the memorandum that I sent to the Kreimer brothers, may they live.

Perhaps it would be worthwhile for you to meet with R' Chazak first and influence him to be in favor of the matter. Afterwards, it would be best if he were also present during the farbrengen with the aforementioned brothers to discuss the matter definitively.

I remain your friend, awaiting good news,

**M. Schneerson**

## 22

22

B"H, 13 Elul, 5702

To our esteemed friend, the outstanding avreich, G-d-fearing and G-d-serving, etc.,
Rabbi Menachem Ze’ev Gringlas, may he live,
Montreal

Shalom u’vracha,

We received your two letters with thanks, and you have surely already received the maamarim and kuntresim that we sent you.

Enclosed with this letter, we are sending you a clipping from the "Morgen Journal" containing an article regarding the work of "Machne Israel."

Since the days of Elul and the upcoming Yomim Noraim are the most appropriate time to conduct outreach regarding the spiritual matters with which "Machne Israel" is occupied, please endeavor to have this enclosed article, or a similar one, printed in the "Canadian Eagle" newspaper during these days.

It is our strong hope that you, together with your colleagues, may they live, will immediately begin vigorous work in all areas of "Machne Israel," for it is a time to act, and Hashem will be their help. I will note here the main points of this important work:

a) To affix the *Rosh Dvarim* of Acharon Shel Pesach to the walls of the shuls. It would be best if you could print the entire *Rosh Dvarim* or a section of it in the newspaper before Rosh Hashanah.

b) To send the letter regarding the Tehillim societies to every shul in your entire region. In shuls where you have acquaintances, it is worthwhile to also include a personal letter from you.

c) To disseminate the idea of purifying the atmosphere by reciting letters of the Torah by heart in every place, and especially Mishnayos by heart.

d) To utilize the days of Selichos and the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah for outreach in the shuls and the like, by inspiring teshuvah and announcing that this is the time of the birth pangs of Moshiach, and that immediate teshuvah leads to immediate Geulah, and similar themes.

Naturally, all of this must be adapted to the conditions of your country and location, and we rely on your discretion.

If it will be of benefit to your work, you may announce that a branch of "Machne Israel" in your country is located at the following address... (If it is possible for it not to be at the Yeshiva’s address, that would be better, in order to emphasize that this is not a matter of a political party **at all**). You have been appointed as the address and the executive committee for "Machne Israel" in your country. Although, in general, it is good for everyone to be connected directly to here, so that any difficult matter can be clarified immediately with the Rebbe, shlita.

We are preparing a collection of letters from the Rebbe, shlita, regarding the above, and as soon as it is printed, we will send it to you; please let us know how many copies to send.

With the blessing of *Kesivah v’Chasimah Tovah*, for immediate teshuvah and immediate Geulah, and regards and best wishes to all his colleagues,

**M. Schneerson**
Chairman of the Executive Committee

***

22

From a photocopy of the letter. From this point forward, many letters were written on the stationery of "Machne Israel" or "Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch," which is why the style is sometimes in the plural and the signature is simply "Chairman of the Executive Committee." For the sake of completeness, the institution from which the letter was sent will be noted in each instance.

From the year 5703 and onwards (see letter 89 below), also of "Kehot." The letters were generally written on a typewriter. Before the aforementioned title, the Rebbe’s holy name was typed with the addition of "Rabbi," and above this was his holy handwritten signature. In places where the copy was taken from the letter that was sent, the signature was copied as it appeared, and where it was copied from the secretariat's copy, it was copied as it appeared on the typewriter.
