## 231

14 Shevat, 5704

To our esteemed friend, the outstanding avreich, G-d-fearing and learned, Rabbi Menachem Zev Gringlas, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your express letter of 3 Shevat and those preceding it (your express letter reached us only on 8 Shevat, surely because it was held up by the censor):

1) One hundred English *luchos* have already been sent to you. You should certainly inform us as soon as possible how many more to send, as there are many who are requesting them, and I do not know if we will print them again.

2) The *sicha* of 18 Elul and another collection of *maamarim* and *sichos* (Chicago, 5702) are currently at the printer, and we are busy proofreading them, as well as our second and fourth volumes.

3) We will ask our friend R. Y.D. to write to you regarding what you asked about his trip.

4) We received a letter from R. Yosef Radal and R. M.A. Gerlitzky regarding "Beis Rivkah" in Montreal; we have already responded to them.

5) We are interested in the activities during the month of Teves regarding education, as mentioned in your letter, and their results, as well as information regarding the state of education that the traveling students acquired.

6) Thank you for your efforts with Mr. Dalfen. It does not matter to us if he pays in installments, as long as the matter is secure. We have not yet decided what to print, and we will inform you in due time.

7) The *shmuessen* from the month of Teves were already sent to you before your letter arrived. Regarding the *Kupah HaGedolah*, we have notified the office.

8) We were happy to hear your news regarding the expansion of the "Shabbos Parties," and we are interested in hearing about this in greater detail.

9) Mr. [...] has not spoken with the Rebbe shlita regarding the printing of the *kuntres*. Therefore, please clarify the matter on the spot, bring it to a good conclusion, and explain to him the concept that "whoever increases [his efforts] is praiseworthy," and that from an increase in quantity, it is possible to achieve an increase and elevation in quality.

10) Regarding the list of memoirs: it has been over a year that we have been speaking about this, and after all the effort—they sent one list, and that is all. You explain in your letter that "he (R. Y. Weinberg) did not have the focus for this until now!" Imagine for yourselves: if someone else were the writer and you were the readers, what kind of impression would such an excuse make on you?

11) Please inform us in a separate note which *sichos* and *kuntresim*—from those published overseas—you require and how many of each, and I will pass it on to those in charge of shipping. If they are found over time, they will be sent to you.

12) Please write a letter of encouragement to Rabbi Kelman in Toronto, as per your instructions.

13) Regarding what you wrote—that it is good to say now that you want to come here several times during the year—it is known what our Sages say on this (Yalkut Shimoni, Tehillim 19), "David is like a merchant..." and this is ruled as *halacha* in the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch (Admur)*, *Orach Chaim* 582:6. What they said regarding four weeks is based on their own advice.

14) It is very surprising that for a long time nothing has been received toward the settlement of the debt, and it continues to grow.

15) As you requested, enclosed is a letter regarding Mishnayos by heart, concerning the *nigleh* alone. If there are any comments from the listeners and readers, you will surely inform us.

16) Regarding what you wrote about the bracketed sections in the *Shulchan Aruch* of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe—see *Shaar HaKollel*, Chapter 1.

With the blessing of "immediately to teshuvah, immediately to Geulah,"

M. Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 132

[14 Shevat 5704]

Regarding the request to explain, also according to the *nigleh* (the revealed dimension of Torah), the concept of reciting Mishnayos by heart.

Rabbi Menachem Zev HaLevi Gringlas
Montreal, Canada

***

**Response:** In response to this question, and in order to once again awaken [the reader] to the great value of reciting Mishnayos by heart and the method for doing so, I am hereby transcribing—with some additions—what I have written previously to several members of the "Mishnayos Be'al Peh" society, which operates under the auspices of "Machne Israel."

In accordance with our Sages' teaching (*Sifri, Parshas Naso*; *Bamidbar Rabbah*, ch. 7) that "one only encourages those who are already diligent," I come here to remind you once again regarding your participation in the oral recitation of the Six Orders of Mishnah, in such a way that the entire group together will complete the study of all six orders within a year, and will recite them during all spare moments: while walking on the road or in the street, while sitting in the shop or in the office—provided it is a place where it is permitted to speak words of Torah. As the Rebbe, my father-in-law, *shlita*, said when he founded this society: "The times demand that one must purify the air... the purification of the air is through the letters of the Torah... It is very necessary that Jews should learn Mishnayos by heart... and recite the Mishnayos at all times and in every place... This is not a matter of a specific faction or a particular ideology... It is a general matter that concerns the entire Jewish people and serves as a protection."

He also said at the time of the first lottery (the week of Shavuos 5702): "The Mishnayos that one will recite, wherever one may be and in whatever place one may be, will illuminate the connection between Israel and *Kudsha Brich Hu*."

And at the time of the second lottery (18 Sivan 5703), he said: "Rabbi Bena'ah said (*Vayikra Rabbah*, ch. 21), 'A person should always immerse himself in Mishnayos, for if he is diligent, they will be opened for him; if [he is diligent] in Talmud, [the gates of] Talmud will be opened for him; if in Aggadah, [the gates of] Aggadah will be opened for him...' Through this, he will become proficient in both Halacha and Aggadah, because *Mishnah* is the letters of *neshamah* (soul)."

And at the time of the third lottery (27 Sivan 5704): "Every member should take upon himself to learn Mishnayos by heart... Do not consider this a trivial matter: every Mishnah [recited] by heart sustains a world and all that is in it."

***

To explain briefly the concept of reciting Mishnayos by heart, also according to *nigleh*, based on the teachings of our Sages, I will proceed in order through the parts of this matter:

**A. The value of the air and its purification.**

The existence of the body and its connection with the soul—whereby a person is considered "alive"—depends, according to the will of the One who performs wonders, who is the One who links soul and body, upon two things: the blood, which is formed from the food and drink that one eats and drinks, and the air that a person breathes. This is known to be the function of the signs—the esophagus and the trachea.

Now, although both—air and food/drink—are necessary for human life, they differ in that food and drink are only for specific times:

—And our Sages said (*Yoma* 75b), "At first, the Israelites were like chickens pecking in the dust, until Moshe came and established for them a time for eating." And in the Rambam (*Hilchos Deos*, beginning of ch. 4), "A person should not eat unless he is hungry, and should not drink unless he is thirsty."

This is unlike breathing, which is constant (in the normal course of events), to the extent that our Sages said that a person can possibly live for up to seven days without food and drink (*Rambam, Hilchos Shevuos*, ch. 5, law 6), but without breathing, one dies within a short time (see the commentaries on *Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer*, ch. 17, sec. 32; *Responsa Tzemach Tzedek, Even HaEzer*, ch. 68-70; and the places cited in *Sdei Chemed, Klalim*, letter *Kaf*, sub-section 108—regarding the measure of this "short time").

It is therefore understood that since the existence of the body and its connection with the soul are maintained through food and drink, the food and drink must be suitable for this function. Furthermore, their quality influences the quality of the body as a whole and its connection with the soul in particular, as our Sages said on this matter (*Berachos* 44b): "Every green [vegetable] makes one green, every small [thing] makes one small, every soul restores the soul, and every [thing] close to the soul restores the soul." And our Sages stated the reason for the prohibition against eating animals that prey and hunt is that it engenders cruelty in the heart of the one who eats it (*Ramban* on the Torah, *Vayikra* 11:11, and elsewhere).

Moreover: even food that would not be forbidden were it not for the person's thought or speech—such as one who slaughters for the sake of idolatry (*Chullin* 39b) and the like—acts to create a bad temperament in the person who eats it. As we find regarding Elisha Acher (*Rus Rabbah* 3:13), whose mother ate from an idolatrous offering while she was pregnant, and this caused Acher to turn to evil ways.

And one cannot say that in the case of Acher this was by way of punishment and not due to the nature [of the food], for if Acher's mother had not committed a prohibition in her eating—and on the contrary, a pregnant woman who smells [food] is fed [from it] (*Yoma* 82a), and she is performing a mitzvah.

—For it is a great strain to say that it was an accessory to idolatry in such a way that it was a "habitual practice." And also, the language "they gave her from that same kind and she ate" implies that they were required to give it to her—

Therefore, one must say that even if you argue that there is an element of punishment here, there is also a natural element, in that the forbidden food engenders specific traits in the person who eats it. However, in a case where there is a danger to life (*pikuach nefesh*), one is commanded to eat the food, even if it will cause him to have a bad temperament as a result,

—For the food has not changed; it remains in its nature and quality, only that this person who is in danger is permitted to eat it. And especially since even regarding a sick person, *pikuach nefesh* overrides the prohibition but does not permit it (*Rambam, Hilchos Shabbos*, beginning of ch. 2, and the commentaries there. And what is written in *Tzafnas Paneach*—by the Gaon of Rogatchov—on the Rambam there requires study. And this is not the place to elaborate)—

And it is analogous to something like cutting off a limb for the sake of saving the entire body.

And in general—if the food is a forbidden item, the soul of the person recoils from it, and the eating of the body is not [considered] eating, even if he ate it unknowingly, according to the ruling of the Rambam (*Terumos*, ch. 10, law 10, based on the *Yerushalmi, Terumos*, ch. 6, law 2).

If this is the case for food and drink, then *a fortiori* the air must be suitable, clean, and pure, both in its physical and spiritual aspects, so that a person can be healthy and whole in body and spirit. As our Sages have taught us general examples of this: "The air of Eretz Yisrael makes one wise" (*Bava Basra* 158b); "The air of Migdal makes one forget" (*Sanhedrin* 109a).

Regarding the effects of the air on the body and its health, in *Bereishis Rabbah* (ch. 34): "The air of that place... when a child is born... it strengthens his brain so that mosquitoes cannot harm him." And in the *Zohar* (Vol. 3, 10a): "Creatures change in their appearance due to the change of the air in every place and place." —Although in these two places, "air" can be interpreted as "climate."

And as we find "air" in this sense in the statement (*Bereishis Rabbah*, ibid.), "A covenant is made for the climates," which is interpreted as climate. —And in the *Shas*, I have not found, as of now, "air" in the sense of climate.

**B. The purification of the air through the letters of the Torah.**

Now, the effects of a person upon the world around him are through action, speech, or thought—and we find in Halacha several matters in which thought produces an effect even on something outside the person who is thinking—

Prominent among them is speech, for that which carries it and conveys it from the speaker to the listener is the air.

It is understood that good speech leaves a good impression in the air, and bad speech leaves a bad impression. The Rambam has already elaborated on the types of speech in his commentary on the Mishnah (end of ch. 1 of *Avos*), "And whoever increases words increases sin." And from his words it is clear—and it is visible to the senses—that the type of loathsome or forbidden speech is found less in the synagogue and the *beis medrash*, and mostly found in the marketplaces, streets, and the like.

The conclusion from this is that the rectification of the purification of the air is most necessary outside the *beis medrash*, even though sometimes the air of the synagogue and *beis medrash* also requires purification and refinement.

It is understood that the purification of the air must also be done through something that produces an effect in the air, namely, through good speech, which is inscribed in the air, and "the breath [of the mouth] expels the breath." And in particular—this is through the speech of the letters of the Torah, regarding which our Sages said (*Rambam, end of Hilchos Krias Shema*, based on *Berachos* 22a), "Words of Torah do not receive impurity, but stand in their purity forever, as it is said, 'Are not My words like fire, says Hashem?' Just as fire does not receive impurity, so too words of Torah do not receive impurity."

—And to note from our Sages (*Sanhedrin* 39a), "The essence of immersion—which is to purify the impure—is in fire."

***

**C. Reciting the letters of the Torah by heart specifically.**

Now, the recitation of the letters of the Torah in the synagogue and *beis medrash* can be either by heart or from a written text,

Although in any case it must be through speech specifically, as our Sages said (*Eruvin* 54a), "Open your mouth and read, open your mouth and study... [you must] articulate them with the mouth."

This is not the case outside and in the streets, and in most cases also in a place of business and the like, where the purification of the air is most necessary, as mentioned above, and it is not a place of rest or a place for books to learn from a written text; therefore, the letters of the Torah must be ready for everyone by heart, to recite them at any time and in any place where it is permitted to speak words of Torah.

Now, our Sages said (*Gittin* 60b), "Words that are written, you are not permitted to recite by heart." And although the Rambam did not bring this, there are also those who say that the prohibition applies only if he is fulfilling the obligation of others through his recitation,

—See *Rambam, Hilchos Klei HaMikdash*, ch. 6, law 7; and see the *Bavli* (and *Yerushalmi*) *Yoma* 70a, and *Tosafos Yeshanim* there; *Taanis* 28a, *Tosafos* s.v. *Devarim*; *Temurah* 14b. And they have already elaborated on this in the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim*, ch. 49. And in *Sdei Chemed*, part of the *Klalim*, system *Dalet*, letter 44, and in *Pe'as HaSadeh*, system *Dalet*, *Klal* 4, he cites the later authorities who discuss this—

But in *Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim*, ch. 49, he rules that it is forbidden to recite by heart unless it is a matter that is common in everyone's mouth.

Therefore, when one learns by heart matters that are in the Oral Torah, there is no doubt about this according to all opinions.

**D. Reciting Mishnayos—specifically—by heart.**

Now, although in general, regarding the entire Oral Torah, the main thing is the attainment and understanding of the law and the Halacha (although it must also be articulated with the mouth specifically, as mentioned above), and if one recites it without understanding, it is not considered study for him (*Magen Avraham, Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim*, ch. 50, sub-section 2), unlike the Written Torah, where even an unlearned person recites the blessing over the Torah even if he does not understand the meaning of the words,

Nevertheless, there is a distinction in the Talmud itself between the Mishnah and what comes after the Mishnah, as the Rambam wrote in his introduction to the *Peirush HaMishnayos*: "And his words in the Mishnah were brief and encompassed many matters... However, these *braisos* were not all as elegant as the words of the Mishnah, nor as precise in their structure and brevity... and everything that came after... did not set its heart and soul except to contemplate the words of the Mishnah..."—see there where he elaborates—from which it is understood that in the Mishnah, the main thing is the precision of its language (although it must be with understanding specifically, as mentioned above in the *Magen Avraham*) and the preservation of its letters, for it includes everything stated in the *braisa*, etc., and as the statement of *Alfa* (*Taanis* 21a), "Every *mishnah* of Rabbi Chiya and Rabbi Oshaya, he resolves it from our *mishnah*." And furthermore, in the *Yerushalmi* (*Pe'ah*, ch. 2, law 4): "Many laws were stated to Moshe at Sinai, and all of them are embedded in the Mishnah." And the second part of the Talmud is what is called—in general—the explanation of the Mishnah (*Mevo HaTalmud* by Rabbeinu Shmuel HaNagid), meaning that its essence is understanding and explaining the reasons, etc.

Therefore, it is understood that even recitation by heart, whose purpose is, as mentioned above, the purification of the air through the articulation of the letters of the Torah, is more applicable to the Mishnah than to the Gemara, for the difference between one and the other is: the articulation of the letters—or the understanding of the matter (although in both, there must be articulation and understanding).

**E. The special value that all of the above has in our time.**

Now, in the generations of the footsteps of Mashiach, when the end of our exile is near, there is a special value and an additional aspect to the Mishnah, according to our Sages (*Vayikra Rabbah*, ch. 7, 3): "All these exiles will only be gathered in the merit of Mishnayos."

—Our Sages also said (the *AriZal*, cited in *Midrash Talpiyos*, branch *Efod*): "When they judge him (the person) for Gehinnom, he calls to every tribe to save him from there, but there is no voice and no answer. And when he calls to Asher, he replies to him, 'Asher, did you study Mishnah?' If he says to him 'Yes,' he immediately takes him out of Gehinnom in the merit of the Mishnah," end of quote. —The connection between these two matters, Gehinnom and exile, is understood, for they replace one another, as our Sages said (*Bereishis Rabbah*, ch. 44, 21), "He (Hashem) said to him (Avraham), 'In what do you want your children to descend: in Gehinnom or in kingdoms?'" And so it is in the *Zohar* (Vol. 2, 83b). Consequently, that which saves from one of them also saves from the other, as it is there in the *Zohar*—

And to explain slightly, according to *nigleh*, the reason that the ingathering of exiles is specifically through Mishnayos, one can say: It is understood that in order for there to be an ingathering of exiles, there must be the removal and rectification of the causes that brought about the exile. And our Sages informed us (*Yoma* 9b) that the destruction of the Second Temple was due to the sin of baseless hatred (measure for measure: scattered and separated among the nations—due to the separation that was among them). Furthermore: since the redemption from the Babylonian exile was not a complete redemption (*Sotah* 36a, and see there in Rashi s.v. *Li'asos*), it must be that the cause that brought about the first destruction was also not entirely removed and atoned for. And this cause is—that they did not recite the blessing over the Torah first. Meaning—as the *Ran* explained (*Nedarim* 81a)—that even though they were constantly engaged in Torah, Hashem, who knows the depths of the heart, explained that it was not important in their eyes, for they were not engaged in it for its own sake, meaning they did not "walk in it" (as the prophet said), that is, with its intention and for its own sake, and because of this the land was lost, the Temple was destroyed, etc.

Therefore, in order for Israel to merit the ingathering of exiles, there must be: 1) That they recite the blessing over the Torah, so that it will be important in their eyes. 2) The unity of the separated, the opposite of baseless hatred—the separation of the united. And these two conditions are fulfilled through engagement in the Mishnah.

For in the three parts of the Torah: *Mikra* (Scripture), *Mishnah*, *Gemara*—in the part of *Mikra*, there is no innovation in unity, for there is no room there so much for separation, since it does not concern legal rulings and practical Halacha.

And also in understanding—well, as mentioned above, this is not the essence in the Written Torah, and even if one does not understand, he recites the blessing over the Torah.

Likewise, there is no innovation when they recite the blessing and are reminded of Hashem while studying this part of the Torah, since it is repeated many times: "And Hashem said, and Hashem spoke, etc."

—One can also say in the negation of *Mikra* in this matter, since this part is not what distinguishes Israel from the nations of the world, but rather the Oral Torah, and as our Sages said in the *Midrash* (*Shemos Rabbah*, beginning of ch. 47), "And the Sadducees also admit to it," unlike the Oral Torah—

Likewise, the Gemara, which is the dialectical analysis of the Mishnah, understanding its reasons, etc., where each one delves into it and understands according to his intellect and attainment and expresses his opinion, at first glance, the unity between those who disagree is not apparent; on the contrary, at first they become like enemies to one another, and "the love is only at the end" (*Kiddushin* 30b)—(and the legal ruling for practical application, when it is in agreement by all together, is included in the Mishnah, as written in the *Shulchan Aruch* of our Rebbe the *Zaken*, *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, beginning of ch. 2)—

And also the study is specifically to understand the matters with human intellect and not only because it is a tradition.

Different from these two parts is the Mishnah, which deals with practical legal rulings, and one must understand it (even according to the opinion of those who hold—*Rambam, Ri"v*, as explained in the *Kuntres Acharon* of our Rebbe the *Zaken* there—that knowledge of its reasons is not necessary). And in all this, everyone agrees to everything stated in it, and there are no differences of opinion—which is why anyone who errs in a matter of Mishnah must retract (*Sanhedrin* 33a). And through this very fact that everyone agrees to one law, even though their opinions are not equal, they show that Hashem is the One who gave us His Torah, and "one Torah for Israel, one nation." And especially since the Sages of the Mishnah, "the essence of their legal rulings was not according to the determination of their intellect, but only from what they received, man from man, until Moshe" (*Rabbeinu HaZaken, Torah Or*, s.v. *Ve'Eileh Shemos*, end of ch. 66).

***

May the Almighty merit us to see the ingathering of our exiles by our righteous Mashiach, about whom it is said, "And the spirit of Hashem shall rest upon him... and he shall be animated by the fear of Hashem... he shall judge by the scent" (*Sanhedrin* 93b).

## 133

B”H, Rosh Hashanah La’ilanos 5704

To the honorable, distinguished Rabbi Kelman, may he live,
Toronto

Shalom u’Bracha!

You have surely heard of the activities of "Machne Israel" and the "Merkaz L’Inyonei Chinuch," which were established by my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita. The main points regarding their objectives are understood from the enclosed leaflets, and we are sending you, in a separate package, samples of the monthly booklets for youth published by the Merkaz.

Based on what we have heard about you, you will surely be able, with the help of Hashem, to accomplish much in the work of "Machne Israel" and the "Merkaz L’Inyonei Chinuch" in your city in general, and among those closest to your influence in particular, and thereby be among those who are meritorious and who bring merit to the many.

Particularly worthy of attention is the distribution of educational books that are on the purity of holiness among the widest circles of youth, even those who are, for the time being, far from a kosher education.

We would be pleased if you would maintain correspondence with us, and it goes without saying that we are prepared to assist you, with the help of Hashem, in your aforementioned work as much as possible.

Scripture states that "man is the tree of the field," and the "vineyard of Hashem Tzevaos" is the "House of Israel." The fruits are the children, both sons and daughters. How great is the responsibility of the leader and the gardener, into whose hand the Master of the vineyard has entrusted His vineyard! This is why our Sages, of blessed memory, said that the teaching of children is called "the work of Hashem." How great is the merit of one whom Hashem has appointed to guard His vineyard! We see that every improvement, even a small one, in the seed, causes great beauty and praise in the tree that grows from it.

With blessings for immediate teshuvah and immediate geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

134

B"H, 19 Shevat, 5704

To the honored and distinguished, G-d-fearing and wise, etc.,
R' Y.Y. Sheinfeld, Shochet u'Bodek,
Roxbury, Mass.

Peace and blessing!

Your donation in the sum of eighteen dollars, which you entrusted to me, reached me through our honored and distinguished friend, who engages in communal needs with faithfulness and possesses noble character traits, the Temim R' Yehuda Leib Horowitz. I have delivered the funds to Machne Israel, to the special fund for the purchase of tefillin for military personnel, and a receipt is enclosed.

An important place in the work of Machne Israel is occupied by the campaign regarding the laying of tefillin among the outcasts of our people in general, and military personnel in particular. With the help of Hashem, the work has been successful, and many of those who for many years had not laid tefillin—and perhaps had never laid them in their lives—have now begun to fulfill this great mitzvah.

In order to ease the challenge for them in obtaining their consent to lay tefillin, tefillin are provided to them by Machne Israel for free or at a nominal price, according to the desire of each individual; and specifically for military personnel, the tefillin are sent as a gift.

Incidentally, it will surely interest you to know that regarding military personnel, we strive to strengthen their spirit and faith through various books and booklets that we send from time to time, free of charge, to all military personnel whose addresses are known to us.

As an example, we enclose herewith the message from my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe shlita, to the soldiers, which has already been distributed in the thousands, as well as a letter regarding tefillin. If you are interested that we also add any of your acquaintances in the military to our list, we will send the various booklets to them as well.

And while I am speaking about tefillin, I would suggest to you that since you have merited to be among those who support with their money the expenses of purchasing tefillin, you should go from strength to strength. Even though you are busy, you should take an interest in speaking with your acquaintances whose hearts have been touched by the word of Hashem, and begin an organized campaign in your city regarding the laying of tefillin. Surely, words that come from the heart will enter the hearts of the listeners, and with the help of Hashem, they will succeed in being among those who merit the many in spirituality—that is, the laying of tefillin—and in physicality—the protection that comes in the wake of fulfilling the mitzvah. It is known what the Rambam writes regarding this (Hilchos Teshuvah, ch. 10, halacha 5), that the unlearned, women, and children are trained to serve out of fear until their knowledge increases and they serve out of love. He explains there that one who serves out of fear intends by this to serve in order to receive all the blessings that come as a reward for the mitzvos.

With the blessing of "Immediately to Teshuvah, Immediately to Geulah,"

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

135

B"H, Tuesday, 21 Shevat, 5704

My esteemed friend, Mr. Bezborodko, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

Enclosed you will find a receipt for your contribution toward the support of the schools under the administration of the Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch. On behalf of the Merkos, I hereby express our heartfelt thanks for your participation in the endeavor of educating the sons and daughters of Yisrael in the purity of holiness.

It is our firm hope that you will continue to take part in this work in the future, and that you will endeavor to influence your acquaintances and those you know to take an interest in this and to assist the Merkos, each according to his ability. For whoever brings his fellow to perform a good deed, the merit of his fellow is dependent upon him.

Furthermore, regarding the auspicious day of this month, which is Rosh Hashanah La'Ilanos (the New Year for Trees), there is much to be learned from it. For a person who sets his heart to everything happening around him will, from every single thing, add wisdom to his wisdom regarding conduct between man and Hashem and between man and his fellow. This is not necessarily from an extraordinary sight, but even from the most common matters—such as a growing tree—one can understand many lessons for the daily life of a person.

I will point out a few of these:

Most vegetation, and trees in particular, are composed of many parts that are generally categorized into three: the root, the body of the tree (trunk, branches, leaves), and the fruits (peel, the fruit itself, and seeds).

Regarding the distinction between them: The root is hidden from the eye of the beholder, yet the essential life of the tree is through it (even though the trees also draw materials necessary for their life from the air through the leaves, and likewise absorb heat from the sun's rays, and the like). Furthermore, it is specifically through the root that the tree stands firm; if its roots are strong, it need not fear any winds that blow, for they will not uproot it.

The body of the tree is the overwhelming majority of the tree's structure. From time to time, it adds thickness in its branches and leaves, until it is through it—and especially through the trunk of the tree—that the years of the tree are known.

However, the ultimate perfection of the tree is in its producing fruit, for through the seed, it sows its seed to sprout new trees, generation after generation.

Now, "man is the tree of the field." This means that in many details, man is similar to what is seen in a tree and the tree of the field, even in his spiritual life. They, too, are generally divided into three categories:

The root is emunah, through which he is connected to his place and the source of his life, the Creator, blessed be He. Even when he grows in wisdom, Torah, and mitzvos, his life is drawn to him through his emunah in Hashem, His religion, and His Torah.

The trunk and body of the tree are the study of Torah and the performance of mitzvos and good deeds. These must be the majority of the structure and the majority of the count of a person's deeds and activities. It is usually by the mitzvos and his growth in Torah that the days of the years of a person's life are recognized—that is, a life filled with the content of wisdom and action.

The fruits: The ultimate perfection of man is when, in addition to fulfilling everything incumbent upon him between himself and himself, he influences his fellows and his environment so that they, too, will be on the level of perfection. This means that his action is a "seed" that sprouts a tree (people) possessing roots (the foundations of emunah), a trunk and branches (Torah and good deeds), and bearing fruit (meriting the many).

The conclusion from all the above: A person's root and essence is simple emunah. Weakness in emunah places the existence of the spiritual life of even a great person in danger.

The majority of the count and the majority of the structure of a person are his good deeds, which go on increasing from day to day.

But the perfection of man is as a bearer of fruit—that is, when he influences the many and merits them to also fulfill their role and the purposes of their creation. Then his action produces fruit, and the fruit of his fruit, generation after generation, and this merit is dependent upon him.

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 136

[Additional version of the above]

For man is the tree of the field; this means that there is a significant part of human life in which he is similar, in various respects, to aspects of the life of the plant kingdom.

The root is emunah, even though it has no place according to intellect and logical reasoning. Yet, through it, he is connected to the place and source of his vitality, which is Hashem, and through this, his vitality is drawn down even when the person has already grown in wisdom and in the performance of mitzvos.

The trunk is the performance of mitzvos. This constitutes the bulk of the tree’s structure. It increases from year to year, for emunah must be in full force even if one’s intellect is not yet developed and one is still "small" in wisdom. This is unlike mitzvos, which must be "coming in days," meaning that every single day one adds more mitzvos, for "days were fashioned for them." And one adorns himself before Him with mitzvos, which is visible to all, and even the intellect admits to their beauty. There are mitzvos that branch out into several mitzvos (tzitzis—four mitzvos; the Beis Hamikdash includes several mitzvos: vessels, offerings, etc.), just as branches also spread out into many smaller branches.

But the merit lies in the fruits; that is, there is a type of mitzvah that influences others as well through their performance. As stated in the commentary on the Mishnah regarding the reason why one who is good to his fellow creatures eats their fruits in this world, this is one of the purposes of man’s creation: that he should also influence others, meaning that his good deed should sow and sprout fruits similar to themselves.

Now, if one desires that the seed be a lasting seed, this depends specifically on the kernel—that is, on that which has neither taste nor scent. This refers to what, and in what manner, it enters the heart of the listener: specifically if he is a G-d-fearing person and speaks with mesiras nefesh and from the point of emunah within him (even though this is covered, and outwardly he speaks logical reasons. Just as the kernel is covered by the body of the fruit and its peel, which have taste, appearance, and scent, yet the cause of his speech and the provider of vitality and enthusiasm in his speech is his mesiras nefesh). Then it sprouts a great tree, bearing fruits and branches.

The conclusion from all the above: The root of man and his connection to the source of his vitality is solely through emunah, without any embellishment, pretense, or adornment. A weakness in emunah, even a slight one, weakens the entire existence of the tree, even if it is large and strong in its trunk, branches, etc., for none of this is effective if the emunah is not in its full strength.

It is man’s task to add mitzvos day by day, like a tree that is constantly growing and expanding. This, and only this, is in its species, quality, and essence. But within the trunk, the branches, and even the leaves, there flows a vitality that the roots draw from the earth—Knesses Yisrael, the source of the neshamah—and send into the trunk, the branches, etc., to animate them. For in the performance of mitzvos, one must always remember the power of mesiras nefesh (as in Tanya, Chapter 26), and "the righteous shall live by his faith"—all his mitzvos [must be lived through his faith].

But ultimately, the purpose of man is to influence others as well.

—Before the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, all trees bore fruit. And in the time to come, all barren trees are destined to bear fruit.—

And as our Sages have said, the first mitzvah is to be fruitful and multiply—one must see to it that there is another Yid.

This is the ultimate perfection of the tree: when one sees that it has already sprouted fruits, sowing seed to sprout yet another tree.

This is the lesson of Tu B’Shvat, the New Year for trees: that first and foremost is the root, emunah; afterwards comes the growth, thickness, and stature of the trunk—mitzvos in abundance and in plenty. And the perfection is seen in the quality of the fruit and the kernel—meaning that the one who brings merit to his fellow, or to the many, depends on this, and he has reached his desired perfection.

From the holy handwriting.

## 137

B"H, Monday, 27 Shevat, 5704

Highly esteemed Mr. Chaim Lieberman,

Shalom and blessings!

I would like to express my thanks for your visit to the Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch.

It gave me great pleasure to make your acquaintance and to discuss the current challenges facing Jewish education in America.

I am certain that the conversation you had during your visit with my father-in-law, the Rebbe shlita, will have a continuation in your practical work for the sake of Jewish education.

In the Midrash of this week’s parsha, it is stated: "The world was not worthy of using gold; why then was it created? For the Mishkan and for the Beis Hamikdash."

The lesson for every person is that the talent with which Hashem has gifted him was created "for the Mishkan and for the Beis Hamikdash" that exists in the heart of every Jew, and only by virtue of this is it worthy of being used in the world at large as well.

Hoping to remain in contact with you regarding matters of practical avodah.

Yours, with the blessing of *l'alter l'teshuvah, l'alter l'geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 138

138

By the Grace of Hashem, Rosh Chodesh Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the esteemed and distinguished, a man of action, R' Yitzchak Neta, may he live, Wellington, N.Z.

Greetings and Blessings,

Your letter from the 1st of Parshas Vayechi reached us, and we were happy to learn that the printing materials we sent you arrived in their entirety and are, with Hashem’s help, bringing great benefit to the chinuch of Yiddishkeit in your region.

We have delivered your letter to your brother, our friend R' Nachum, may he live.

Per your request, we are now sending you additional seforim and booklets according to the list attached to this letter. Please be kind enough to inform us how many copies of each type of sefer and booklet published by us we should send you in the future, and also to confirm their receipt.

We were pleased to hear the news that a committee has already been organized for the construction of a mikveh in your city. Surely you recognize and know that this is a matter of pikuach nefesh for both body and soul, and that one must hasten the construction of the mikveh with all possible effort, without delaying it at all. There is no doubt, nor even a shadow of a doubt, that if you only approach the matter with a tremendous desire to do everything in your power, Hashem will be your help in bringing your intention from potential into action.

We received a letter from a certain man from... in New Zealand, whom we have not known until now. Since it is possible that he lives in your neighborhood and perhaps it is worthwhile for you to speak with him, we are enclosing a copy of his letter to us and our response to him, and we ask that you inform us of anything you know about him.

Surely, as far as possible, you are also exerting effort in establishing a Tehillim society—as explained at length in the second collection of letters from my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, may he live, which we sent you some time ago.

I will conclude with words of inspiration regarding the day, the first of Adar:

Our Sages of blessed memory state (Shekalim 1:1), "On the first of Adar, announcements are made regarding the shekalim and the kilayim." One must explain the connection between these two matters in a person’s avodah to his Creator during these days of the ikvesa d'meshicha:

For our Sages of blessed memory said (Rosh Hashanah 11a), "In Nissan they were redeemed, and in Nissan they are destined to be redeemed."

- We hold according to Rabbi Yehuda that the Geulah is in Nissan, based on the rule that in a dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehuda, the halacha follows Rabbi Yehuda, as is brought in the sefer *Klalei HaShas*. See also Rashi and Tosafos in Rosh Hashanah (12a) regarding the statement of our Sages of blessed memory about the Flood according to Rabbi Eliezer, where they write that since they calculate the *tekufos* according to Rabbi Yehuda, it follows that they hold like Rabbi Yehuda. And in Tosafos (s.v. *Keman*, Rosh Hashanah 27a), they question this, as we hold like Rabbi Yehuda, etc.

Even according to Rabbi Eliezer, who holds they will be redeemed in Tishrei, Nissan is the month of Geulah because the Exodus from Egypt—the first complete Geulah—took place then.

Just as regarding the Geulah from Egypt, our Sages of blessed memory sought many reasons and merits through which our ancestors were redeemed (see Sotah 11b, Shemos Rabbah ch. 1, and elsewhere), all the more so now, after we have been exiled from our land due to our sins. First, there must be a rectification of the wrong—that is, the removal of the cause of the galus—and only then will the Geulah come. As it is stated in Sanhedrin (97b), "Rabbi Eliezer says: If Yisrael does teshuvah, they will be redeemed... Rabbi Yehuda says: If they do not do teshuvah... He will appoint a king over them whose decrees are as harsh as Haman’s, and Yisrael will do teshuvah." Thus, all agree that teshuvah must precede the Geulah.

In this galus, which is the final galus, beyond the fact that it must rectify the specific matter that caused it—regarding which our Sages of blessed memory said (Yoma 9b) that the Second Beis Hamikdash was destroyed because of sinas chinam—the avodah during the time of this galus will bring about the completion of the birurim. This is hinted at in the words of our Sages of blessed memory (Sanhedrin 98a), "Mashiach will only come in a generation that is entirely meritorious or entirely guilty," so that there will no longer be a mixture of good and evil, the beginning of which was the sin of the Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil.

This is the hint in the Mishnah regarding the beginning of shekalim: "On the first of Adar." The month of Adar is the introduction and preparation for the month of Geulah, which is Nissan (see Rashi s.v. *Mishenichnas Adar*—Taanis 29a—who explains that these were days of miracles for Yisrael, Purim and Pesach). It is known that the day of Rosh Chodesh—the first of Adar—is the head and includes the entire month, which is why we pray then, "Renew for us this month," etc. Therefore, on the first of Adar, the Beis Din announces the general avodah of this month, which is the preparation for the Geulah. There are two aspects to this preparation:

Regarding the shekalim, where all Bnei Yisrael are equal—"the rich shall not give more and the poor shall not give less"—and they become integrated—through the participation of everyone together—into a complete stature to offer a sacrifice to Hashem. They all become one group to do the will of their Father in Heaven, which is the rectification of the specific matter that caused this galus, namely, sinas chinam, as mentioned above.

Furthermore, they announce regarding kilayim, that the kelipah and sitra achra—which is the "other side" and the absolute opposite of the side of holiness, and there is no kilayim (mixing of species) like the mixture of good and evil—must be uprooted and eradicated.

Through this avodah, the statement of our Sages of blessed memory (Taanis 29a) is also fulfilled: "From when Adar enters"—that is, immediately from Rosh Chodesh—"we increase" (a term of command and also a promise) "in simcha."

May the Almighty grant us that for all Bnei Yisrael there will be light, gladness, joy, and honor.

With the blessing of "Immediately to Teshuvah, Immediately to Geulah,"

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

B"H, Sunday, 10th of Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, honorable, and elevated merchant who deals with integrity,
Rabbi Y. Katz, may he live

Peace and blessing!

In response to your first and second letters (which were received together),

Enclosed is the receipt for the donation to the *Melach* [Military Relief] fund.

And although the words of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *shlita*, and his instruction regarding the sending of the "Pocket Encyclopedia - Daily Digest" to you, require no reinforcement,

Nonetheless, a hint and an example can be found in this week’s *parsha*, which begins with the *mitzvah* of the lighting of the *menorah* by the *Kohen*.

We see tangibly in every act of lighting that the one who lights only initiates the first illumination, and thereafter the candle burns of its own accord.

And this same phenomenon applies to every single Jew; even if for some reason he has become distanced from *Torah* and *mitzvos*, it is usually sufficient to awaken him, and then the "candle of Hashem is the soul of man" that is within him will continue to shine of its own accord.

And fortunate is the person who illuminates the world by lighting these candles—the children of Israel—to brighten the darkness of the world, for this merit depends upon him.

With blessings for immediate *teshuvah* and immediate *geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

140

B"H, Purim 5704
Brooklyn

To the honorable Rabbi and Chassid, man of action, etc.,
The Tamim, Moreinu Harav R. M. P. (may he live), Hakohen

Greetings and blessings!

The booklet "Chai Elul, 5703" has just arrived from the bindery. A copy is being sent to you at cost; once you inform us how many copies you desire, they will be sent to you as soon as possible.

In the name of the "Otzar HaChassidim" editorial board, we hereby extend our thanks to you for taking it upon yourself to provide the necessary funds for the printing of the book, which made it possible to publish it promptly.

* * *

The magnitude of the importance of disseminating Chassidus through printing and the like requires no lengthy explanation for you and those like you. The value of this matter is even greater in our current time, in the "heels of Mashiach," when the end of our exile is near. According to the response Mashiach gave to the Baal Shem Tov (during the well-known *aliyas haneshamah*), the coming of Mashiach depends on the spreading of the wellsprings of the Baal Shem Tov’s teachings outward (*chutza*). As explained in the *sicha* of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe (Simchas Torah 5690, see there, sections 32, 52, 53), the precise meaning of the word "outward" is significant.

Now, one must explain the matter of disseminating Chassidus in connection with the allusions of this day of Purim. Incidentally, several details regarding the *mitzvos* of the day will be explained as well:

* * *

The *mitzvos* of the day of Purim are *mishloach manos* (sending portions) from one person to another and *matanos la'evyonim* (gifts to the poor). Our Sages (Megillah 7a and in the *poskim*) state that one must send two types of food to one's fellow and two gifts—either money or food—to two poor people. There are several points here that require precision:

1) What is the connection between *mishloach manos* and Purim? If it is because they made these days days of feasting and joy, and one is obligated to become intoxicated, etc.—surely there is also an obligation of a feast and *Kiddush* on *Yom Tov*. On *Yom Tov*, there is no joy except through meat or wine (in addition to the four cups on Pesach), and on Erev Yom Kippur one is obligated to increase eating and drinking (see *Aruch HaShulchan*, *Orach Chaim* 604, where he lists the opinions on whether this is Rabbinic or Biblical). If so, they should have instituted *mishloach manos* for those days as well, or we should learn from there and not institute it on Purim either.

2) Why is the word "sending" (*mishloach*) used regarding *manos*, whereas regarding the poor it is written simply ("gifts")? Even though one could say that the word "sending" applies to both, it does not say "sending gifts to the poor and portions to one's fellow." Especially since, seemingly, the "sending" is most praiseworthy regarding gifts to the poor, for in *tzedakah* there is the virtue that the giver and receiver do not know one another, as stated in *Bava Basra* (10b). This is only possible when one does not give hand-to-hand, but sends through a *gabbai tzedakah* or the like. In contrast, regarding "one's fellow," this reason does not apply. If so, the concept of "sending" should have been linked to gifts for the poor rather than to portions for one's fellow.

3) What is the precision behind the obligation of one fellow, and two poor people?

4) Why is one obligated to send two items to one's fellow, whereas for a poor person one item suffices? It should be the opposite, as the increase should be in the matter pertaining to *tzedakah*, not to one's fellow. As the Rambam writes (*Hilchos Megillah*, end of ch. 2), it is better for a person to increase gifts to the poor than to increase his feast and *mishloach manos* to his friends. Likewise, the primary obligation should have been an increase in gifts to the poor, not only in the number of people, but also in the number of gifts for each of them.

5) Why is one obligated to send food specifically to one's fellow, while for a poor person it is at his discretion whether to give money or food, even though we find in *tzedakah* that there is a virtue in giving food specifically, as stated in *Taanis* (23b)? If so, all the more so on Purim, where one sends food to one's fellow, should one be required to give food specifically to the poor.

All these points will be understood together, once we understand the essence of Purim properly—or at least in brief, as time does not permit writing at length.

* * *

The essence of Purim is a general matter. This means that just as in the physical realm and the event as it literally occurred, the decree was to destroy all the Jews in all the provinces—and especially according to our *Shas*, which disagrees with *Midrash Rabbah* (*Magen Avraham*, ch. 1) and holds (*Megillah* 11a and there) that Achashverosh ruled over the entire world and his kingdom was complete—the decree was against the entire Jewish people, and the salvation was a general salvation.

Likewise, in the spiritual realm, there was a general aspect to it. As our Sages say (*Shabbos* 88a), at that time they "fulfilled what they had already accepted"—meaning that at that time there was a completion and fulfillment of the acceptance of the Torah and *mitzvos*. Moreover, it was precisely then that it was through an awakening from below (*is'arusa d'letata*), of their own volition, without an awakening from above. This is unlike the Giving of the Torah and the initial acceptance of the Torah, where there was "a great coercion" regarding the Torah.

The Giving of the Torah and the *mitzvos* is about Hashem having a "dwelling place in the lower worlds" specifically. As Moshe Rabbeinu replied to the angels (*Shabbos* 88b): "Did you go down to Egypt? Were you enslaved to Pharaoh? Why should the Torah be yours?" They did not answer him that the Torah should be given to them in a spiritual sense.

This has already occurred in the world in a manner similar to the Giving of the Torah, but afterwards, the Jewish people and the world became coarsened. Since then, the matter depends on the *avodah* of *mitzvos* of the entirety of the Jewish people, through which they also elevate the entirety of this lower world, as is understood from *Tanya*, chapters 36 and 37. Just as at the Giving of the Torah it is said, "And Israel encamped there" (*Vayichan*)—in the singular, as one person—and through this they merited the Giving of the Torah (see *Yalkut Shimoni*, Yisro, remez 273; *Torah Or*, *Biur* on the *maamar* "U'bvo'ah Lifnei Hamelech," at the end), so too regarding the miracle of Purim it is said, "The Jews accepted" (*V'kibel*)—in the singular. Also, in our *avodah* now, a primary condition is that we must all be "as one," as stated in *Tanya*, chapter 32, and explained well in the book *Derech Mitzvosecha*.

The conclusion from all the above: Since Purim is the fulfillment and completion of the acceptance of the *mitzvos*, the essential matters of *mitzvos* must be alluded to within it—namely, His dwelling place in the lower worlds, and the condition that we must all be as one. Furthermore, on Purim, all this must come not from an awakening from above, but through the *avodah* of man.

* * *

We now come to the explanation of the details of the allusions of Purim.

Our Sages say (*Shemos Rabbah*, beginning of Yisro; Rashi on *Shabbos* 31a): "Your friend and your father's friend, do not abandon"—this refers to the Holy One, Blessed be He. Seemingly, what friendship and connection can exist between a lowly creature and the Essence of the Infinite? Rather, it arose in His will that at the giving of the Torah, "I was, as it were, sold with it" (*Shemos Rabbah*, ch. 33), and the reward of a *mitzvah* is a *mitzvah*—meaning *tzavta* (connection) and union, etc., as explained at length in the words of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, in *HaTamim* (1, 28; the core of the matter is in *Hayom Yom*, p. 102).

It is known that this is the reason that the *mitzvos* are referred to with terms of eating and drinking (*Midrash Koheles* 2:24: "Every eating and drinking mentioned in this Megillah refers to Torah and good deeds," etc.), and they are compared to bread, etc.—both from the perspective of man and from the perspective of Above, as it were. As it is written, "Go, eat of my bread" (*Mishlei* 9:5; see *Likutei Torah*, *Biur* on the *maamar* "Vayedaber... HaMatos," and elsewhere, that this also refers to *mitzvos*). "My bread," and "you too, eat."

This is the general concept of *mitzvos*. But specifically, there is a distinction within the *mitzvos* themselves: all the *mitzvos*—and the love and fear that are merely "wings" (*Tanya*, end of ch. 40; see there for the reason)—are called gold and silver (in Chassidus—*Torah Or*, *Torah Chayim*, and elsewhere—the *drushim* of *Vayeshev*), whose purpose is to acquire the matters of eating and drinking mentioned above.

This is the *mitzvah* of Purim: 1) *Mishloach manos*—"man" (this is the Jewish people) to his "fellow" (this is the Holy One, Blessed be He, as above), and specifically types of food and drink. For gold and silver (love and fear)—even if one is a perfectly righteous person, he will not reach the level of his attachment to Hashem in the world of *Tohu* before his descent into this world (*Tanya*, ch. 37). And that is not the purpose of his descent below, which is to create a dwelling place in the lower worlds through "today to do them" specifically.

And specifically *two* portions: if one gives only one portion, meaning one type of *mitzvah*, there is room for the concern that he does so because of his bodily nature, and there is no element of a "dwelling place in the lower worlds" in it, similar to the doubt raised by R. Yochanan ben Torta (*Avodah Zarah* 18a), "What am I for the life of the World to Come?", as explained in *Torah Or*, *Toldos*, regarding "many waters."

It is specified that it be *mishloach manos* (sending portions), because the *mitzvos* must be elevated through natural or intellectual love and fear, etc., as explained in *Tanya* (ch. 40 and the glosses there), and not remain below in this physical world, which is the abode of the *kelipos*.

Now, all this is regarding oneself. But as explained above, we must all be one—"encamped" and "accepted" in the singular. Therefore, the second *mitzvah* of Purim comes: *matanos la'evyonim* (gifts to the poor). This means that one must strive and rebuke one's fellow, the "poor" one—poor in knowledge—so that he, too, will engage in *mitzvos*. This does not contradict what was explained above, that on Purim the *avodah* must be from one's own self specifically. For this comes to negate an awakening from above, which is a kind of compulsion and necessity, like "He coerced them with the mountain like a cask... there shall be your burial," as is also understood from the proof in *Torah Or* (beginning of *maamar* "Chayav Inish L'vasumei") that in the days of Purim there was no awakening from above because of the verse, "And I will surely hide My face," etc. But it does not come to negate the matter of "you shall surely rebuke" and the like through human beings; that is simple. However, the methods to awaken one's fellow are not the same for everyone: some are moved and begin to put on *tefillin*—for example—by seeing another putting on *tefillin*, while others are moved and begin to put on *tefillin* by having another explain to them the concept of the love and fear of Hashem. Therefore, the "gifts" can be money or food, because their role is not to create a dwelling place in the lower worlds themselves, but to be the cause for the poor person to create a dwelling place for Him in the lower worlds.

Therefore, even one gift suffices, for even if you say that the giver gives the gift because of his bodily nature, if he only succeeded in having the poor person fulfill a *mitzvah* for its own sake, that is sufficient, as is understood.

This is similar to what our Sages say (*Bava Basra* 9a) regarding physical *tzedakah*: "If they do *tzedakah*, good; and if not, the nations of the world come and take it by force, and even so it is considered *tzedakah* for them," etc.

But one must give to *two* poor people, because in general there are two types of people who need awakening from their fellows. For the fulfillment of *mitzvos* must be through the participation and *avodah* of both the body and the soul together, as our Sages say (*Sanhedrin* 91a and there) in the parable of the lame and the blind. In the language of Chassidus, the *avodah* must be both from the perspective of the animal soul and from the perspective of the divine soul, and one cannot suffice without the other. Both require effort, for the soul also became coarsened through its descent below. Or, among the types of servants of Hashem, there are: 1) those who serve Hashem with their bodies, 2) those who serve Hashem with their souls. This is why one must give to two poor people.

* * *

Now, in the effort to influence one's fellow to bring him closer to *mitzvos*, there are several ways, which can be broadly divided into two:

1) Describing to him the lowliness of man, the loathsomeness and filth of evil, the punishments of *Gehinnom*, etc.—meaning that the engagement and speech are about what one must distance oneself from. Broadly speaking, this is the path of the *Mussar* school.

2) Explaining to him and elaborating on the greatness of the Creator, "How great are Your works," the infinite loftiness of *mitzvos*, etc.—meaning that the engagement and speech are about what one must draw close to. Broadly speaking, this is the path of the Chassidic school.

Now, one of the differences between them is: when one strives with his fellow by proposing the first type—how evil and bitter is the fate of the sinner, the lowliness of the man who craves the pleasures of this world, and the like—the proposal of the matter from its own perspective is not a *mitzvah*, were it not for the hope that through this his fellow will return to the straight path. In contrast, in the second type, even the proposal of the matter from its own perspective is a high and exalted *mitzvah*, for it is the knowledge of the *Seder Hishtalshelus*, "Know the G-d of your father," to unify Him, etc. And in the allusions mentioned above: the first type is only a matter of *matanos la'evyonim*, whereas in the second type, if there is no ulterior motive, it is *mishloach manos* to one's fellow and also a gift to the poor. Within Chassidus itself, there are both matters in a way of "from above to below" (within man himself). In contrast, *sichos* that consist of stories, explanations of intellectual matters in Chassidus, awakening to *avodah*, etc., pertain to both types of "poor" mentioned above.

Through the publication—for its own sake—and the widespread dissemination of a *sicha* of Chassidus that includes matters of Torah and Chassidus, awakening to the fear of Heaven and the fulfillment of *mitzvos*, and the history of our Rebbes and the elder Chassidim—from whom each person will learn and do likewise in his own way—this is a matter that includes the allusions of the *mitzvos* of *mishloach manos* and *matanos la'evyonim* in all their details.

The above is by way of possibility, as is understood, and may Hashem atone for me.

With the blessing of a happy Purim, immediately to *teshuvah*, immediately to *Geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 144

144

B"H, Purim 5704

To the honorable, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, etc.,
Rabbi D. Halevi, may he live.

Greetings and Blessings!

The *kuntres* "Chai Elul 5703" has just been published, and a copy is enclosed herewith.

It is with pleasure and joy that I inform you that this *kuntres* was printed through the efforts of your children, may they live, and in particular your son-in-law, the Torah scholar, G-d-fearing man of energy, etc., the *Tamim* Rabbi M. P., may he live, and his household, on the condition that the printing be in the name of his father-in-law—that is, yourself (together with the name of another individual, whom your son-in-law persuaded to participate in the expenses of this *kuntres*), as you will see printed in this *kuntres*.

It is known from our Sages (Rashi, *Shabbos* 23b) that "a son-in-law is like a son," although, naturally, there are several differences between them (see also *Yevamos* 24a; *Shulchan Aruch Yoreh Deah*, sec. 240, sub-sec. 24, and the *Bach* and *Beurei HaGra* there). Regarding the differences concerning the father versus the father-in-law, this is based on what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *shlita*, said (Sicha of Shemini Atzeres 5737, sections 48-49): "For children born to one, one is not a *baal bechirah* (one does not choose them)." (It is understood that there is no contradiction here from *Eduyos*, ch. 5, mishnah 7, and similar statements of our Sages; see also *Tanya*, end of ch. 2). "The students of the Yeshiva are 'children of choice'—one can choose them." From this it is understood that one can better discern the quality of a person by seeing the nature of the son-in-law he chose for his daughter (as it is written, "I gave my daughter to this man") than by seeing the nature of his own son.

Now, Yisroel are called children to the *Makom* (the Omnipresent), as it is written, "You are children to Hashem your G-d." They are also a *chassan* (son-in-law) to the *Makom*. As it is stated in *Shemos Rabbah*, beginning of *Parshas Terumah*: "A parable of a king who had an only daughter (this is the Torah). One of the kings (this is Yisroel) came and took her." The difference between the two levels is—in the language of Chassidus—that being a *chassan* to the *Makom* is only through the marriage to the Torah; that is, through great *avodah* in *mitzvos* until the Torah becomes his own and he is the one who governs it, like a husband and wife, as our Sages say in *Kiddushin* (32a and there). This is unlike a son, for even a minor or an incompetent son is considered a son in every respect, because a father’s love for his son does not depend on the son’s manifest merits, but is an essential love.

Furthermore, our Sages mention that there is a superiority in a *chassan* over a son in *Shir HaShirim Rabbah* on the verse "We will rejoice and be glad in you": "He said to them, 'Your children have come,' etc., and he adds later: 'He said to them, 'Your *chassanim* (sons-in-law) have come,' etc.," which implies that there is an additional joy here beyond the redemption of the children (see there; see also *Likutei Torah*, end of the *biur* on "V'chol banayich").

This is also the virtue of this day, the day of Purim. Our Sages say (*Shabbos* 88a), "The Jews fulfilled and accepted"—they fulfilled what they had already accepted. For at the Giving of the Torah, the acceptance of the Torah was through the mountain being held over them like a barrel. Our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe explains (*Torah Or*, discourse beginning "Chayav Inish L'vasumei B'puraya") that this refers to the revelation of the Supreme Love from Above upon Yisroel... a love that embraces the Community of Yisroel and encompasses it from every side, even to the level of the "back," so that it does not allow one to turn away from Him, and one is compelled to stand face-to-face with Him.

Purim adds to this, for then they "fulfilled"—in the words of the Alter Rebbe—"and accepted it with complete willingness." This was through *mesiras nefesh* by all of Yisroel themselves, not through an arousal from Above initially, in the manner of the revelation of the level of "His right hand embraces me." For at that time, there was a state of *hester panim* (concealment of the countenance)... as our Sages say, "Where is Esther hinted at in the Torah? In the verse, 'And I will surely hide (haster astir), etc.'"

At that time, it is considered the completion of the acceptance of the Torah—the marriage of the only daughter of the King of Kings—one becomes the "son-in-law of the Almighty."

May it be the will [of Hashem] that you receive much *nachas* and pleasure from all your children and sons-in-law, may they live, and may each one add strength to increase and glorify the Torah.

May we all merit, together with the entire House of Yisroel, in this time of *hester panim* (*Chagigah* 8a and there), on that day, the fulfillment of the prophecy of Zechariah (14): "And it shall come to pass on that day... at evening time there shall be light... and Hashem shall be King over all the earth; on that day Hashem shall be One and His Name One."

With blessings for a happy Purim, immediately to *teshuvah*, immediately to *geulah*.

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

B"H, Purim 5704

2) Since all the copies of the Tanya that were in our possession have been exhausted, and, thank G-d, the number of those wishing to study them has increased, we ask you to endeavor to have approximately one hundred copies of the Tanya printed in Eretz Yisrael sent to us, if possible, and also to inform us of their price.

3) One of our friends has asked us to find someone who could inspire a certain bochur in Eretz Yisrael, whose name is... who in the past, it seems, studied and walked in the straight path, but has since turned away from it. The grandfather of this bochur is R'...

It is possible to clarify with him everything necessary regarding the bochur.

We would appreciate it if you would inform us of the results in this matter.

With the blessing of "Immediately to Teshuvah, immediately to Geulah,"

M. Schneerson
