## 142

18 Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the distinguished Avreich, Torah scholar, G-d-fearing, etc.,
The Tamim, Moreinu HaRav Avraham Shmuel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your notification regarding your engagement conditions:

May it be the will of Hashem that all the blessings with which the Rebbe, my father-in-law, Shlita, has blessed you be fulfilled literally and in their intended meaning, in a revealed and manifest good.

One may explain the precise language of the *Shtar Tena'im* (engagement contract) at its beginning and core—following our custom, the custom of the house of our Rebbe: "The beginning of the matter... neither this from that, nor that from this."

The words "this" (*zeh*, masculine) and "that" (*zo*, feminine) indicate that one can point to the object in a tangible, concrete way. As our Sages of blessed memory state (end of *Taanis* and elsewhere), "He pointed with his finger and said, 'This is the G-d...'" Therefore, our Sages learned (in *Yalkut Shimoni* on the passage, from the *Mechilta*) from the scriptural phrasing "This is my G-d," that the maidservant at the Sea saw what the prophets Yechezkel and Yeshayah did not see. For the prophets prophesied with the term *koh* ("thus"), while only Moshe prophesied with the term *zeh* ("this") (*Sifrei, Matos*). The Rambam explains (in *Yesodei HaTorah*, ch. 7, law 6) the distinction between them: "All the prophets... see... in a parable and a riddle... Moshe Rabbeinu... sees the matter clearly..." (This concept is explained according to Chassidus in the *maamarim* on *Parshas Matos*—in *Likutei Torah* and elsewhere).

Therefore, the true definition of the term "this" applies specifically to Hashem and those who are attached and unified with Him. As is written in *Erech Chaim* (by the author of *Seder HaDoros*, and this book is cited in *Likutei Torah*, *d.h. Mizmor Shir Chanukas*, and elsewhere) under the entry for *Zeh*: "All created beings are subject to change; what existed yesterday is not today, and what is today will not be tomorrow, so it is not accurate to say of them, 'This is.' But regarding Hashem, there is no change before Him, as it is written, 'I, Hashem, have not changed.'" Only by way of analogy can "this" be applied to other things, and even to the realm of *zeh l'umas zeh* (the forces of impurity), as noted in *Likutei Torah*, *d.h. Re'u* (end of section 1) and *d.h. Chukas* (end of section 2).

With this, we can understand the statement of our Sages (*Bava Basra* 16b) that Esav denied the fundamental principle, for it is written, "Why is this (*zeh*) to me?" and it is written, "This (*zeh*) is my G-d." And in *Sanhedrin* (42a), "Whoever blesses the new month at its proper time is as if he welcomes the presence of the Shechinah, for it is written here, 'This (*hazeh*) month,' and it is written there, 'This (*zeh*) is my G-d.'" Seemingly, the word "this" is written many times in the Tanach—but with the above, it is understood.

And in *Menachos* (53a), "The mighty one will come... the beloved one... the good one... this (*zeh*), that (*zo*), this (*zos*)..." This is difficult to understand: it is understandable that the titles "mighty," "beloved," and "good" carry a level of praise, but what is the elevation in calling them "this" or "that"? However, according to the above, the true name "this" applies only to Hashem, and by extension to Israel (*zo*) and the Torah (*zos*), since Israel, the Torah, and Hashem are all one.

(See *Menachos* in *Chiddushei Aggados* for his words—similar to what is written in *Likutei Torah*, *Chukas*, mentioned above.)

Now, that this revelation—that Hashem is called "this," and that this applies to the whole world (for at the Splitting of the Sea it was only for Israel)—was to be at the time of the Giving of the Torah, as mentioned in *Menachos*. However, afterwards, it became materialized in the world, and through the *avodah* of *mitzvos* by all of Bnei Yisrael, it will be so in the time to come, as mentioned in *Taanis*. See *Sefer Shel Beinonim*, chapter 36 and onwards.

It is explained in many places that at the Giving of the Torah, and especially in the time to come (when the reasons for the Torah—the inner dimensions of the Torah—will be revealed), this is the aspect of the marriage of Hashem ("this") and Israel ("that").

Now, the lower man is in the likeness of the higher man, and regarding Bnei Yisrael—"You are called 'man'" (*Yevamos* 61a)—it is said, "Hashem is your shadow," meaning that, as it were, He is like a shadow that follows the person (*Likutei Torah*, *d.h. Mah Tovu*, end of section 4, and elsewhere). Therefore, when they engage in the marriage of man and woman, the beginning of the matter is... "this"... "that"... and the wise will understand.

With the longing that we may all merit the fulfillment of the prophecy, "And it shall be said in that day, 'Behold, this is our G-d...'"

Immediately to Teshuvah, immediately to Geulah.

Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

After a long time: I found in *Ohr HaTorah* (351b) a discussion of this "this" and "that" in the *Tena'im*. It also explains the conclusion, "and they shall have dominion over their possessions equally," meaning that *Zon* (Ze'er Anpin and Nukvah) will be equal in their stature (in the time to come).

## 143

143

B"H, 18 Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the distinguished Avreich, the G-d-fearing, refined, and honored...
The Tamim, Rabbi [Yitzchak] HaKohen, may he live.

Peace and Blessing!

In response to your invitation to participate in your joy, the joy of the engagement (Tana'im) on this day, I offer my prayer that it may be fulfilled in you: "He who finds a wife finds good, and obtains favor from Hashem, the Good."

Regarding the text of the Tana'im at their inception—in the version of our Rebbe’s house: "The first matter... he shall marry in a good hour, according to the law of Moshe and Yisrael, and they shall not cause to flee, and they shall not cause to conceal... and they shall dwell together in love and affection, as is the custom of the world."

Seemingly, it is puzzling, for it would have been appropriate to place "and they shall dwell together..." immediately after "he shall marry in a good hour—according to the law of Moshe and Yisrael," and only afterwards to state "and they shall not cause to flee, etc."

This can be understood by first introducing a general premise: that Bnei Yisrael are children to Hashem. Just as a child is in the image and likeness of the father, so too, as it were, "man is likened to the Supernal." It is self-evident that all of his matters and conduct—those that are according to Torah, or according to the custom of Yisrael, which is Torah—are in the likeness of that which is Above.

Specifically in marriage, and everything pertaining to it in the most minute detail, the man and woman are in the likeness of Ze'er Anpin and Malchus, and higher than that, in their very root. With this, we can understand why the marriage blessings begin by recounting the purpose of the entire creation, for creation is through ZON.

Now, the beginning of the marriage arrangement is the arrangement of the Tana'im (the first ones), and at their beginning comes the instruction of the purpose and the essence. This is: "The first matter is that he shall marry in a good hour," and it explains the matter—that "they shall not cause to flee and they shall not cause to conceal, etc."

The explanation of this is as follows:

It is known regarding the manner of creation: initially, there was a simple Supernal Light filling the entire reality. When it arose in His simple will to create the worlds, He Tzimtzum-ed that light, and a void and empty space remained. Afterwards, He drew down a single Kav, etc.—as is written and explained in Torah Or, in the discourse *Pasach Eliyahu*, in Likutei Torah, in the discourse *Lehavin Mah Shekasuv* in Etz Chayim, and elsewhere. Now, this Tzimtzum differs from all other Tzimtzumim, in that its essence is removal, not merely diminution. Through this, it is possible for the Kav to be drawn down afterwards—that is, the light that is enclothed in vessels—and for the existence of the worlds to be possible (see the discourse *Lema'an Yedei Doroseichem* 5708, and elsewhere). This is the effect of the Tzimtzum on the infinite light and the boundary (see the discourse *BeYom HaShemini Atzeres* 5699).

Now, the ultimate intent is the revelation of the light that was before the Tzimtzum. The innovation is: a) that this light should be revealed in the worlds; b) that there should be a revelation of a Supernal Light that is even higher than the previous light. This is the "addition of lights" that is drawn down through Mitzvos and Torah (see Yom Tov of Rosh Hashanah, *Resh* 266, *Lema'an Yedei* mentioned above, and elsewhere). However, all of this is possible only because the first Tzimtzum was for the sake of the aforementioned revelation.

Now, the revelation of these two matters will be specifically in the time to come, for then is the true aspect of marriage (see Likutei Torah, end of the explanation of *Al Kol Kavod Chuppah*), the revelation of the inner dimension of the Torah, etc. But it is dependent upon the Avodah of Torah and Mitzvos that precedes it.

This is what is meant when we explain that in the Tana'im, the purpose is that he shall marry in a good hour; we say that they shall not "cause to flee"—the light from before the Tzimtzum that was Tzimtzum-ed and removed shall not be caused to flee, but rather it shall be revealed. Included in this are also the revelations that are higher than that (see *Tik'u* 94, that in the time to come there will be a revelation of the Essence, not in the manner of "giver and receiver," and note the explanation of *Velo Tashbit* 63). And "they shall not cause to conceal"—the light that was concealed in the vessel, the existence of the worlds which is currently in a state of concealment, will in the time to come be "the soul shall be nourished by the body," and the wise will understand.

I pray that in our own days, we shall all merit the fulfillment of the prophecy: "Soon there shall be heard... the voice of the groom and the voice of the bride" (see the explanation of *Kol Kavod* mentioned above).

Immediately to Teshuvah, immediately to Geulah.

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

144

B"H, Wednesday, 20 Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi, the distinguished Chassid, a man of stature, a scholar and teacher of Torah, etc., the Tamim, Moreinu HaRav R' Sh. (may he live),

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter, after requesting forgiveness for the delay due to being occupied—and even now, time does not permit me to answer except briefly, point by point, following the order of your letter:

1) You commented on what I wrote regarding the matter of whether the halacha follows Rabbi Yehuda or the Sages—in their dispute as to whether a person is judged every day—noting that in Chassidic thought it is explained (Likkutei Torah, end of Parshas Korach; Kuntres U'Maayan, p. 17) that "this one says one thing and this one says another," and they do not actually disagree.

My letter was written in response to a questioner who relied on the commentary of the sefer *Mikraei Kodesh* regarding the sugya in Rosh Hashanah where Rabbi Yehuda and the Sages disagree. He writes there, quite simply, that the halacha follows the Sages. Regarding this, I expressed surprise, for according to his interpretation, it is not so simple; on the contrary, etc.

2) You bring a proof from Rabbi Chananel (Rosh Hashanah 16a), who derives the reasoning of Rabbi Yehuda from the verse, "To perform the judgment of His servant and the judgment of His people Israel, each day in its day." You argue that we must say "His servant" refers to every person, and this is the proof that one is judged every day. Since he immediately says, "And Rabbi Chananel says: A king and a community—the king enters first, as it is written, 'the judgment of His servant,' and then 'His people Israel,'" which interprets the word "His servant" as referring to the king—then, in order not to contradict himself, we must say that the first statement was said according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, but he himself does not hold that way.

In my humble opinion, all of this is untenable for several reasons: a) It is not at all the style of the Gemara to first seek a reason for Rabbi Yehuda, even though he does not hold like him, and say his reason is from here, and then immediately state a teaching that necessitates the opposite interpretation of the verse without noting or hinting at this at all. b) If "His servant" refers to every person, then what is added by the words "and the judgment of His people Israel"? One cannot answer that it comes to add the judgment of the community, for if so, the request is redundant; if the judgment of the individual is performed every day, it is obvious that the judgment of the community, which is more important, is performed. c) If "His servant" refers to every person, then the judgment of the individual would precede the judgment of the community, which is the opposite of the reasoning of Rabbi Chananel. d) "To perform the judgment of His servant" is from the prayer of King Solomon, and the simple interpretation is that "His servant" refers to the king. If so, what proof or necessity is there from this verse that every person is judged every day, contrary to the opinion of the Sages, when we are, on the contrary, taking the word out of its simple meaning, or at least saying that it refers to a king, and the same applies to anyone else who is not mentioned in the verse?

We must say that the proof for the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda that a person is judged every day is from the verse, "and the judgment of His people Israel, each day in its day," in one of two ways: a) That Rabbi Chananel does not hold of distinguishing between the judgment of an individual and a community regarding being judged every day. b) From the extra word "Israel." The first seems more likely to me.

3) You bring a proof from Rabbi Kruspedai in the name of Rabbi Yochanan, "Three books are opened on Rosh Hashanah," etc., which is brought in the Rambam as halacha, implying that the halacha follows the Sages that one is judged on Rosh Hashanah.

Regarding the ruling of the Rambam, there is no proof, for one could say that he holds as is written in the sefer *Asarah Maamaros* and in Likkutei Torah, that there is no dispute here at all.

And from the statement of Rabbi Kruspedai in the name of Rabbi Yochanan, there is no proof—even for one who holds that Rabbi Yehuda and the Sages do disagree—as one could interpret it according to the view of the Tosafot (s.v. *V'nechtamin*), that it refers to the life of the World to Come. It is possible that even Rabbi Yehuda admits this, since the verse "the judgment of His people Israel," which is the reason of Rabbi Yehuda, and the fact that we pray for the sick and the ailing, refers to the life of this world.

4) You write that since Rabbi Meir (Rosh Hashanah 18a) holds like Rabbi Yitzchak that a cry is effective even after the decree is sealed, presumably Rabbi Yitzchak also holds like Rabbi Meir that a person is judged on Rosh Hashanah, for why should we multiply disputes in a Baraisa?

I did not understand this. Multiplying disputes applies to the same subject itself. But just because Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yitzchak said the same thing regarding the tearing of a decree, does it mean that their opinions are also identical regarding the judgment of Rosh Hashanah? If one were to bring a proof from this Baraisa, the opposite is more logical—that they disagree on this; otherwise, Rabbi Yitzchak would be Rabbi Meir.

5) You bring a proof that the halacha follows the Sages from the words of our Sages (ibid. 18a), "And an individual, when? During the Ten Days of Repentance." Now, there is no proof from this other than that it is easier to do teshuvah during the Ten Days of Repentance than throughout the rest of the year, as it is written, "Seek Hashem when He is found," and consequently the decree is torn up. But there is no proof from this that a person is not judged every day. (And similarly regarding Yom Kippur, which is explicitly stated in the verse, "For on this day," etc.—and yet, there is no contradiction from this to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda.)

6) Regarding what I wrote—that all those Amoraim who hold that the decree of an individual is not torn up must hold like Rabbi Yehuda that a person is judged every day, since they pray for the sick and the ailing—this is explicitly stated in the Gemara (Rosh Hashanah 16a), as I wrote in my previous letter. The words of the Tosafot (s.v. *Keman*) are to explain, according to the Sages, why we recite the blessings of *Refa'enu* and *Hashanim* (and for the sick—according to the version of the Bach). What you wrote to contradict the words of the Gemara, I did not understand.

With the blessing of "immediately to teshuvah, immediately to Geulah,"

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 145

B"H, Wednesday, 20 Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, etc., pursuer of tzedakah and kindness, G-d-fearing, etc.,
Mr. Aryeh Yaakov Lehman, may he live,

Shalom and blessings!

Enclosed, we are sending you a copy of the Kuntres Chai Elul, which we have recently published. Through our friend, the esteemed Rabbi and Chassid, G-d-fearing, etc., Rabbi Moshe Pinchas Katz, may he live, and his wife, may she live, of Newark, we have received your preliminary donation of fifty dollars ($50), which you gave through them as a contribution toward the costs of printing a sefer. In accordance with your request—conveyed through them—we have inscribed the name of your father, R' Chaim ben R' Kalonymus, may he live, as you will see on the cover of the sefer.

Great is the merit of enabling the many to merit, especially through the printing of this important Kuntres.

One of the greatest mitzvos is the mitzvah of tzedakah. However, tzedakah does not mean only helping someone physically—that they should have what to eat, what to wear, for their body, and so on. When one helps someone spiritually, ensuring that their neshamah receives the "sustenance" it requires, and that it does not go about naked, lacking Torah and maasim tovim, this is among the most refined and highest forms of tzedakah that a person can perform, as the Gemara expresses it: "This brings him to the life of this world—this brings him to the life of the World to Come."

And one way in which one can help another spiritually is by enabling them to read the words of hisorerus and the deep explanations and guidance of the great leaders of Yisrael in every generation, and in our generation—the Rebbe, may he live and be well.

May it be His will that in the merit of this great mitzvah, you should soon hear good tidings from your parents and all your friends, and may you merit, together with all of Yisrael, to greet Moshiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days.

With blessings for immediate teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 146

B"H, Tuesday, 26th of Adar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the illustrious Torah scholar and Chassid, a man of stature, maskil, etc.,
The Tamim, Rabbi M.L. Radstein, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

1) Your letter (enclosed in the envelope of the Rebbe, shlita) has reached us. We were unsure whether to wait—in our letter of thanks—until the matter is brought to actual fruition or not. However, judging by your recent correspondence, it seems your opinion is that the letter should be sent immediately. Enclosed is a copy of the letter to Mr. Fleier and to the Weissman brothers.

2) Regarding the completion of the printing: The Rebbe, shlita, has promised to provide his notes from the sichos in Chicago. As soon as they reach us, they will be added to what has already been typeset and prepared for publication.

3) Printing the picture of the deceased: This does not seem appropriate to me at all. Nevertheless, I asked the Rebbe, shlita, and he answered in the negative. That is the way of it.

4) You will surely provide us with the information regarding the life history of the deceased—whatever is known clearly.

5) We received a letter from the bookstore in Chicago. Since we do not know the situation there locally—so that there should be no contradictions between what you have discussed and our response—we have replied as per the enclosed copy, together with the letter we received (the latter, please be kind enough to return to us). We would appreciate it if you could inform us of what is being done in this regard.

6) It would be of interest to know if you have spoken with Mr. Fleier regarding an article from him in the press about our publications (Yalkut Yomi, sichos, our books) and if there were any results from this.

7) "Chai Elul" and our books, Vol. 2 and Vol. 4, have surely arrived, and you will certainly engage in all possible advocacy for their distribution. Enclosed is what we have printed here.

8) We are preparing to publish a bulletin in the coming days, in which we will write about the events in the Chabad world during the period from 19 Kislev 5704 to 9 Adar 5704. If you could provide us—as soon as possible—with this information regarding what has taken place in your camp and its surroundings during this time (appointment of a rabbi, opening of a Talmud Torah, establishing a shiur, and the like), I hope there will be "sufficient time" to include them in this bulletin. It is better that you provide an abundance of information and we condense it here, rather than the reverse, as is understood.

9) It has been suggested to me that perhaps it would be worthwhile for you to speak with the editorial board of *Pardes* so that they might print articles, or at least short reviews, regarding everything published by Kehot, etc., and for the future. And to stir the editor with a loud noise: "You, etc., why has this not been done?" Regarding the matter of payment—if, as I have heard, it is impossible without it—I will rely on your judgment as to whether it is worthwhile.

10) Since the *Morgen Journal* wrote about *Ma'ayan* and *Hayom Yom* (and they did not take money for it!), it is possible that the *Courier* would do the same regarding the aforementioned or our other books. Regarding payment, if it is impossible otherwise, it is as mentioned in the previous paragraph.

I await your response regarding the above, and I will conclude with heartfelt thanks for your efforts in enabling the publication of the kuntres of Chassidus and the dissemination of Torah—and you will surely continue to show strength in the future as well.

The hint in these days between Parshas Parah and Parshas HaChodesh: The reason for the order of these parshiyos is that one must be purified before the offering of the Korban Pesach (end of Megillah in the commentaries)—to be purified and to reach the level of the Exodus from Egypt (Likutei Torah, *d'h Zos Chukas*, end of section 2). As it is written in the Haftorah of Parshas Parah: "And I will sprinkle upon you pure water, and you shall be purified, etc." Now, our Sages have said (Sanhedrin 39a) that the primary "immersion" is in fire (except that anyone who cannot come into the fire because he will be burned, you shall pass him through water—Rashi).

Now, purification (from Above—see *Siddur*, *d'h Eretz Chafetz*, *Hemshech* 656)—in spiritual life—is through Torah. As our Sages said (Eichah Rabbah, beginning): "They have forsaken Me and have not kept My Torah; the light within it would have returned them to the good." And in the Torah itself, which is compared to both water and fire, the primary "immersion" is in fire—the inwardness of the Torah (see *Hayom Yom*, p. 79, regarding this). And as the precision of the language "the light within it" implies, and as is written in the commentaries on the Midrash, there are those who might be burned; therefore, they precede it with passing through water—the revealed aspects of the Torah—and afterwards, fire. (And perhaps it can be said that this is the doubling of the language "pure water, and you shall be purified.")

It can be said that the teachings of Chassidus include both qualities: that of fire, and also that one cannot be burned. On the contrary, the teachings of Chassidus explain the virtue of *shov* (return), and the *avodah* in the animal soul is also necessary, and not in the way of breaking and coercion, as the Baal Shem Tov explained on the verse "If you see the donkey," as is known.

Therefore, specifically in our generations, the *ikvesa d'meshicha* (heels of Moshiach), when the complete redemption "as in the days of your exodus from Egypt" is being revealed, the light of Chassidus is being revealed more and more (see the letter of the Rebbe Rayatz printed in *HaTamim*, Vol. 3, p. 55, and *Kuntres Etz Chaim*)—purification in water and in fire that precedes the redemption—Parshas Parah and afterwards Parshas HaChodesh. And anyone who assists in the purification of the Jewish people, his reward is understood from what is written in the Zohar (Vol. 3, 98a): "Fortunate is the portion of the *chavraya* (the colleagues) when the King calls for the Matronita, who is it that prepares her ornaments, etc."

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

147

B"H, 2 Nissan, the Hilula of the Rebbe [Rayatz]

To the young scholar, the G-d-fearing man, a man of many accomplishments, learned in Torah and wisdom, etc.,
The Tamim, the great scholar, Rabbi A. Hecht, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your invitation to participate in your joy—the joy of the engagement ceremony on this day—I offer my blessing that there be fulfilled in you all that is written in the text of the *Tana'im*: "He who finds a wife finds good, and obtains favor from Hashem" (*Mishlei* 18:22), which encompasses all material and spiritual good, as explained by our Sages (*Yevamos* 63b): "‘He who finds a wife finds good’—does the verse speak of her? ... Or does it speak of Torah?"

For just as a person is composed of two parts—the *neshamah* (the essence and the interiority) and the body (the secondary—see *Shaar HaYichudim*, ch. 6—and the exteriority)—so too are his needs divided into two: a) The needs of the *neshamah*, which are *Torah* and *mitzvos*. This is why they are referred to as "eating and drinking" for the *neshamah*, as our Sages say in *Koheles Rabbah* (2:24), using the analogy of eating and drinking, which are essential for the life of the body. b) The needs of the body, which are all material matters.

Our Sages state in *Pe'ah* (1:1) that the study of Torah is equivalent to all the *mitzvos*, and in *Kiddushin* (40b) that study is great because it leads to action. Rashi explains that both are found in his hand (see the careful analysis in the language of the Alter Rebbe in *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, ch. 4, secs. 2-3—and note the reference to the *Pe'ah* commentary there—and in *Likutei Torah*, *d.h. Vayedaber G-d... Anochi*, in *Parshas Bamidbar*). These are the needs of the *neshamah*. Regarding the needs of the body, our Sages state (*Yevamos* 63a): "In what way does a woman make a man a man? He says: 'A man brings wheat, he chews wheat; he brings flax, he wears flax,'" which includes "all the needs of man," in the language of the Ramban on *Devarim* (10:18).

On a deeper level, through Torah, one draws down all revelations, both regarding spiritual life and material life, as they say regarding Torah (*Berachos* 61b), "for it is our life." These are drawn downward so that there will be "a blessing to rest upon your house" (*Yechezkel* 44:30) specifically through the wife (*Yevamos* 62b, and as explained in *Likutei Torah*, end of *Parshas V'zos HaBerachah*).

However, all this is not yet sufficient, and one must also "obtain favor from Hashem." For regarding Torah, our Sages say (*Yoma* 72b) that if, G-d forbid, one does not "merit" (*zacha*—a term implying refinement and clarification), it—the Torah—becomes for him [a potion of death].

—And one should not raise a difficulty against this statement from the teaching of our Sages (*Eichah Rabbah*, introduction) that "the light within it returns one to the good," as explained in *Hilchos Talmud Torah* there and in the *Kuntres Acharon*.—

The same applies in *Sotah* (17a), where Rashi explains that both are found [in his hand], and regarding material matters, as our Sages say (*Pesachim* 119a), and the Rashbam explains that through [Torah]... (see *Kuntres U'Ma'ayan*, ch. 40). Therefore, they interpret the blessing (*Yevamos* 63b) "and obtains favor" to mean that his *avonot*—a term for crookedness and distortion, for he has strayed, G-d forbid, from the path of the King, the King of Kings—are "straightened out" (*mitpakkin*). This is through *Havayah*—specifically the Name *Havayah*, as explained in the *Hemshech* of 5666 and in the *maamar* *Anochi* 5688—whereupon he does not incline to the right or to the left, but is directed toward the absolute truth, both in Torah and in *avodah*.

In *Talmud Torah*, this is through the study of the interiority of the Torah—quite simply, to learn *Chassidus* and to toil until one understands—as is understood from the *Kuntres Eitz Chaim* by the author of the *Hilula*, and hinted at in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, Epistle 26; see also *Likutei Torah*, *d.h. Velo Tashbit*.

In *avodah*, this is through *yiras shamayim* and being "placid and humble." As the *maamar* *Anochi* mentioned above concludes: "And even though... they were sharp-witted... yet because of their *bitul*, they were specifically the vessels to grasp the absolute truth."

With the blessing of *Mazal Tov*, for immediate *teshuvah*, for immediate *Geulah*.

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee
