## 149

B"H, Erev Pesach, 5704

To the honorable Rav, the Chassid, the expert in Chassidus, etc.,
Moreinu HaRav Avraham Eliyahu, may he live,

Shalom u’Bracha!

I have received your two letters regarding the books from the estate of the late Neuhausen, and I immediately conveyed the matter to my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita. Surely, you have already received a response from him regarding this.

I trust that our recent publications reached you in due time: the Kuntres of 18 Elul, our books (Volumes 2 and 4), the Seder signs for Pesach, and the Kovetz Lubavitch.

It is a pity that you are not utilizing your influence more forcefully among the various circles in your place of residence to increase the number of people reading and studying these books and similar materials.

This is especially true regarding the books on Chinuch. There is a double benefit here: by studying these books, those containing heresy, etc., are pushed out of the school environment.

There are several Chinuch institutions here that cannot be considered Chassidei Chabad in any way—on the contrary, and so on—yet they have introduced the study of these books for this very reason.

It is certainly worthwhile to apply the trait of "his heart was lifted in the ways of Hashem" (divrei ha-yamim II 17:6) in this matter, as even in this country, importance is given to things stated with firmness and decisiveness. Based on what I have heard regarding your influence, I am confident that if you were to attempt to express your opinion with conviction (especially since you can bring proof that even in Bais Yaakov and similar institutions, they study books and pamphlets from the Kehot publication society), you would succeed. A Talmid Chacham must possess a "one-eighth of an eighth" [of pride]. It is explained in Chassidus (Drushei Purim) that through this, one ensures that the wicked have no nourishment.

While on this subject, I will mention what has been difficult for me and others like me in those same Drushim, which explain according to the opinion that one must have "one-eighth of an eighth," whereas the Halacha follows the opinion (Sotah 4a) that one should have "nothing of it, and not even a part of it," as written by the Rambam in Hilchos Deos (Chapter 2, Halacha 3), and as ruled by our Rebbe HaZaken in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim (55:5 and 56:3).

I will conclude with what you concluded regarding the Geulah. I have heard a saying common among the Chassidim of Poland: "Shemini shemonah shemenah" (meaning: the year in which we read Parshas Shemini—like this year—will be "fat" [prosperous]). Perhaps it can be said that this has a basis in what is explained in Chassidus regarding the virtue of the number eight: that in the future, the harp will have eight strings, as written in Likkutei Torah (Drush "LaMenatzeach Al HaSheminis"). And the reading of "Vayehi Bayom HaShemini" is like what is written in Chassidus regarding the drawing down of Binah into all the boundaries (Drush "Lehavin... Yom Tov Shel Rosh Hashanah," section 6), the two aspects of the fiftieth gate (Drush "Me'at Tosefet Beur Al Inyan U'Sephartem"), and in general, it is that which is within Binah itself—the three upper Sefiros, and not the seven lower ones—which in their entirety are the Sefirah of Binah, as is known regarding "Tavo'i Be'edei Adim" and "Kodesh HaKodashim" (Drush "Eileh Pekudei"). First, one must bring proof from the Torah that this is how it must be—similar to what is written regarding the verses of Malchiyos, etc. (the aforementioned Drush "Lehavin" and in Hemshech 5705). This is the meaning of the idea that when we read Parshas Shemini, the year is "fat." As my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita, related in the name of the Maggid of Mezritch: the explanation of "On the eighth day, an Atzeres" (Bamidbar 29:35) is: "Shemini" (eighth) and also "Shemen" (oil/fat), and the virtue of Shemini Atzeres is "one bull" (Par Echad), etc., and "there shall be no stranger with you." This will be fulfilled upon the arrival of the complete Geulah, speedily in our days, through Moshiach Tzidkeinu.

With blessings for a Kosher and joyous Pesach, immediately to Teshuvah, immediately to Geulah,

Menachem Schneerson

## 150

[Beginning of Summer, 5704]

For some time now, we have been approached with requests to publish the sichos, maamarim of Chassidus, and letters of the Rebbe Rayatz that relate to his visit to the city of Chicago in the month of Shevat 5702.

For several reasons, this matter has been delayed until now.

\* \* \*

I am taking this opportunity to respond to the many who ask questions and raise difficulties regarding the sichos or maamarim.

Although the vast majority of the sichos are understandable to everyone—even to those who have no prior knowledge of the Chabad system—there are nonetheless matters within them, especially those that were mentioned only in passing, that cannot be fully understood without first studying the teachings of Chassidus. At the very least, one requires knowledge of the fundamentals of this system, which are not explained from beginning to end in every single sicha, in accordance with the well-known principle that "words of Torah are poor in one place and rich in another."

It is self-evident that if someone finds a difficulty in a sicha or maamar, and based on the fact that he does not know the answer to his question, he concludes that the sicha or maamar has no validity for him—even though his entire knowledge of the Chabad system and its foundations is only a day old, and often even that "knowledge" is superficial or mistaken—then the parable used by the Rambam in his introduction to the Commentary on the Mishnah applies to him. In discussing the study of the secrets of the Torah, the Rambam writes: "I will give you a clear parable: Suppose a man... intelligent and wise, who is awake and knowledgeable in geometry and astronomy, is asked, 'What do you say about a man who claims that the body of the sun...' [and he knows] how many miles it measures... That man, being of clear mind... will have no doubt that this claim is false... But when he accustoms his soul to the study... the claim will appear to him as a truth beyond doubt... and he will believe it with complete faith. How much more so is this the case with one who has no wisdom at all... but has only traveled from the intellect of his mother to the intellect of his wife, and we ask him one of the divine questions that are hidden in the drashos; there is no doubt that they are as far from his eyes as the sun is from the earth, and his intellect is too limited to understand any of them," end quote.

Just as a person suffering from a foot ailment would not go to seek a cure from an architect—even if that architect has used his wisdom to build great and magnificent structures—but would instead seek the advice of a physician who is an expert in that field; and if the architect is an honest man, he himself would explain to the patient that although he is a genius in the art of building houses, he has no understanding whatsoever of the art of medicine, and therefore the patient must turn to a doctor—

So too, one who finds a difficulty in a point or detail of a maamar of Chassidus and wishes to have it explained truthfully should turn only to those who study the teachings of Chassidus and are engaged in them.

And if, despite this, he errs and brings his question to someone who is not an expert in the teachings of Chassidus, then if the person he approached is an honest and upright man, he will admit and say: "I am great, thank G-d, in such-and-such a field of Torah, but I have not studied the teachings of Chassidus as required; therefore, you must turn specifically to someone who is an expert in this system."

#### ***150***

From the Rebbe’s holy handwriting. Written as an introduction to the *Kuntres* of the Chicago visit, which was printed at the beginning of the summer of 5704. For reasons unknown to us, this was not printed in the introduction. A portion of what is presented here was incorporated into Letter 154 below.

## 151

B"H, Monday, 1st of Iyar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the honorable Rabbi and Chassid, a man of action, possessing refined character traits, etc., R' Chaim Tzvi, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your invitation to the Bar Mitzvah of your son, may he live: since I am unable to be present, I send my blessings that he may go from strength to strength in both material and spiritual matters, and that he may be a G-d-fearing person, a Chassid, and a scholar.

Regarding connecting the concept of Bar Mitzvah with this day, the 26th of Nissan, one may suggest a homiletical insight:

The concept of Bar Mitzvah is that one becomes obligated in the performance of mitzvos, at which point the *yetzer hatov* becomes joined to the person (*Koheles Rabbah* 4:13). This means that this is the completion and the primary entry of the *neshamah kedoshah* into the person (*Shulchan Aruch Admur HaZaken*, *Orach Chaim*, *Mahadura Basra*, section 64).

—It is worth noting the precision of the language in *Koheles Rabbah*, "joined" (*mizdavveg*) specifically—

At that point, he goes out to war against the *yetzer hara* to conquer (*Tanya*, ch. 9) the "small city," which is the body (*Nedarim* 32b, and see the commentary of the Rosh there).

And how is it possible to defeat the *yetzer hara*? Regarding this, our Sages said (*Bava Basra* 16a): "The Holy One, Blessed be He, created the *yetzer hara*, and He created the Torah as its antidote."

Now, the main battle with the *yetzer hara* began upon entering Eretz Yisrael. For in the desert, the Jewish people had almost no engagement with the affairs of this world; they ate bread from heaven, their garments did not wear out, and the Clouds of Glory surrounded them and killed the snakes.

—In *Sanhedrin* (108a), regarding the generation of the desert, there is a dispute between Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Eliezer. It is implied that the *halachah* follows Rabbi Eliezer, that they have a share in the World to Come, as Rav Bivi bar Abaye cites Rabbi Yochanan in accordance with Rabbi Eliezer (*ibid.* 110b). Specifically, according to what is written in the book *Asarah Maamaros*, *Maamar Tikkun*, Part 2, ch. 8, Rabbi Akiva’s opinion that they have no share in the World to Come means that they have no *need* for the World to Come. According to this interpretation, the difficulty is resolved: according to Rabbi Akiva, how could the dead of the desert be compared to those who are asleep—as noted in *Tosafos* s.v. *V'damu* (*Bava Basra* 73b).—

Therefore, it was specifically Yehoshua bin Nun, who was the leader of the generation that entered the Land, of whom our Sages said (*Megillah Rabbah*, *Pesichta* 10): "Yehoshua was the first of the conquerors." And since the ability to win this war is through Torah, as mentioned above, Yehoshua bin Nun received the entire Torah from Moshe Rabbeinu, as our Sages said (*Temurah* 16a): "He said to Yehoshua, 'Ask all the doubts you have.' He replied, 'My master, have I left you for even one hour...?'"

—Except that afterward, as a punishment, three hundred *halachos* were forgotten by him, etc. One should examine the precise language of the Rambam in his introduction to the *Yad* and in the commentaries on the Mishnah, beginning of *Avos*, ch. 4, but this is not the place to elaborate.—

Now, our Sages said (end of *Berachos*): "Torah scholars have no rest, not in this world, etc.; they go from strength to strength." This means that from day to day they ascend in their holy service, and thus it is understood that on the day of their passing, they are at their most exalted level.

—To be noted from the commentary of the *Metzudos* on *Michah* 2:10, and all the places cited in the book *Maavar Yabok*, *Maamar Alef*, ch. 17.—

Furthermore, there is this advantage on the day of passing: "All the labor of a person that his soul labored during his lifetime, which was in a state of concealment and hiddenness, becomes revealed and shines in a state of revelation from above to below at the time of his passing."

—The language of the Alter Rebbe in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, section 28. One may suggest that this is the intent of our Sages (*Avos*, ch. 6): "At the time of a person's passing, he is not accompanied by... but only by Torah and good deeds"—at the time of passing itself.—

And this illumination shines throughout the entire world in general, and upon his students in particular (as stated in *Iggeres HaKodesh* there and in section 27).

And it is similar every year on this day, as is known regarding the matter of Lag B'Omer. See also *Yevamos* (122a), Rashi s.v. *Telata*, in the name of the *Teshuvos HaGeonim*, that an illumination of the Torah and service of that *tzaddik* shines in the world. The effect of this illumination is assistance from above to walk in the ways of the *tzaddik*. If so, on the day of the passing of Yehoshua bin Nun, it is the same: there is a special *siyatta d'shmaya* to be a "conqueror," just as Yehoshua was the "first of the conquerors." And the passing of Yehoshua bin Nun was on the 26th of Nissan, as stated at the end of *Megillas Taanis* and brought in *Tur Shulchan Aruch*, *Orach Chaim*, section 580.

With blessings for immediate *teshuvah* and immediate *geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

## 152

B"H, Tuesday, 3rd of Iyar, 5704
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

...I have said previously that there is room to suggest that this is the allusion in the words of our Sages (Yevamos 114a) regarding the three times the obligation to "warn the adults concerning the children" is stated: *shkatzim* (abominable creatures), *dam* (blood), *tumah* (ritual impurity).

For regarding the one being educated and guided (who is the "child" in relation to the "adult" who is educating and guiding him in wisdom), there are those who are discouraged and despair of the influence taking effect upon them, due to several reasons which can be categorized into three:

a) They say that the current situation, especially in a certain place, is such that the nature of the people is extremely coarse and materialistic, to the point that "man has no advantage over the beast," and how can matters of Torah and *yiras shamayim* enter their ears? To this comes the command that even regarding one who eats *shkatzim*—which indicates that he is not within the category of a human being at all, for the human soul naturally recoils from them (Horayos 11a)—nevertheless, you are commanded to educate and warn him. And as our Sages testified (Bamidbar Rabbah, beginning of ch. 12) that the Holy One, Blessed be He, only demands according to the capacity of the one commanded, it must be that the educator has the power to act and to achieve results.

b) They say that this applies where it is possible for there to be benefit in advocacy and education—regarding matters to which one has not been accustomed all his life. But if one has already repeated an act two or three times, etc., and has become set in his conduct and actions, there is no hope that he will return from his ways. The negation of this view is explained in the warning to "adults concerning the children" regarding *dam* (blood), for our Sages said (Sifrei) on the verse "Only be strong not to eat the blood"—from here you learn that they were steeped in eating blood. Thus, even in these matters, education is necessary and effective.

c) They say that this applies where one must engage in effort and education only in matters that can be explained to the one being educated through intellect, reason, and logic. But regarding matters that depend on *emunah* and *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke), and the person claims that he does not believe, there is no opening to even begin a discussion with him. To this comes the command to "adults concerning the children" regarding *tumah* (ritual impurity), even though this has no basis in intellect and is merely a *gezeiras hakasuv* (a divine decree). As the Rambam writes (end of Hilchos Mikvaos), it is a clear and revealed matter that the laws of *tumah* and *taharah* are divine decrees and are not matters that human reason can determine; they are among the *chukim* (statutes), see there.

The command itself provides the strength to influence the one being educated and guided, similar to what is written regarding "If you walk in My statutes" (briefly in *Hayom Yom*, 6th of Cheshvan).

And how education can be effective in such matters can be explained according to what the Rambam writes in *Hilchos Geirushin*, end of chapter 2.

Signing with the blessing of immediate *teshuvah*, immediate *geulah*.

## 153

B"H, Thursday, 11 Iyar, 5704
Brooklyn

To the honorable Rabbi, renowned for his praise, a man of faith and many deeds, Rabbi Eliyahu Jung, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

As per your request, our volumes 1 and 2 have been sent to you in a special package, along with samples of the monthly publication we issue (in Yiddish and English).

Thank G-d, the aforementioned books have brought great benefit to several yeshivos ketanos, Talmud Torahs, and schools that utilize them. From time to time, the circle of those studying and reading them expands. We are confident that you, too, will use your significant influence to introduce and implement them in all the educational institutions under your leadership or that heed your guidance.

If in all countries the spirit prevailing in schools in general, and the purity of the books for study and reading for the youth in particular, have a profound influence on the entire life of the younger generation, then all the more so in this country, where the influence of parents on their children is minimal compared to other countries. Schools require excellent supervision, and the reading and study material provided to the students must be of pure holiness, in addition to being pedagogically appropriate for its purpose. If this is done, we may hope that the younger generation will be a generation of the upright, blessed in every detail, even if the conditions of the environment and the times are not favorable.

I have said previously, based on the teaching of our Sages (Yevamos 114a), that the obligation to "warn the adults regarding the children" is mentioned three times: regarding *shekatzim* (creeping things), blood, and *tumah* (ritual impurity).

There is a hint here regarding the field of education, as this is the essence of warning adults regarding children.

For regarding the nature of the one being educated and guided (who is "small" in knowledge relative to the educator and guide—the "adult" in knowledge), there are those who despair and feel it is impossible to exert influence on students for several reasons, which can be categorized into three types:

Type 1: They say: The current situation, especially in a certain place, is such that people’s nature is coarse and materialistic, and "man has no advantage over the beast." If so, how can matters of Torah and fear of Hashem enter their ears? To this comes the command that even regarding one who eats *shekatzim*—which indicates he is not within the category of a human at all, for the human soul finds *shekatzim* repulsive (Chullin 11a)—you are still commanded to educate and warn him.

Since our Sages have testified (Bamidbar Rabbah 19:1) that the Holy One, blessed be He, only makes demands according to the capacity of the one commanded, it must be that the educator has the power to act upon and influence even such a student.

Type 2: They say: This applies only to matters to which one has not been accustomed his entire life. But if one has already repeated an act once, twice, and three times, and has become entrenched in his conduct and actions, there is no hope that he will turn back. The refutation of this view is explained in the warning to adults regarding children concerning blood. As our Sages said (Sifrei) on the verse, "Only be strong not to eat the blood," you learn that they were addicted to eating blood; yet, even in these matters, education is necessary and effective.

Type 3: They say: This applies only to matters where it is possible to explain to the student through intellect, reason, and knowledge. But regarding matters that depend on the faith of the heart—which are *chukim* (statutes) without a revealed or understood reason—if the person claims he does not believe, there is no opening to engage with him. What is the use of effort in these matters? To this comes the command to adults regarding children concerning *tumah*, even though it has no basis in logic and is merely a decree of the King. As the Rambam writes (end of Hilchos Mikvaos), it is clear and manifest that the laws of *tumah* and *taharah* are decrees of the King and are not matters that human reason can determine; they are among the *chukim*.

How can education, advocacy, and effort be effective in such matters and regarding such individuals? This can be understood based on what the Rambam writes regarding every single Jew, without distinction. He states (Hilchos Geirushin, end of chapter 2): "One whose evil inclination has overpowered him to nullify a mitzvah or commit a sin... he has forced himself against his better judgment... for he desires to be of Israel, and he desires to perform all the mitzvos and distance himself from sins, but it is his inclination that has overpowered him," see there.

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 154

[Iyar 5704]

One of the purposes for the founding of this collection is to serve as a platform from which *anash* and all who join them may discuss any difficult matter in the study of Chassidus in general, and in the books published in recent times in particular, as stated in the editorial introduction to the first issue of the collection.

Naturally, only those questions and answers that are of interest to the entire readership will be printed in the collection.

Questions that are of interest only to the individual will be answered either by letter to the inquirer or in this section of the collection, but in brief.

***

At the opening of this section, I wish to answer a general question that I have heard many people ask when reading the *sichos* published by the "Otzar HaChassidim" editorial board, namely:

"A certain point in the *sicha* is not explained at all."

"The interpretation of the *pasuk* and the words of our Sages in the *sicha* is nothing more than a clever witticism (*tzachus*) and has no basis or source."

The answer to this is: Although the *sichos* (that are printed) are, in the vast majority, understandable even to those who have not studied the Chassidus of Chabad, they nevertheless contain several points—especially those that appear in the *sicha* only incidentally—which cannot be understood except after the prerequisite of studying the teachings of Chassidus, or at the very least, knowing the foundations of this system. In the *maamarim* of *d'chassidus* or in another *sicha*, this matter is certainly explained as needed, for "words of Torah are poor in one place and rich in another" (*Beraita d'Lamed-Beis Midos* 15; see also *Yerushalmi Rosh Hashanah* ch. 3, end of *halacha* 5).

Likewise, the interpretations of the Scriptures and the words of our Sages in the *sichos* are, for the most part, sourced in the words of our Sages, or are based on what is explained in the words of our Sages in both the revealed and the hidden Torah.

***

As an example, I bring here one of the "puzzling" points (in the opinion of the questioners) that was asked about in the *kuntres* "Bikur Chicago," which has just been published. I have chosen this question because the inquirers considered it a puzzle for which there is no room for an answer whatsoever. And as we shall see, even such a question arises only from the questioners' lack of knowledge—and furthermore, the "puzzling" *maamar* has its source explicitly in the words of our Sages—so from this puzzle, one may judge *a fortiori* regarding the other questions.

When the *sicha* was first published—in mimeograph, winter 5702—the critics were divided into two camps:

The camp of those who criticize for the sake of contention, happy to find a fault, declared: "This is what we have said all along—that the way and custom of the Chassidim is to say allusions and witticisms without any basis or source, and here is further proof. For even though the words 'Akavya' and 'Mahalalel' are a play on words for 'eikev' (heel) and 'hillel' (praise), is there any room in logic to say that what is stated in the *Mishnah*, 'Akavya ben Mahalalel says,' has any connection to 'the heel of a *davener* who praises the Almighty'?!..."

And the Chassidim were not satisfied with this, but added sharpness upon sharpness, for Akavya and Mahalalel are separate people, and the Chassidic interpretation is as if it were one person!

The second camp, those who wish to judge every person—"even the Chassidim"—favorably, said: "It is true that the statement of R' Boruch Mordechai is merely a witticism and has no basis—but it is explicitly stated in the *sicha* that it is merely a witticism. And if it is permitted for the best of the *maggidim* to say such interpretations, why should the Chassidim be any different? Especially since the idea involved in this statement is a lofty one: regarding the great value of the service of the heart and emotion, etc."

The saying of R' Boruch Mordechai aroused such puzzlement that even among *anash* there were those who asked: "Why publish in print matters that give the opponents room to contend: 'Look, the Chassidim have added *another* interpretation that logic cannot bear and which has no source in the words of our Sages'?"

***

Now, if we place before our eyes the character of the man who said the aforementioned saying and the occasion on which he said it—for Rabbi Boruch Mordechai was one of the great *geonim* of his day, and while still young—in the days of the *Gra*—he was already the head of a *mesivta* in the city of Vilna—and he said this witticism in response to his brother-in-law, a *gaon* in the revealed Torah who was an opponent of the Chassidic system and the son of an opponent of this system, and he said it in the presence of the city's *geonim* and scholars after hearing a deep *pilpul*—

There remains no room for doubt that R'B'M would not have answered his brother-in-law with a mere witticism and a moralistic remark without a strong basis in the words of our Sages—so strong that even his brother-in-law and all those gathered would be forced to admit it. Especially since in those days, the overwhelming majority of the *misnagdim* held to the system that *maggidim* and those who speak of *mussar* have no purpose except for women, the unlearned, and scholars when their heads are heavy and they are unable to engage in study. And in every matter, they would ask first and foremost: "From where are these words, and where is it alluded to in the Torah?"

***

Now, the system of *midrash sheimos*—to seek connections between the meaning of a name and the essence of the one called by that name—is a common phenomenon in the Written and Oral Torah.

And not only in general names—names of species—where the name indicates the nature of the species, as our Sages say (*Bereishis Rabbah* ch. 17): "He [the Holy One, Blessed be He] said to the angels: 'His [man's] wisdom is greater than yours.' He brought before them the beast, the animal, and the bird. He said to them: 'What is the name of this?' They did not know. He passed them before man. He said: 'What is the name of this?' He said: 'This is an ox, this is a donkey,' etc." And the Ramban explains (*Bereishis* 2:19) that the Holy One, Blessed be He, brought all the beasts of the field and all the birds of the sky before man, and he recognized their nature and called them names, meaning the name appropriate for them according to their nature (see also *Bachya* on this verse; *Shelah* in the introduction to *Beis HaMikdash* at the end; *Tanya*, *Shaar HaYichud VeHaEmunah* ch. 1, *d.h. Ar"i... lo avo biYerushalayim shel maalah...* of the year 5700—printed in *Kuntres* 41, and more). Rather, it is also so with private names, where they interpreted the meaning of the name according to the essence of the one called by that name. And the first is explicitly in the Scripture (*Bereishis* 3:20): "And he called... Chavah, because she was..."

More than this: We find that they interpret names even in a place where the giver of the name did not intend such an interpretation.

And this matter is written in the Torah (*Bereishis* 27:36): "Is he not rightly named Yaakov? For he has supplanted me..." and it is repeated in the Prophets (*I Shmuel* 25:25): "For as his name is, so is he; Nabal is his name..." And it is found frequently in the words of our Sages.

Beyond this: Even if you were to say that there is no proof from the interpretations of our Sages regarding names brought in the *Tanach*, because in those cases one could say the reason for the interpretation is that since the name was brought in the Torah, it certainly contains some allusion and lesson (and with this, the note of the *Maharsha* in *Chiddushei Aggados Yoma* 83b is answered).

Yet we find (*Yoma* ibid.) that Rabbi Meir would be precise about the names of people who lived in his day. And the actual event proved the words of Rabbi Meir.

—And the fact that Rabbi Meir concludes there, "I say this as a concern, but do I say it as a certainty?" is because the bearer of the name is not compelled in his actions, for the choice is in his hands; rather, his nature inclines toward this (similar to what is written in the *Rambam*, beginning of *Hilchos Deos*, and see also *Hilchos Teshuvah* ch. 5, *halacha* 4. And what is written there in *Lechem Mishneh*—it is explicit in the beginning of ch. 4 of the *Shemonah Perakim* of the Rambam). And it is possible that this is also the intention of the *Midrash* (*Bereishis Rabbah* ch. 71): "There are those whose names are ugly but their deeds are beautiful, etc.," to let us know that the name does not compel in a way—in the language of the *Rambam*—that it is impossible to deviate from it.

And we find something greater than this in the words of our Sages: that the name not only indicates the essence of the person but also causes it, as our Sages say (*Tanchuma*, end of *Haazinu*): "A person should always check names when naming his son... for sometimes the name causes good or causes evil." And in *Yoma* (38b): "See what became of him" (meaning that his name—after the name of a wicked person—caused his death; for one cannot say it was by way of punishment, for if so, the punishment would be too great, and furthermore, the punishment should have been upon the one who gave the name, not upon the bearer of the name). And it causes [effects] in the person bearing the name not only regarding what pertains to himself, but also to his descendants, as it is found in *Berachos* (7b): "What is [the meaning of] Ruth? Rabbi Yochanan said: That she merited and David came forth from her... From where do you know that a name causes...?"

Consequently, it is understood that there is room to interpret the names of the Patriarchs also regarding the essence of the children. And we find two ways of this in the words of our Sages: a) That the Patriarchs were named after the essence of the children, as mentioned above regarding Ruth, and as our Sages say (*Megillah* 12b): "Mordechai ben Yair ben Shimi ben Kish—it was taught: all of them were called after his [Mordechai's] name." b) That even if they were not named after the children, nevertheless, when the names of the Patriarchs are mentioned regarding the children, it comes to indicate the traits of the children, as our Sages say (*Sanhedrin* 109b): "Korach... ben Yitzhar, the son who heated up the whole world against him like noon (*tzohorayim*)... and let him also count 'ben Yaakov,' the son who made himself crooked (*akav) for Gehinom..."—from the fact that they ask simply, "And let him also count 'ben Yaakov'," it is proven that even though the Patriarchs were not named after the children, nevertheless, they interpret their names if they are mentioned regarding the children.

***

Thus far regarding the interpretation of names in general, which is the basis for this type of *derush* in the words of our Sages: to be precise about the name, and not only the name itself, but also the names of his ancestors if his father's name is mentioned with him (as with Akavya ben Mahalalel).

And in the case at hand, regarding the witticism of Rabbi Boruch Mordechai in particular—its source is explicit in the words of the *AriZal*.

We shall preface this with what is written in *Midrash Aggadas Bereishis* (Buber edition, introduction ch. 11), and these are its words: "And why was his [the son of Kenan's] name called Mahalalel? Because he was the first to return in *teshuvah* to praise (*lehalel*) and began to offer praise before the Holy One, Blessed be He."

And these are the words in the writings of the *AriZal* in *Shaar HaGilgulim* (Introduction 31): "Know that all the souls... are included and suspended in Adam HaRishon... every limb and limb of his 248 limbs... and the heel... is divided into more than 613 sparks of souls, and among them are the soul of... and the soul of Akavya ben Mahalalel."

And with more explanation there (end of Introduction 32), and these are its words: "Akavya ben Mahalalel... in the secret of the heel (*eikev*)... and therefore his name was called Akavya, and because this Akavya also rectified the aspect of Mahalalel ben Kenan, therefore he is now called Akavya ben Mahalalel."

—And it is worth noting from the *Mishnah* (*Eduyos* ch. 5, *mishnah* 6): "The *Azarah* is not locked before any person of Israel in wisdom—and some have the version 'in humility'—and in fear of sin, like Akavya ben Mahalalel." And see in *Baal HaTurim*, beginning of *Parshas Eikev*, *Shelah* there, and more—on the verse "The heel (*eikev*) of humility is the fear of Hashem"—that both, humility and fear, are called *eikev*.

***

The explanation of the foundation of this "puzzling" witticism can serve as an example and a sign for us, for certainly all the *maamarim* in the *sichos kodesh*, even those that seem puzzling in our eyes, have a proper interpretation and explanation. And regarding such things, our Sages said (*Yerushalmi Peah* ch. 1, *halacha* 1): "For it is not an empty thing from you"—and if it is empty, it is from you, because you do not toil in Torah.

And if the witticisms of the early Chassidim are such, then their Torah and their service are all the more so.

## 455

B"H, Wednesday, 9 Sivan, 5704
Brooklyn

To the distinguished avreich, G-d-fearing, wise, and understanding, etc.,
The Tamim, R' Avraham Shmuel, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your invitation to your wedding, may it be in a good and auspicious hour, I send my blessing: Mazal Tov, Mazal Tov. May you build a home in Israel upon the foundations and the inner essence of Torah and mitzvos.

It may be suggested that the general wedding of the Jewish people with Hashem took place on Shavuos, as our Sages of blessed memory state in Mechilta Yisro (cited by Rashi on the Torah, beginning of Parshas Beracha) that Hashem went out to meet them like a groom, etc.

Now, we rule that the Torah was given on the sixth of Sivan, as our Rebbe writes in his Shulchan Aruch, beginning of section 494 (and this is also implied by our custom regarding the Torah reading, based on what is written in Rashi, s.v. *v'haidna*, Megillah 31a). The seven days of feasting extend until the twelfth of Sivan, inclusive. If so, this can be connected to the fact that the *tashlumin* (make-up days) for Atzeres extend until the twelfth, inclusive (Chagigah 17a), and with what our Sages of blessed memory say (Yoma 4b) that according to the opinion that the Torah was given on the sixth, the days until the twelfth, inclusive, were set apart—for separation—from the days that followed.

In any event, according to all opinions, these days are among the seven days of feasting of the Groom, the King of Kings, Hashem.

May it be His will that,

In accordance with our Sages of blessed memory (Pirkei D'Rabbi Eliezer, ch. 16), just as the King’s face shines like the light of the sun (as written in *Ohr Pnei Melech Chaim*, Radal), so too the groom’s face shines like the light of the sun—

May Hashem shine His face upon you, so that all the blessings with which my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita, has blessed you may be fulfilled.

With blessings of Mazal Tov, and for immediate teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

#### ***455***

From the secretariat's copy.

**R' Avraham Shmuel:** Stein. Another letter to him—above, 142.

**To your wedding:** On the 9th of Sivan. See Kovetz Lubavitch, issue 3, p. 47.

## 456

22 Sivan, 5704

To the honorable Rav, the chossid, G-d-fearing, and accomplished scholar,
Teacher of law to Yaakov, Moreinu HaRav R’ A. HaKohen, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

Under separate cover, I am returning to you the sefer *Ve’aseh Tov*, and I thank you for the question.

I found therein some highly perplexing statements regarding the *shita* of [Chassidus], and my bewilderment grew, especially since experience has shown over the generations that have passed since then that this *shita* has raised many thousands and tens of thousands of G-d-fearing individuals, etc.

However, my mind was somewhat put at ease when I saw there (page 12, bottom of column 2) that he also disputes earlier Mekubalim.

Since it has come to my attention, I have seen fit to note a few points regarding the contents of the sefer:

1) He writes there (12, top of column 3): "Both (Zachor and Shamor) are one mitzvah from the 613." (Perhaps his source is the language of the *Sefer Mitzvos Gadol*, end of Positive Mitzvah 29, which writes: "It is stated in the first set of Commandments 'Zachor' and in the last 'Shamor,' and they are one.") This requires study, for: a) Zachor is a positive mitzvah and Shamor is a negative mitzvah (see Ramban on the Torah, Shemos 20:5), so how is it possible for them to be one mitzvah in the count of 613? b) Zachor is the positive mitzvah of sanctifying the day through speech or over wine (Positive Mitzvah 155 in the Rambam’s *Sefer HaMitzvos*), while Shamor is the prohibition against performing work (Rashi, *Berachos* 20b, s.v. *b’shmirah*), except that it is not counted separately because the prohibition "do not do any work" (Negative Mitzvah 320) is already counted.

2) He further writes there: "We have proven that the opinion of the Zohar is to recite two blessings (on Tefillin)." This requires study: a) In *Tikkunei Zohar* near the end (Vilna 1867 edition, page 46b), it is implied that he holds this is a dispute between the Bavli and the Yerushalmi, and the Bavli holds that one only recites one blessing (as per Rashi). He concludes there: "And from the side of the middle pillar, one only needs one blessing." See the Gra’s commentary on *Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim* 25. b) Even though in *Shaar HaKavanos*, *Drushei Tefillas HaShachar*, Drush 2 and 3, he brings both opinions and explains them according to Kabbalah (mentioned in the *maamar* beginning *Vayomer D’s Eilav Mi Sam Peh* in *Torah Or* by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe), nevertheless, in *Pri Etz Chayim*, *Shaar HaTefillin*, Chapter 1, it states: "He shall not recite a blessing over the Shel Rosh unless he has interrupted with speech." It is difficult to say he would have written this if the opinion of the Zohar were the opposite. See *Minchas Chinuch*, Mitzvah 423, and *Shaar HaKollel* (by R’ A.D. Lavut, *Shaar* 2, subsection 6).

3) He also writes on the following page (page 2, bottom of column 3): "I have seen someone (in the *Shaar Krias Shema* section, s.v. *Lehavin Parsha Rishona*) write in the name of R’ R’ M.D. (may his soul rest in Eden) a resolution to this question (why the blessing was not recited beforehand): that time itself was also created, and initially there was no time." In truth, I do not believe this came from the holy one, etc. And what will the Rav do with the *Midrash Bereishis Rabbah* (beginning of Chapter 9), which says it teaches that a sequence of times existed beforehand? And in the Gemara they said, "The Torah preceded the world by two thousand years," etc. He is alluding to the *Moreh Nevuchim*—specifically Part 2, Chapter 30.

— See *Shomer Emunim*, Second Debate, sections 17–27 —

Now, aside from the opinion of the Rambam (there) that time was created, many of the great figures and Geonim of Israel have stated this explicitly: Rav Saadia Gaon (*Emunos V’Deios*, Treatise 1, s.v. *v’hara’ayah ha-revi’it*), the Rashba (Responsa 418), Ramah of Pano (*Asarah Maamaros*, Part 1, Chapter 17), Sforno at the beginning of his commentary on the Torah, and others.

At a deeper level, one could say: There are two aspects to time: time that is measured and quantified (by some motion), and the essence of the passage of time (which is called in the language of the Jewish philosophers in *Moreh Nevuchim*, Part 2, Chapter 13—"a measure of time," "a form of time"—or in the language of *Sefer HaIkkarim*, Chapter 2, 18—"unmeasured time"). Even the essence of the passage of time is created (contrary to the opinion of the *Ikkarim* there). See the *Sefer HaMitzvos* of the Tzemach Tzedek, *Mitzvas Emunas Elokus*, section 11. This is also the implication in *Sefer Asarah Maamaros* (there).

As for the *Chazal* in *Bereishis Rabbah* mentioned above, so that it not lean toward the opinion of [the eternity of the world], they have already resolved it in various ways; see the *Ikkarim* there, *Rabbeinu Bachya* at the beginning of *Parshas Bereishis*, and others.

According to *Chassidus*, there is a distinction between "time" and the "sequence of times" mentioned in the aforementioned *Bereishis Rabbah*. The "sequence of times" is more subtle than the concept of "time" (the opposite of the explanation of the author of the *Ikkarim*), and it is the root of time (as implied in *Pardes Rimonim*, *Shaar Erech HaZemanim*, page *Seder Zemanim*, and in *Sefer Avodas HaKodesh*, Part 4, Chapter 5), as explained at length in the *Sefer HaMitzvos* (there) and elsewhere.

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah—immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

Regarding what you noted from *Bechoros* (50a), "He sent it," as proof for *Pidyon HaBen* via a *shaliach*, contrary to the Rema (*Shulchan Aruch Yoreh Deah* 305, end of section 1): It can be explained simply that he only sent the money via a *shaliach*, but he performed the *Pidyon HaBen* himself. See the *Chiddushei R’ Akiva Eiger* there.

## 157

B"H, Thursday, 24 Sivan, 5704

To the honorable, G-d-fearing, distinguished and elevated, one who conducts his business with integrity, R' Yehuda Katz, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

Your letter from before Shavuos was received in a timely manner. I have not replied until now because I have been occupied with preparing the *Kuntres Chicago* and *Kovetz Lubavitch* number two for print, and so on. I ask for your forgiveness.

The little book "Shavuos" had great success, and this gives us encouragement to continue the series of such booklets.

I thank you on behalf of *Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch* for taking a large part in their distribution.

Three years ago, when my father-in-law, the Rebbe shlita, set out to establish the *Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch*, many people said: In America, such a thing cannot have a future. America is different; many things that are forbidden elsewhere must be done in America, and in America, it is permitted.

However, the work has shown us that in America there can—and must—be *chinuch al taharas hakodesh*, textbooks and reading brochures completely free of coercion, groups of young children born here in this country who advocate for *Shabbos* and *Yiddishkeit*, and so on.

However, we must have helpers, because the work is very great and very important.

And therefore, every friend who joins us is so precious to us, and we hope that he will bring his friends along with him as well.

In this week’s *parsha*, the story of the *meraglim* (spies) is told. The *meraglim* were leaders of the Children of Israel, and at that time they were righteous. They also began by saying: "It flows with milk and honey," and "However, the people who dwell in the land are fierce... Amalek dwells in the land of the Negev," and so on—all of which was true. But their conclusion—"We cannot ascend to the people, for they are stronger than we"—was false. The *Gemara* explains (*Sotah* 35a) what this means: *kiviyachol*, "even the householder cannot remove his vessels"—this is *kefirah* (heresy).

In this same vein, people argue that in America it "flows with milk and honey," that one has all physical *taanugim* and is occupied with them, and that one is surrounded by *Amalekim*. So how can one even speak to another person who is far from *Yiddishkeit* about adopting a strict life of *Torah* and *mitzvos*? "This is not the place for that." This, G-d forbid, is the opposite of *emunah*. One only needs to have the resolve within oneself, as Calev said: "We shall surely ascend"—and then, automatically, "we will be able to do it."

With blessings for *l'alter l'teshuvah, l'alter l'geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 158

B"H, Tuesday, 29 Sivan, 5704
Brooklyn

To the outstanding avreich, G-d-fearing and wise, etc.,
The Tamim, Moreinu HaRav Yitzchak HaKohen, may he live.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your invitation to your wedding, may it be in a good and auspicious hour, I send my blessing of Mazal Tov. May you build a home in Israel upon the foundations and the inner dimension of Torah and mitzvos.

It may be suggested that, as is well known from our Sages (Eruvin 54a), "This world, which we are departing from, is like a wedding celebration." It is explained in Chassidic thought (Ohr HaTorah – by the Tzemach Tzedek – at the end of the discourse beginning "Vayomer Yehonasan," 24b) that the descent of the soul into a body in this world is, by way of example, like a wedding. Through this, a level of Ein Sof is created. Likewise, the soul in this world becomes a level of "one who is walking," meaning we are departing from it—this is the level of "without limit," *bechol me'odecha* (with all your might).

There are various types of this "wedding"—that is, the avodah of the Jewish person in this world—which can be broadly divided into: 1) Their avodah as it relates to themselves: those who serve Hashem with their bodies and souls. 2) Their avodah within the world: those who dwell in the tents of Torah and those who are involved in worldly affairs.

It may be suggested that this is hinted at by our Sages (end of Kiddushin): "The world cannot exist without a perfumer (one who deals in spices; a scent, something the soul derives pleasure from – Berachos 43b) and without a tanner (dealing with the skins of the body and the animal soul)."

Our Sages conclude (Kiddushin, ibid.), "Happy is he whose craft is that of a perfumer." In the context of the Jewish people as a whole, this refers to the tribe of Levi, who (in the language of the Rambam, end of Hilchos Shemittah V'Yovel) "were set apart to serve Hashem, to minister unto Him, and to teach His straight and righteous ways to the masses... therefore they were set apart from the ways of the world... rather, they are the army of Hashem."

And how much more so for a Kohen, who (in the language of the Rambam, ibid.) "his spirit moved him and his knowledge enlightened him to set himself apart to stand before Hashem, to minister unto Him, and to serve Him... he is sanctified as holy of holies, and Hashem will be his portion... and He will provide him in this world with that which is sufficient for him."

With blessings of Mazal Tov, for immediate teshuvah, for immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

## 159

By the Grace of G-d
Thursday, 1 Tammuz, 5704
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the respected and distinguished Mr. Tzvi Palmer
New Jersey

Greetings and Blessings,

Your brother, the highly esteemed Mr. Shlomo Palmer, spoke to me about you, and it is for this reason that I am writing this letter.

First, a few lines regarding the objective of the organization "Machne Israel."

My father-in-law, the Lubavitcher Rebbe, Rabbi Yosef Yitzchak Schneersohn, shlita, founded "Machne Israel" with the purpose—among others—of making known and publicizing to Jews of all backgrounds the true meaning and high value of Yiddishkeit, and also to provide answers to the various questions that may arise regarding Judaism, Torah, and mitzvos.

Since your brother has informed me that you have an interest in these matters, this explains my writing to you: to say that we are prepared to remain in contact and, to the best of our knowledge, to clarify various details connected to the aforementioned questions.

* * *

One of the fundamental concepts of Yiddishkeit is unity—not only faith in one G-d and one Torah, but also unity within every person in their inner life.

In contrast to philosophies that suggest one should indulge the body without regard for the neshamah, and even in contrast to the philosophy that suggests one must constantly torment and afflict the body so that the neshamah might be entirely bound through the body, the philosophy of Torah states that although the neshamah must lead the person, its task is not to torment the body. Rather, it is to ensure that the body conducts itself in a healthy manner, according to the guidance of the Torah, which is called *Toras Chaim* (the Torah of Life).

Unity in the life of the neshamah, in particular: there are three opinions regarding the guiding principle of human life:

a) One school of thought holds that one should be guided exclusively by cold intellect and logic.

b) A second school of thought says that one should rely only on the heart and warm emotion.

c) A third group says that what one thinks or feels in the heart is not so relevant; the main thing is that in action—in deed—one should conduct oneself as one ought.

The Torah states and rules that a person must be a *shalem* (a whole being); one must strive to be perfect. And one can only be an *Adam HaShalem* (a complete person) if one ensures that the head, the heart, and the hand (a symbol of the body) are all in unity and harmony in their conduct, according to the instructions of the Ribono shel Olam.

This guiding principle is so important that our Sages explain that this is one of the concepts behind the mitzvah of tefillin: when a Jew puts on tefillin, he should remember that the hand (*tefillah shel yad*), the heart (opposite which one must place the *tefillah shel yad*), and the head (*tefillah shel rosh*) must all be in agreement with one another and live a Jewish, Torah-true life.

Deeper still: the fulfillment of the mitzvah of tefillin—the act itself of laying tefillin—will strengthen the Jew and tremendously increase his neshamah-powers to carry out in his life the harmony of hand, heart, and head in the way of Torah.

Although we do not understand how the act of *hanachas tefillin* can affect the powers of the neshamah, it is just as a child does not grasp the connection between the bread he eats and the fact that the neshamah sustains his body.

The importance of the mitzvah of tefillin can be seen from various stories and explanations of our Sages; I will bring only a few here:

a) (*Kiddushin 35a*) "The entire Torah is compared to tefillin"—the entire Torah with all its mitzvos is likened to—and various details of them can be learned from—the mitzvah of tefillin.

b) Although one can fulfill the mitzvah of tefillin by laying them once during the day, and only in shul or at home, in the past—when people were purer and more attached to spiritual matters—they would wear tefillin all day long, whether in shul, at home, or in the street.

The extent to which everyone held and guarded this mitzvah can be seen from the fact that the Gemara (*Shabbos 130a*) complains, recounting the attitude toward tefillin at a certain time.

There was a decree at that time that anyone who wore tefillin would be killed.

One might think that they stopped laying tefillin? No, the Gemara has no complaint about that; they continued to lay tefillin.

Does it mean that they were satisfied with the obligation of the mitzvah of tefillin alone, by laying them once a day, which fulfills the mitzvah? No, the Gemara does not say that either; they wore tefillin in the street as well, without regard for the danger to their lives. That is how important and precious tefillin were to Jews, even at that time.

However, since only individuals did the latter—as, for example, Elisha Baal Knafayim—the Gemara considers this insufficient *mesiras nefesh* for what the mitzvah of tefillin deserves.

Tosafos explains even further that there was no complaint about that either. So in what way was it expressed that the mitzvah of tefillin was not as strong among Jews as it should have been, and why does the Gemara complain and wonder about it?

The Gemara brings an example: during the time of the decree, Elisha was walking in the street, and naturally, he was wearing tefillin. An official saw him, chased after him, and asked what he was carrying. Elisha replied: "The wings of a dove."

The fact that he did not have the strength to say that they were tefillin (even though that would have meant certain death for him) proves that the attitude toward tefillin was not as fervent as the mitzvah of tefillin deserves and requires.

* * *

From all mitzvos we can learn a great deal, and all mitzvos strengthen a person in his neshamah-powers (even though we cannot always grasp this, as mentioned above).

Especially the mitzvos that we were commanded to perform daily, such as tefillin (the obligation for which is every day, except for Shabbos and Yom Tov), which are called an *os*—a sign that the Jews and the Ribono shel Olam are one.

* * *

I will be pleased to hear from you, and please do not hesitate to turn to us if you think we can be of any help, as mentioned at the beginning of the letter.

With best wishes for immediate teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 160

160

B"H, Thursday, 1 Tammuz, 5704
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the honorable Rabbi, the Gaon, the G-d-fearing, the pious, etc.,
Rabbi A. A. Axelrad, may he live

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter:

a) Regarding what you wrote in reconciling the matter of what is explained in Chassidic teachings concerning the necessity of a "one-eighth of an eighth" (at the beginning of one's avodah), even though the halachic ruling (Rambam, Hilchos De'os, ch. 2, halacha 3; and Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, ch. 156, end of subsection 1, and ch. 157, subsection 3) is "neither this nor a part of this"—for one refers to a Torah scholar, and the other refers to all other people.

See the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, Avos, ch. 4, on the Mishnah "Be exceedingly humble of spirit," where the Rambam writes explicitly that the opinion of "neither this nor a part of this" disagrees with the opinion that a Torah scholar must possess "one-eighth of an eighth." This is also evident in the Gemara (Sotah 5a), as it brings all the opinions on this one after the other (though the halachic ruling is a matter of study, as it says "Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak said," and not "Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak says," according to the rule that in a dispute, the final opinion is the one cited last).

See *Kitzur Klalei HaTalmud*, *Klalei HaTalmud* by the author of *Knesses HaGedolah*, subsection 111—printed in the Romm Shas after Maseches Berachos—the *Shelah*, beginning of the section on the Oral Torah, *Tosafos Yom Tov* on *Bikkurim*, ch. 3, mishnah 6, and elsewhere. This is also implied in the Shulchan Aruch of our Rebbe, as he concludes there regarding the methods of imposing awe upon the people and the conduct of a Torah scholar who has been insulted.

b) Regarding what you wrote that there is no benefit in expressing your opinion in the city concerning the necessity of studying holy texts specifically, because they will not listen to this as they are "free" [secular/unaffiliated]—you can emphasize in your speech that these texts are also appropriate and suitable from the perspective of a pedagogue and the like. It is known from our Sages (Shabbos 55a), "If it is revealed before You, is it revealed before them? Who knows?" It is possible that your outcry will be effective.

c) Regarding a book of Jewish history in Lashon HaKodesh and holy language—the only one I know of is *Divrei HaYamim LeBnei Yisrael* by the Yaavetz.

d) The Beis Rivkah High School—for the time being, it is only for holy studies; regarding the future, there is no decision yet.

With the blessing of "Immediately to Teshuvah, immediately to Geulah,"

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

B"H, Tuesday, 27 Tammuz, 5704
