Rabbi A. A. [shlita],

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of Wednesday, Parshas Chukas:

1) Regarding what you wrote—that Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak, who holds "not from it and not from a part of it," does not disagree with the one who says a Torah scholar must possess eight praiseworthy traits—and that you wish to say this is the opinion of the Rambam:

Please see the Hagahos Maimoniyos, Hilchos De'os, Chapter 2, Halacha 3, where he writes explicitly that Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak disagrees with all of them.

Similarly, in his Commentary on the Mishnah (Avos, Chapter 4, Mishnah 4), he writes that the one who says "may he be under a ban" refers to one who lacks the eight praiseworthy traits. It must be that the Rambam holds that this also refers to a Torah scholar (as is the simple reading of Rashi), and it is regarding this that Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak disagrees.

2) Regarding what I noted concerning the Tosafos Yom Tov and the Shelah—this was in reference to the distinction between the phrasing "Rabbi So-and-so says" and "So-and-so says." I have now seen it noted that this is discussed in the Sdei Chemed, Kuntres HaKlalim, Letter Alef, subsection 365, but I do not have it with me to examine.

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee

#### 161

From the Secretariat copy.

**Rabbi A. A.:** Axelrod. Further letters to him appear above, 26, and in the references noted there. This is in continuation of letters 149 and 160 above.

162

[Before Menachem Av, 5704]

In the sefer *Emes L’Yaakov*—on the Aggados of the Shas—by the Rav of Lissa, regarding the statement of our Sages (Yoma 86a), "Great is teshuvah, for it reaches up to the Kisei HaKavod," and Rabbi Yochanan says, "Up to, but not including" (this is the version in *Ein Yaakov* and *Yalkut*), he writes: A single individual who has sinned, even if he subsequently performs teshuvah out of the greatest love—like the teshuvah of King David—is nevertheless considered a "blemished one" who has been healed, and it is no longer possible for the Shechinah to rest upon him such that he would be called the Kisei HaKavod. Only for the tzibbur (the community) does teshuvah avail for this, and the proof is that Yisrael are called the "Heichal of Hashem" even though they worshipped idols several times. This is the essence of the words of the Rav of Lissa regarding our subject.

Since the author of the *Chavas Daas* is one of the greatest of the later poskim, these words are heart-rending. I have seen in the *Chavas Daas* on Shabbos (56a): "Anyone who says David sinned is merely mistaken, for it is said... and Hashem was with him... is it possible that a sin came to his hand while the Shechinah was with him?" One may counter that the implication of the Shas is from the plain meaning of the verse, that Hashem was always with him, and therefore there was not a single moment in which he sinned. Especially since our Rabbeinu HaZaken writes in *Tanya*, Chapter 46, that the Patriarchs were in the category of a *merkavah*, and so it is with every soul of Yisrael at the time of engaging in Torah and mitzvos—and he does not distinguish between a baal teshuvah and a complete tzaddik. And there, in Chapter 47, it is proven that this matter applies to every person. How, then, can these two opinions be reconciled?

Rabbi A. A. HaKohen Yalles, Philadelphia

***

Response: To understand the view of Admur HaZaken on this, one must first explain the difference between the category of the Patriarchs—who are the *merkavah* (Bereishis Rabbah, Ch. 47)—and the category of any other person engaged in Torah and mitzvos, who are certainly not equal.

The distinction is explained in the language of the *Tanya*, where he writes in Chapter 23: "The Patriarchs were the *merkavah*, for all their limbs were holy and separated from the matters of this world... the Patriarchs were the *merkavah*, for all their days they never ceased, even for one hour, from binding their consciousness..." And in Chapter 38: "For service in the category of a *merkavah* means to be nullified and included in His light, may He be blessed, they and all that they possess, through the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, in the manner that they said, 'The Patriarchs were the *merkavah*,' and this is because this was their service all their days." End quote.

Thus, the greatness of the Patriarchs in this matter consists of two things: 1) Their service is for all their days without interruption. 2) Their service is with all their limbs and all that they possess. It is specifically through the fulfillment of these two conditions that our Sages said they were the *merkavah*.

Yet, in *Tanya*, Chapters 23 and 46, he writes that every soul of Yisrael can be in the category of a *merkavah*—and this is by virtue of the specific soul-power that is enclothed in the performance of this specific mitzvah (see there, Chapter 37), and specifically at the time one is performing the mitzvah.

Therefore, this is not a *merkavah* in the absolute sense, since it is only: 1) A specific power, 2) It passes and vanishes afterward. As he writes in Chapter 34: "Therefore... he should bring to his heart that from the smallness of my intellect and the root of my soul, it is impossible to be a *merkavah*... in the absolute sense... I will make for Him a dwelling place... through Torah study according to my free time... through giving tzedakah..." It is explicit here that even though he studies Torah and gives tzedakah, he is still not in the category of a *merkavah* in the absolute sense, and this is as stated above.

However, since every person can "turn from evil and do good" by fulfilling Torah and mitzvos in thought, speech, and action with all that he has, at all times and every hour (*Tanya*, Chapter 14), it follows that even at a time when he is not engaged in Torah and mitzvos (in a way that there is no prohibition, of course), he still has the power even then to return to awaken and cleave his soul to Hashem through Torah and mitzvos. Thus, it can be said that regarding his level, this too is called the category of a *merkavah*—similar to what is said in *Tanya* at the end of Chapter 13 regarding love. With this, it is simply understood what is said in Chapter 23, that every soul of Yisrael can be in the category of a *merkavah*, and how this does not contradict what is said in Chapter 34.

Furthermore, we learn from what is said in Chapter 23 that "every soul..."—it is obvious that this is not meant to exclude a person who committed a sin once in his entire lifetime, even if he performed teshuvah afterward, for if so, "you have left nothing..." By necessity, we must say that this baal teshuvah is also included in the category of "every soul."

And even though in many places Rabbeinu HaZaken emphasizes that the category of *merkavah* requires one to be so "all his days without interruption, even for one hour," as mentioned above—and even if we say, as above, that since at this moment he is a *merkavah* in actuality, it does not contradict what will be later, provided that at every hour he can awaken to be in the category of a *merkavah* (which exists in potential)—nevertheless, for one who has committed a sin, at the time of committing the sin he could not awaken, since a "spirit of folly" entered him. On the contrary, at that time he was a *merkavah* for the absolute evil of the three impure *kelipos*. And even if he performed teshuvah afterward, he still interrupted for one hour, which is the hour of committing the sin.

To ensure that the words of the *Tanya* do not contradict one another, one can say his intent—based on the rule (Rosh Hashanah 16b) that a person is judged only according to his deeds at that moment—is that if at this hour his state and the path of his soul is such that he will not interrupt, even for one hour, from being a *merkavah* (in actuality, or at least in potential), and there remains no impression at all from the sins he committed in the past, for he has already performed the proper teshuvah—then this person has the name *merkavah* upon him now.

And what is said in *Tanya* that the Patriarchs "all their days did not cease..." describes the holiness of the Patriarchs and their greatness, that all their days they were in the category of a *merkavah*. But it does not compel us to say that a person who interrupted for one hour has lost his hope forever.

In this same way, one must interpret the *Tanya* where it says (Chapter 12) that a *beinoni* is one who has never committed a sin in his life and the name "rasha" has never been called upon him, not even for one hour or one moment in his life. Yet in Chapter 14, he writes that every person can be a *beinoni* at any time and every hour. To avoid a contradiction from one chapter to the next, it must be that, as above, the intent is that from the perspective of his current state, he has not committed (and no impression remains) a sin, and will not commit one, etc. (and not as Rav Cohen wrote in *HaTamim*, issue 8, regarding this).

Based on the above, the principle that our Sages included (Rambam, *Hilchos Teshuvah*, end of Ch. 3; see also Yerushalmi, *Pe'ah*, Ch. 1, end of Halacha 1) is also upheld: "There is nothing that stands before teshuvah."

***

In all the above, there is no contradiction to what is said in *Emes L’Yaakov* that one who has sinned, even once, even if he performed the proper teshuvah, it is still impossible for the Shechinah to rest and for the name "Kisei HaKavod" to be upon him.

For the aforementioned sefer interprets this according to the statement of Rabbi Yochanan, who said "up to, but not including," and Rabbi Yochanan follows his own view (Berachos 34b) that complete tzaddikim are greater than baalei teshuvah. But we hold that baalei teshuvah are greater—as the Rambam writes (*Teshuvah*, Ch. 7, Halacha 4) and in *Tanya*, Chapter 7; see also *Iggeres HaTeshuvah*, end of Chapter 8.

See also in Yoma (ibid.) where Rami bar Chama holds that through teshuvah out of love, his name is not called upon him and the sin is uprooted from its inception; if so, he also disagrees with Rabbi Yochanan. And one cannot say—according to Rami bar Chama—that these words apply only to the tzibbur, and in this Rabbi Yochanan also agrees. For while regarding the resting of the Shechinah and being called the Kisei HaKavod there is an advantage to the tzibbur, as it is written, "He who dwells with them in the midst of their impurities, even at a time when they are impure" (Yoma 56a), the uprooting of the sin depends on the intensity and the greatness of the matter of teshuvah. If so, what difference is there between the tzibbur and the individual?

***

Service in the category of *merkavah* is explained in many places in *Chassidus*. For example, besides the above: *Torah Or*, the *maamar* beginning "HaAvos Heim HaMerkavah"; the *maamar* "VaYatzeg Es HaMaklos" of the year 5659; the *maamar* "Kol Machlokes" of the year 5671; the *maamar* "Atah Hu" of the year 5700, and more.

163

In the *Shelah*, at the beginning of Maseches Shavuos, in the discourse entitled *Chayav Adam Lismoach*, he cites from the sefer *Tolaas Yaakov*, which states: "On this day (Shavuos), the korban comes as *chametz* and *matzah*; the *chametz* corresponds to the body, and the *matzah* corresponds to the soul. And since the *chametz* and the *matzah* are offered together upon the Altar, so too will the soul and the body delight together in the pleasure of the World to Come (that which is after the Resurrection), which is the radiance of the *Shechinah*, as we find regarding Moshe and Eliyahu." This is based on what is written in the *Zohar* (Vol. II, 183b): "On the day of Shavuos... they offer *chametz* to be burned upon the Altar, and they offer two other loaves together, and the *chametz* is burned between them in the fire of the Altar, and it cannot rule over or harm the Jewish people, etc."

This is puzzling, for the *Shtei HaLechem* (Two Loaves) of Shavuos are eaten by the *Kohanim* and are not offered upon the Altar, as stated in the Mishnah in *Menachos* (74b). Furthermore, one who offers them upon the Altar violates a negative commandment (*Menachos* 57b). This requires explanation.

Rabbi Yeshaya HaLevi Horowitz, Winnipeg, Canada.

***

Response: According to the aforementioned version of the *Zohar*, one must add another difficulty to the one already raised:

The language of the *Zohar* implies that in addition to the *chametz*, they offer two other loaves, and the *chametz* is burned with them—at the very time that the *Shtei HaLechem* are the *chametz* offered on Shavuos, and no other *chametz* is offered then.

The early authorities have already addressed this passage in the *Zohar*. See *Shaar HaGilgulim* at the end (cited in the glosses printed on the margin of the *Zohar*), *Mikdash Melech*, *Or HaChama*, *Matzpes Sefarim*, the glosses of the *Chida* on the margin of the *Zohar*, and others.

In *Mikdash Melech*, he resolves that the *Zohar’s* statement that "the *chametz* is burned upon the Altar" refers to the *Yetzer Hara*, which is called *chametz*, and through the waving of the *Shtei HaLechem*, etc., it is burned, etc. Thus, the statement "and they offer two other loaves, etc." implies that the *chametz* being burned, etc., does not refer to the *Shtei HaLechem*. However, the *Chida* did not find this explanation satisfactory and wrote regarding it: "In *Mikdash Melech*, he sat and expounded allegories." (Seemingly, according to the *Mikdash Melech*, the word "other" is difficult, since the *chametz* mentioned before it is not bread, but the *Yetzer Hara*.)

In *Shaar HaGilgulim*, he cites a different version of the *Zohar* that contains neither the word "other" nor the word "between them." Thus, the second difficulty is removed. As for the first difficulty, he attempts to explain there that it refers to *Shtei HaLechem* that come on their own—for instance, if the sheep were lost—in which case they are burned, as mentioned in the Rambam, *Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin*, Chapter 8. However, he rejects this resolution, as it is forced to interpret the *Zohar* as referring to a case where the sheep were lost, since the verse itself speaks of the standard case—and it remains in need of study.

(Seemingly, even without this, one cannot say that the *Zohar* refers to the *Shtei HaLechem* being burned, for the burning of these *Shtei HaLechem* takes place in the *Beis HaSereifah*, while the *Zohar* states that it is burned upon the Altar. It may be said that the *Shaar HaGilgulim* provides a better resolution than this.)

***

In my humble opinion, it is possible to explain the language of the *Zohar* that "it is burned upon the Altar" as being exactly along the lines of what is stated in *Menachos* (57b): that the *Shtei HaLechem* have a portion that goes to the *Eshim* (the fires of the Altar), to the extent that they are subject to the prohibition of "You shall not offer up" (burning leaven on the Altar).

Even though the *Shtei HaLechem* themselves are not offered upon the Altar, as mentioned above, the fats of the sheep of *Atzeres* (Shavuos) that come with them are considered like the body of the *Shtei HaLechem* themselves, as Rashi explains there. Therefore, the *Shtei HaLechem* also have the status of "leftovers" (that are subject to the laws of *notar*).

Thus, Shavuos differs from the rest of the year: throughout the year, all *minchos* (meal offerings) come as *matzah*, and the Torah forbids leavening the leftovers of the *minchos* (*Menachos* 55, Mishnah 1-2). This is not the case on the festival of Shavuos, where the *Shtei HaLechem* are specifically baked as *chametz*, and the fats of the sheep—which are considered like the body of the leavened *Shtei HaLechem*—are offered upon the Altar.

One should not raise the objection that if so, what is the novelty of Shavuos, as throughout the year there are also the loaves of the *Todah* (thanksgiving offering) which come as *chametz*.

This is not a difficulty, for the loaves of the *Todah* are different, as there is no *mincha* sacrifice associated with them, unlike the *Shtei HaLechem* (*Menachos* 46b and *Tosafos* s.v. *Kol*, 52b). Furthermore—and this is the main point—the sheep that come with the *Shtei HaLechem* are considered like their body, as mentioned above, and we also say that the sheep come only because of the bread (*Horayos* 13a).

(Regarding Rashi’s language there, "sheep because of the *Shtei HaLechem*," one should note from *Menachos* (15a) that even if the term is the same, the intent is different.)

Especially according to the view of Rabbi Akiva (*Menachos* 45b)—and this is how the Rambam rules (*Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin*, Ch. 8, Halacha 15)—that the bread is a prerequisite for the sheep (and see *Minchas Chinuch*, Mitzvah 307, who explains that the passage in *Horayos* necessitates the view of Rabbi Akiva).

Therefore, when the fats of the sheep are burned, it is as if a portion of the *Shtei HaLechem* were burned upon the Altar, as mentioned above, that the *Shtei HaLechem* are considered to have a portion that goes to the *Eshim*—a status that does not exist in the *Todah*.

One should also note from *Menachos* (15a) regarding the *Todah*, etc., that they were not dependent on each other (the *Todah* and its loaves) regarding the waving, but the sheep are dependent on each other (with the *Shtei HaLechem*), etc.; and from *Tosafos* s.v. *Lo* (*Menachos* 78b), that the waving of the *Shtei HaLechem* is akin to its offering, whereas here (in the *Todah* and its loaves), it comes merely for the sanctification of the bread, etc.

***

With this distinction between the *Shtei HaLechem* and the loaves of the *Todah*, we can also understand the words of the Ramban in his commentary on the Torah (Vayikra 7:14), where he writes: "I am puzzled: when the verse excluded leaven from being offered as a *reishis* (first-fruit) offering, why did it not also say 'and a *Todah* offering'? But this is not a question, for the verse says, 'And upon the Altar they shall not ascend for a pleasing aroma,' and the loaves of the *Todah* have no portion that goes to the Altar, etc. And even though it says regarding the *Lechem HaPanim* that one violates a prohibition by leavening it, that is because the frankincense upon it is for the bread as a memorial, an offering made by fire to Hashem; but the loaves of the *Todah* have no portion that goes to the Altar." Seemingly, this is exactly what is written in the aforementioned *Zohar*. The *Tur* on the Torah already challenged the Ramban, arguing that the *Shtei HaLechem* also have no portion that goes to the Altar, and yet both the *Shtei HaLechem* and the loaves of the *Todah* have a sacrifice that comes with them; what is the difference between them?

(In the sefer *Ittur Sefarim* on the sefer *Matzpes Sefarim*, he suggests that the *Zohar* and the Ramban hold that the *Shtei HaLechem* are offered upon the Altar, which is puzzling, as is understood.)

However, according to what we have established above, the *Tur’s* challenge to the Ramban is resolved.

***

Now, for the sake of completeness, we must clarify one more question: It is true that according to the above, the language of the *Zohar* and the *Tolaas Yaakov* regarding the burning of the *Shtei HaLechem* is resolved, as their intent is only regarding the sheep, which are considered like the body of the *Shtei HaLechem*. But the matter is still not fully settled: since according to the interpretation of the *Tolaas Yaakov* (and one may interpret the intent of the *Zohar* similarly), the offering of *chametz* on Shavuos hints at the fact that in the World to Come, the body, like the soul, will also delight in the radiance of the *Shechinah*, then the law of the *Shtei HaLechem* should have been that it is offered from its actual body upon the Altar, just as the *mincha* of *matzah* that comes on Shavuos—which hints at the soul—is offered from its actual body upon the Altar.

To resolve this, we must provide a brief explanation regarding the relationship between the soul and the body.

The purpose of the creation of man, which is the purpose of all of creation, is to serve Hashem, as our Sages say (Mishnah, end of *Kiddushin*), "I was created to serve my Maker." This is achieved through the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos*, as our Sages expound (*Avodah Zarah* 3a) on the verse, "If not for My covenant by day and night (Torah), I would not have set the laws of heaven and earth." This is impossible for the soul as it is Above, but only when it descends and is enclothed in a body; therefore, the Torah was not given to the ministering angels, but to human beings (*Shabbos* 88b).

However, the participation of the soul and the body in the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* differs: the merit of the soul is that it is spiritual; it desires and yearns for Torah and *mitzvos*. The body, which is physical, conceals and hides the spiritual and is drawn specifically toward materiality, and only through its soul which is within it does it also desire the performance of Torah and *mitzvos*.

The merit of the body over the soul is that the soul cannot fulfill Torah and *mitzvos* except through the powers and limbs of the body.

(All this is hinted at—regarding the matter of transgression of Torah and *mitzvos*—in the words of our Sages (*Sanhedrin* 91a and elsewhere), who compared the soul to a lame person who cannot walk, and the body to a blind person who cannot see where to go, and only through their joining is a complete thing achieved. See also *Chullin* 7b: "The Jewish people are holy; one desires, etc., and one has, etc.")

Since the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* is impossible except through the joining of the soul and the body, therefore the reward for this in the World to Come is also specifically for souls in bodies, as our Sages say (*Berachos* 17b), "The World to Come has no eating or drinking, etc., but the righteous sit, etc., and enjoy the radiance of the *Shechinah*," meaning that the body will also be nourished by this radiance, like Moshe and Eliyahu—as stated in the aforementioned *Tolaas Yaakov*, and as the Ramban elaborated in *Shaar HaGemul*, where he disagrees with the interpretation of the Rambam (*Teshuvah* 8:2 and elsewhere) regarding this statement of our Sages.

For then the body will be so refined—see there in *Shaar HaGemul*—that it will be able to be nourished by the radiance of the *Shechinah*.

It is explained in *Chassidus* (*Torah Or*, Parshas Toldos, discourse entitled *Mayim Rabim*) that there will also be a change regarding the vitality, for there will then be a revelation of light so lofty that it will be able to animate even a physical body, unlike now, where the soul animates the body.

Now, in all of this, even in the World to Come, the joining of the soul and the body is necessary even regarding the vitality, for in order for this lofty light to animate the body in an orderly and gradual manner—and this vitality, which the *Tolaas Yaakov* writes will cause the body to delight in the radiance of the *Shechinah*, will be enclothed within it specifically in a detailed way—the vitality must pass through the soul and be measured by it, as all this is explained well in the discourse *LeChol Tachla* of the year 5659 (1899).

Thus, even in the future—to which Shavuos hints, according to the *Tolaas Yaakov*—there will be this distinction between the vitality that the soul will receive and the vitality that will reach the body, which will not come to the body except through the mediation and passage of the soul (though the vitality will not originate from it as it does now).

Therefore, the offerings that hint at both of them also differ: the *matzah*, which hints at the soul, is offered from its actual body—without an intermediary—upon the Altar, whereas the *chametz*, which hints at the body, is not actually offered upon the Altar. And this is well understood.

## 164

B"H, 4 Menachem Av, 5704

To the honorable Rav, the G-d-fearing man, a man of many accomplishments, whose lips guard knowledge, etc.,
Rabbi A. A. HaKohen, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

Surely your honor has already received, in due time, the first and second volumes of Kovetz Lubavitch.

In the "Responsa and Explanations" section of the third volume, I intend to include your honor’s questions regarding the apparent contradiction between the author of Chavas Daas and what is written in Tanya, as this is a matter of interest to many, as well as the question from the Rav of Toronto, etc.

Both will be included, along with what appears to me to be the answer to them. It is my desire, in accordance with the custom that has existed for some time, to print the question under the name of the one who asked it—that is, your honor. This will also serve as encouragement for many to engage in the study of Chassidic discourse.

Please be so kind as to inform us if you are in agreement with this.

My previous letter—and also the book *Sefer HaMoadim*—were surely received in due time.

With blessings for immediate Teshuvah and immediate Geulah,

## 165

B"H, Wednesday, 6 Menachem Av, 5704

To the distinguished and honored *avreich*, *yerei shamayim*, etc.,
Mr. Shalom, *sheyichyeh*,

Regarding your inquiry concerning the *Tanya*:

1) At the beginning of Chapter 2, regarding the phrase "And You breathed it into me" (*v'atah nafachta bi*): You noted that *nafachta* is written without a *hei*, even though in the *Siddur* it is written with a *hei* at the end (*nafachta*).

- This is also the case in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, Epistle 12, where it is written as it is in *Tanya*.

To sharpen the point, one could suggest that in *Tanya*, the intent is to provide proof that the *neshamah* is from the inner, essential vitality of the *Ein Sof*, *kiviyachol*. If the text were to use the version *nafachtah*—which implies "You breathed *it* [the soul]"—one could argue that the *neshamah* itself is not from the inner essence, but rather that the act of placing it into the body was done through a "breathing" that stems from the inner vitality of Above. (In truth, both are true: the act of placing it into the body and the *neshamah* itself are both from the inner essence, etc.; see the *maamar* "Vayigash" 5667, at the end of the *hemshech* 5666). Therefore, he wrote *v'atah nafachta*—meaning he explains that the *nefesh* is created from the "breathing" itself. The verse "And He breathed into his nostrils" (*vayipach b'apav*) is interpreted similarly: from the "breathing," the "soul of life" is created.

Conversely, in the blessing of *Elokai Neshamah*, which speaks of the *hishtalshelus* (process of descent) of the *neshamah* and the worlds through which it passed, it must be *nafachtah*—with a *hei*.

However, aside from this being somewhat forced, the Alter Rebbe wishes to bring proof for his words from other sources. In every place where the text of the blessing *Elokai Neshamah* is cited (e.g., *Berachos* 60b, *Rambam*, *Hilchos Tefillah* 7:3, and others), it is found as *nafachtah* with a *hei*.

(In *Pri Etz Chaim*, Shaar HaBerachos, Chapter 7, it is printed as *nafachta*—without a *hei*—but one cannot bring proof from this printing, as it is full of typographical errors. In that very same passage, it is printed *baratah* with a *hei*, and *yatzarta nafachta* without a *hei*. In *Shaar HaKavanos*—Jerusalem 5662 edition—it is *barata yatzarta* without a *hei*, and *nafachtah* with a *hei*. In *Machzor*, Seder Moed, *Maseches Chatzos* 2:4, all are with a *hei*.)

Therefore, it seems almost certain to me that originally it was written *nafachta* with a line over the *tav*, where the line represented the place of the *hei*. Often, this was done to avoid extending beyond the line. Later, the line was omitted due to a mistake or error by the typesetter.

A quasi-proof for this can be found in *Likutei Torah*—the corrected edition—in the *maamar* "Ki Sihyena L'Ish," where these chapters of *Tanya* are contained, and it is written *v'atah nafachtah* with a *hei*.

(In the first edition of *Likutei Torah*, Zhitomir 5608, it is also *nafachta* without a *hei*, but many typographical errors were found in it in subsequent editions.)

***

Note: There is another difficulty in *Tanya* here—it is surprising that you did not note this in your letter as well—and that is the word *v'atah* with a *vav*, even though the standard text is *atah* without a *vav*.

Seemingly, one could say that the *vav* does not belong to the word *atah*, but rather it comes to add another proof to the previous one, as if it were written: "And another proof from what is written: *Atah*, etc." However, in *Likutei Torah* (ibid.), one cannot say this, since it begins with *v'atah nafachtah bi*, and the word *v'atah* is repeated again later; see there.

It is possible that this is according to the *Rambam* (ibid.), whose version is *v'atah nafachtah bi*. In the *Otzar HaTefillos* commentary, *Iyun Tefillah*, it is noted that this is also the version in *Machzor Vitry*, in the Yemenite *Siddurim*, and in the Roman *Machzor*.

But the matter itself requires explanation: Why did he change the text in *Tanya* and *Likutei Torah* to adopt the *Rambam's* version, given that *halachah l'maaseh*—in his *Siddur*—he ruled to say *atah* without a *vav*?

Perhaps it can be explained based on what is clarified in *Chassidus* (the *Peirush HaMishnayos* of the Tzemach Tzedek, printed in Vol. 2 of *Sefer HaMitzvos*, in the *maamar* "V'Atah Tetzaveh," and at greater length in the *maamar* "Atah Hu D'L'Vadecha" 5701): that the addition of the *vav* in the word *v'atah* indicates an *hemshech* (drawing down) from a place that is extremely lofty, the illumination of the *Kav*, etc.

Consequently, it is understood why in our version regarding *baratah, yatzarta, nafachtah*, we say *atah*, and only regarding the matter of *shemirah* (guarding) is it said *v'atah* with the addition of a *vav*. For the "Guardian" is greater than that which is "guarded." Therefore, both from the perspective of the illumination from Above, the *shemirah* comes from a higher place than the level of *atah nafachtah* (*Hosafos L'Torah Or*, *maamar* "Lehavin Inyan Simchas Chochmah," end of section 3), and from the perspective of the *neshamah*, *v'atah* refers to the level of the *neshamah* that is clothed in the body, whereas *v'atah meshameirah* refers to the essence of the *neshamah* (*Likutei Torah*, *be'ur* to *maamar* "Levavtini," end of section 2). Nevertheless, *v'atah meshameirah* refers to the time the *neshamah* is in the body in this world, which is the level of *nafachtah bi*, as is the simple explanation of this blessing. For the connection of the illumination of the *neshamah* and its essence, as well as the revelation of the level that guards, etc., is specifically in this physical world.

See the *maamar* "Vayigash" at the end of *hemshech* 5666, and the *maamar* "Atah Hu D'L'Vadecha" 5699 on this. There it is written that *v'atah meshameirah* is from the "Great Circle" (*Igul*) that is before the *Kav*. This requires further study in light of the aforementioned *maamar* "Atah Hu" 5701, which writes that it is from the *Kav*. It may be that the addition of the *vav* hints at an extremely lofty *hemshech*, and in every place, it is according to its context: in the *maamar* of 5699, which discusses *v'atah meshameirah*—and all *shemirah* is from the *Makif*—the *hemshech* is from the *Igul*, etc.; and in the *maamar* of 5701, which discusses *v'atah mechayeh es kulam*—and *chayus* (vitality) is from the *Pnimiyus*—the *hemshech* is from the *Kav*. There is more to say on this, but this is not the place.

Therefore, in *Tanya* and *Likutei Torah* (ibid.), which primarily discuss the level of the *neshamah* that is clothed in the body (see *Likutei Torah*, *be'ur* to *V'Lo Tashbit*, section 1), he brought the version *nafachtah bi*. And in wishing to also hint at the essence of the *neshamah*—especially since the essence of the *neshamah* is connected with the level that is clothed in the body, for it is a *Makif* upon them specifically and guards them—he used the version *v'atah* with the addition of a *vav* to hint at *v'atah meshameirah*, which is connected specifically with *nafachtah*, as mentioned above.

See *Likutei Torah*, beginning of *Parshas Haazinu*, which writes *Atah baratah, atah yatzarta, v'atah nafachtah*. However, I do not know how precise that printing is regarding such *chaser* and *yaseir* (defective and full) spellings.

***

2) In *Sefer Shel Beinonim*, Chapter 24: "And that which *pikuach nefesh* overrides other prohibitions, and even 'transgress and do not be killed'—this is according to the explanation of our Sages: 'The Torah said, desecrate one Shabbos for him so that he may keep many Shabbosos,' and not because of the lightness or severity of the prohibitions... rather, it is a *gezeiras hakasuv* (a scriptural decree)."

You noted: What is added by the matter of "transgress and do not be killed"? If you say that the term "*pikuach nefesh* overrides" implies permission, and he comes to add that "even *ya'avor* (transgress), etc." means that it is an obligation to transgress, then this law depends on a dispute among the *poskim*, and surely our Rebbe would not enter into a *machlokes*.

If this is the concern, it is not a difficulty: a) In many places, the term "overrides" (*docheh*) implies an obligation, such as "a positive commandment overrides a negative commandment," "impurity is overridden in a communal offering," "the first *Pesach* overrides the Shabbos," and the like.

b) Regarding the law that some say one is permitted to be stringent with oneself and be killed in other prohibitions, this is specifically when the non-Jew intends to force him to apostatize—where, aside from the act of this prohibition, there is also the matter of the religion in general, as the *Yerushalmi* (*Sanhedrin* 3:5) says: "If a Jew is a Jew..." But regarding the performance of the prohibition alone—i.e., when the non-Jew intends for his own benefit—even according to the *Tosafos* and those who follow his view, it is forbidden to be stringent, as written in *Mishneh Lamelech* (*Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* 5:4), and so it is ruled in *Shulchan Aruch Yoreh Deah* 157:1 (and the sources cited there). If so, in the case at hand in *Tanya*, he is well-justified in bringing proof from the fact that according to all, one must "transgress and do not be killed" in the performance of other prohibitions for one's own benefit.

Now, there is more to note in *Tanya* here, namely: If the proof that "even *ya'avor*, etc." adds to what was said that "*pikuach nefesh* overrides, etc.," he should not have brought the matter of "*pikuach nefesh* overrides, etc." at all. It must be that there is something in one proof that is not in the other.

Perhaps it can be resolved as follows: The term and concept of "*pikuach nefesh* overrides, etc.," which is brought in the *Shas* (*Yoma* 85a and there), is like the law of "a positive commandment overrides, etc.," meaning: a) The act is a *mitzvah*, such as clearing away the debris, but it occurred at a time of prohibition or regarding a prohibited item, as is the law for a sick person or one seized by *bulmus* (extreme hunger), and as the *Shas* compares it there to *milah* on Shabbos.

(See also *Tosefta Shabbos*, end of ch. 17: "Rabbi Yehuda said: From where do we know that *pikuach nefesh* overrides Shabbos? As it is said, 'You shall keep My Shabbosos.' One might think this applies to *milah* and *avodah* and *pikuach nefesh*...")

b) It is a complete obligation to save a life and override the Shabbos or other prohibitions, etc.

The term and concept of "*ya'avor v'al yeihareig*" (transgress and do not be killed) is: a) The act is a transgression, such as cutting *asfasta* (*Yevamos* 121b) and the like, but it occurred (or the non-Jew) connects this with the killing of a soul. b) There is no clear implication of obligation in this term, which is why room remains for the dispute among the *Rishonim*, as mentioned above.

Therefore, the *Tanya* brought both matters—*pikuach nefesh* and *ya'avor v'al yeihareig*—to strengthen the difficulty and prove simply: a) That overriding the prohibition is an obligation—from *pikuach nefesh*; b) That the overriding is not specifically when the act is a *mitzvah* in itself—from *ya'avor v'al yeihareig*.

This still requires study from *Kesubos* (19a), which says "*pikuach nefesh*" even though there is no *mitzvah* there. It may be said that regarding another's life, where it is not applicable to say that one is permitted to be stringent upon another even though according to the law he should "transgress and do not be killed," in any case, it is considered a matter of a *mitzvah* ("Do not stand idly by the blood of your neighbor," etc.). Only regarding oneself is there a distinction, as mentioned above, since one could say he is permitted to be stringent upon himself.

I have seen in *Sefer Minchas Chinuch*, *Mitzvah* 296, that he explains that "*pikuach nefesh* overrides, etc." adds two details: a) It overrides even if the danger does not come through performing the *mitzvah*, such as one seized by *bulmus*; b) That even for the *pikuach nefesh* of another, one should transgress, such as clearing the debris for him. Perhaps the language of the *Tanya* can be explained simply: "And that which *pikuach nefesh* overrides other prohibitions, and even (in a place where the overriding is not so clear, such as regarding oneself, in all this the law is) 'transgress and do not be killed,' etc."

This still requires further study.

***

3) You noted the reason why our Rebbe (ibid.) brought "desecrate one Shabbos for him so that, etc."—which is from *Shas Yoma* (85b)—since he concludes that it is a *gezeiras hakasuv*, why is the reason of "desecrate one Shabbos for him, etc." needed?

It can be said that the Rebbe's intent is that it is a *gezeiras hakasuv*—since he equates a betrothed maiden to a murderer (*Sanhedrin* 74a)—that regarding *gilui arayos* (forbidden relations), they do not say this logic that one should override once so that he may keep it many times, and not because of the severity of the sin. And in that which the Torah said "desecrate one Shabbos for him so that, etc.," the Rebbe explains that the Shabbos is not overridden by another matter and because it is not so severe, but rather that the *mitzvah* of Shabbos itself—that he should keep it many times—overrides the keeping of the Shabbos this one time. And *halachah l'maaseh*, this reason is needed, as well as the reason of "live by them and not die by them" (*Yoma* ibid.), for from "live by them" we learn that even a *safek pikuach nefesh* overrides other prohibitions. And in the law of one who wants to remove his daughter from the Jewish people, etc., the reason of "desecrate one Shabbos for him, etc." is needed (*Shulchan Aruch HaRav*, *Orach Chaim*, end of 306, and see there in the *Kuntrus Acharon*).

***

Note: Our Rebbe ruled in *Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim*, end of 306, that one who is forced to worship idols one time, we do not desecrate the Shabbos for him with a Torah prohibition to save him from this... because the Shabbos was not given to be overridden for the sake of saving from a prohibition more severe than it. He follows his own view in *Tanya* here, where he wrote that it is not because of the lightness or severity of the prohibitions.

And even according to the opinion (*Levush*—brought in *Mishbetzos Zahav* 306:5) that we do desecrate Shabbos to save him one time from those prohibitions for which one must be killed and not transgress, it can be said that he also does not disagree with our Rebbe's view in *Tanya*, but rather he holds that these three prohibitions have the status of *pikuach nefesh*, since the Torah obligates him to be killed for them—as all this is written in *Sefer Ohalei Yosef*—to the student of the Alter Rebbe—*Dinei Kiddush HaShem*, sections 22-23.

Note: It should be studied whether what our Rebbe wrote, that it is a *gezeiras hakasuv*, applies also to *shefichus damim* (murder) or only to *gilui arayos* and *avodah zarah*.

See *Kesef Mishneh*, *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* 5:5.

Likewise, it should be studied if the verse were silent, what would logic dictate: that for all *mitzvos* one should be killed and not transgress, or that for all *mitzvos* one should transgress and not be killed? See *Tosafos* s.v. "Ben Noach" (*Sanhedrin* 74b), *Rambam Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* beginning of ch. 5, *Ra'am* on *Shemos* 4, *Parshas Derachim*, beginning of *Drush* 2, and others. This is not the place.

***

4) You noted regarding what is written in Chapter 30: "That this love is called *ratzon d'liba* (will of the heart), as mentioned above," whether "as mentioned above" refers to Chapter 17 or later in Chapter 40.

- I have seen in writing (a copy, not the original manuscript) the *Hagahos* of the Tzemach Tzedek on the *Tanya*, and on "as mentioned above" he did not note anything, but on the words "and *meruta d'liba*" (desire of the heart) that follow, it is noted: "See Chapter 17 and *Iggeres HaKodesh* 4."

One should add a question to your question: a) "As mentioned above" is surely not a mere citation that in another place the Rebbe wrote that such love is called *ratzon d'liba*, for what benefit is there in this knowledge?

Surely he is pointing to the place where the matter is explained more fully, and it must be what the text adds for us in Chapter 17 over what is written here. b) What is the practical difference if this love is called *ratzon d'liba* or not?—and this question applies also to Chapter 40.

What seems to me in all this is that "that this love is called *ratzon d'liba* as mentioned above" refers to what is written in Chapter 17 and not in Chapter 40. For aside from the fact that everywhere in *Tanya*, it seems, what is written in later chapters is referred to as "later" (*lekaman*) and not "as mentioned above," furthermore, in Chapter 40, he speaks of the natural love where the action of the intellect was only to bring it from concealment to revelation. Conversely, in Chapter 30, he speaks of love and fear that are drawn from the intellect. Also, in Chapter 17, he speaks of love born from contemplation, not the love of *mesiras nefesh* which is in the nature of all Jews; see there.

And even though in Chapter 17 he speaks of a very low level of love, as he writes there: "that this matter is very close and easy for every person, etc.," and here in Chapter 30 it is the level of the souls of *tzaddikim* who serve Hashem (and see what is written on this in *Kuntrus HaAvodah*, section 1), nevertheless, the common denominator between them is that both come from contemplation of the greatness of the *Ein Sof, Baruch Hu*. And regarding this, he brings reinforcement from Chapter 17: there he wrote that the love which is in the "hidden recesses of the heart" is *ratzon d'liba*. If so, *a fortiori* here—in Chapter 30—where the love is in revelation, it is called *ratzon d'liba*. And since such love is called *ratzon d'liba*, from this there is proof that the dwelling place of these *tzaddikim* is in the World of *Beriah*—for this is what is discussed here—because in the *Zohar*, *Vayakhel* (apparently it is what is written on page 210b), it is stated that from *ratzon d'liba* a garment is made for the *neshamah* in the Garden of Eden, and the Garden of Eden is the World of *Beriah*.

This is the proposed explanation of the *Tanya*, in my opinion, and if I have erred, you will correct me.

This is also what he emphasizes in Chapter 40, that it is called *ratzon d'liba* (and he also adds the word "in the *Zohar*"), because from this there is proof for what he concludes there: "And their place is in the *Heichalos* of *Beriah*," as mentioned above.

***

Note: To reconcile what is written in different places explaining the matter of *ratzon d'liba*, it must be that there are several levels of *ratzon d'liba*: a) The lowest level: *ratzon d'liba* that is in the hidden recesses of the heart and in the externality of the heart.

This is a novelty, because in the vast majority of the discourses of *Chassidus*, it is explained that *ratzon d'liba* is in the interiority of the heart. But it must be that the aforementioned level is also *ratzon d'liba*, from what is written in *Sefer Shel Beinonim* Chapter 17, that this matter is very close, etc., and the mind rules, etc., over the left chamber of the heart, which of course all relates to the externality of the heart. This is also indicated somewhat by *Likutei Torah*, *maamar* "Mi Manah," end of section 2, which writes: "There is a level of *ratzon d'liba* which is the will of the heart, and there are two levels in this: interiority and externality" (unless one is forced to interpret the word "in this"—that it refers to "the heart" or "the will of the heart" and not to *ratzon d'liba*). This is also indicated in *Likutei Torah*, *maamar* "Hinach Yafah," end of section 2, by his explaining that *ratzon d'liba* means when the will is revealed in the heart—and this is applicable even in the externality of the heart.

- And there it is written that it must spread and be revealed in the heart literally. And so that it does not contradict what is written in Chapter 17, perhaps it can be said that since the love in Chapter 17 comes from the mind to the heart until it is said regarding this "and in your heart," and it leads to the actual performance of *mitzvos*, this too is called revelation.

And that which in most *maamarim* it is explained that *ratzon d'liba* is specifically in the interiority of the heart, it can be said that the intent is to the *ratzon d'liba* in the *Zohar* passage: "For the *Zohar* does not mention anything except that he is grasped in *ratzon d'liba*."

b) In the externality of the heart and in revelation, and this is what is brought in Chapter 40, since he did not explain there that it must be a revelation specifically in the interiority of the heart.

c) In the interiority of the heart—and from what is written in *Kuntrus HaAvodah*, section 1, it is implied that even the *ratzon d'liba* of Chapter 30 is in the interiority of the heart—as is written in other places (*Torah Or*, *maamar* "Vayeshev" and many others). In the *drush* of *Parshas Matos* 5673, three modes of this very thing are explained: a) *Ratzon d'liba* that comes from contemplation of the wonder of the *Ohr Ein Sof*—this is the level of love in delights (*ahavah b'taanugim*) (see *Iggeres HaKodesh*, beginning of Epistle 18), which is the level of interiority, and as is known, the interiority of the *Makif* is the level of interiority, meaning that it is in the state of yearning to delight in Hashem. b) The contemplation is on the matter of the wonder of the *Ohr Ein Sof*—then the *hispaalus* (arousal) is above intellect and reason, where the intellect is lost at the time of the *hispaalus*. c) *Ratzon d'liba* which is from the side of the Essence, not through attainment and contemplation at all, the *Makif* of *Yechidah*, where it is not related to the intellect at all, such as the *avodah* of *Rosh Hashanah* and *Yom Kippur* and the Ten Days of Repentance.

He also wrote there that in any case, *ratzon d'liba* is the essential will, and the intellect is only to bring about the revelation of this will. Perhaps with this, one can say that it will not be difficult from the *maamar* "Lech Lecha," end of section 6, which implies there that only in the third mode—*ratzon d'liba* which is from the side of the Essence—is he grasped in *ratzon d'liba*.

***

5) Regarding what is "the *Imrei Binah* in *Sefer Tzava'as HaRivash*" that were mentioned and explained in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, Epistle 25. - This section from the aforementioned book is copied below, and see also what relates to this in *Sefer Beis Rebbe*, Vol. 1, Chapter 12.

***

With the blessing of "Immediately to *Teshuvah*—immediately to *Geulah*,"

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

The explanation is that every thing is from the expansion of the Holy One, blessed be He, through His *middos* (attributes), love and fear, as is known. Only that love is in exile, clothed in physicality, in a woman or in foods. And when a person takes to heart that this love is a garment for Him, may He be blessed, and *chas v'shalom* he strips Him, may He be blessed, of His garment, he will tremble with a great trembling when remembering the evil deed he did, and he will be ashamed and humiliated. And he will say in his heart: "If I love this, which is only love that fell into the *shevirah* (shattering), clothed in a putrid drop, how much more should I love Him, may He be blessed!" And likewise in fear: if he fears a non-Jew or a sword, he will say in his heart: "What is there to fear from a person like me? Rather, surely He, may He be blessed, the Creator, is clothed in this person, and how much should I fear Him, may He be blessed!" And likewise *Tiferes* and all the rest. And likewise, if he hears someone speaking while he is praying, he should say: "Why did Hashem bring this person here to speak during my prayer? Is not all this by Divine Providence? Rather, the speech is the *Shechinah*, and the *Shechinah* rested in the mouth of this person so that I should strengthen myself for *avodah*. How much should I strengthen myself in *avodah*, which is prayer!" And especially if the person speaking is a non-Jew or a child, it is found that, *kiviyachol*, the *Shechinah* is in such a person; how much is it fitting for him to act with alacrity!

## 166

B"H, Thursday, 14 Menachem Av, 5704

The esteemed and exalted Mr. Zvi Palmer,

Greetings and blessings!

First, I would like to apologize for not having responded to your letter until now, due to circumstances beyond my control.

a) I agree with you that each of us has his own "glasses" through which he views everything. I only do not understand why you think this is the case only when looking at the past. I believe that one applies these "glasses" much more when observing the "present," simply because the present touches one’s own life more directly, making it harder to evaluate it according to the pure truth without mixing in personal biases and self-love.

b) What is the advice for avoiding the mistake of one's own "glasses" when evaluating an event from the past?

The surest way is to immerse oneself in the words of the one who recounts the event, who lived, approximately, in the same era. And if that is insufficient, to use the explanations of the people who were closest to the time of the event.

Therefore, in order to correctly understand the meaning of the words of R' Shimon ben Elazar (Shabbos 130a) that "Yisrael did not sacrifice their lives [for tefillin]..." I paused over a matter that—at first glance—is entirely incomprehensible in the Gemara there. R' Shimon states what seem to be very clear words: 1) That for tefillin, one did not sacrifice one's life to the point of death, and 2) That tefillin are "weak" (merufa) among Jews. So why does the Gemara need explanations for this, such as "For R' Yannai said..." etc.? One should have simply looked at what was happening among Jews regarding tefillin; one would have seen whether it was weak or not. And if R' Shimon relates that "they did not sacrifice their lives," what does the story of Elisha Baal Kanafayim add to strengthen R' Shimon's account? (When the Gemara brings a proof, it is to strengthen an opinion, an interpretation, or an explanation, but not to prove that what a Tanna or Amora relates actually happened in that specific way).

Therefore, one must arrive at an explanation for this. I utilized the explanations of Rashi or Tosafos—who lived 500–600 years removed from the Gemara and who received the interpretations of the *peshat* of the Gemara through R' Gershom Meor HaGolah, etc., from Bavel, where the Shas was written. According to this, it becomes clear why the Gemara brings: "For R' Yannai said..." etc.—it is to understand R' Shimon's words not according to the superficial meaning, but according to the true meaning: 1) In what way was the "not sacrificing their lives to the point of death" expressed, and 2) What is meant by "weak among them."

—All as brought in the previous letter.

c) I brought the statement of R' Shimon as an illustration of: 1) What kind of dedication the mitzvah of tefillin requires of a person, and 2) How, at a time when it meant certain death, they nevertheless fulfilled the mitzvah, to the extent that some would wear them even in the street and for the entire day (see previous letter).

d) You write: In America, everyone holds by *milah*, but many do not fulfill the mitzvah of tefillin. You reason: This is what R' Shimon meant—that *milah* is not "weak," but tefillin is indeed "weak."

As stated, in my opinion, based on the commentaries of people who were closer to us than the time of R' Shimon by 900 years, R' Shimon means "weak"—feeble, i.e., not as strong as it should have been, as written above.

However, I can agree with you on the following, which I will clarify with an example: People are walking together on a path. One person steps off the path in the dark and wanders until, after some time, he realizes that he is in the middle of a forest where no path, person, etc., is visible.

How did this happen? One does not jump directly from the middle of a major road into the depths of a dense forest. Rather, first he stepped off the path by an inch, then another inch was added, and with time, one is in a deep, dense forest. The same applies to our matter:

From the fact that in the times of R' Shimon, people were weak—in that they wore tefillin at home but did not go to their deaths to wear them in the street all day—perhaps, inch by inch, the current situation among some Jews came to be.

But I ask you: If one looks at the intermarriages of a certain segment of people, at the movement in the form of a priest and rabbi on Shabbos and Sunday, at the seminaries/yeshivos where one learns about both rabbi and priest together, at the rabbi whom hundreds of thousands of American Jews consider a leader, and who gifts away a menorah that had been in a beis medrash for hundreds of years—to a church—and boasts about it in all the newspapers and remains a leader—if you were to gather all the "champions" that America possesses in these areas—what will remain of the Jewish people among our children?

With best wishes and with the blessing of *la'alter l'teshuvah — la'alter l'geulah*,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson

You mention in your letter the French philosopher Voltaire—his life, what he preached, and his personal conduct *bein adam l'chaveiro* (between man and his fellow) is one of the best illustrations of how much one must have the harmony of head and heart (desires)—as indicated in the previous letter—and what it leads to if that harmony is absent.

167

B"H, 21 Menachem Av, 5704

Rabbi Moshe Yehuda Tzeachoval, may he live,
Refugee Camp, Oswego, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings,

Surely you have received the telegram from our Nasi, the Rebbe, may he live, and the Yiddish copy is enclosed herewith.

As Chairman of the Executive Committee of Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch and Machne Israel, I hereby reiterate our blessings—blessings of peace upon you, and welcome—in the name of all our colleagues.

By order of our Nasi, the Rebbe, may he live, we have sent to you, in the name of the camp office, a package of textbooks and reading material, as well as the monthly publication *HaKriah VeHaKedusha*, which will serve as a proper foundation for a library at the camp’s beis medrash. You will surely see for yourself the great value and benefit of these printed materials.

As stated in the telegram from the Rebbe, may he live, to you, as well as in the telegram to the head of the camp, and in the telegrams sent from the offices of Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch and Machne Israel, we wish to send a representative from these institutions to visit you and our fellow Jews, the refugees, to ascertain on the spot how we can extend a helping hand and provide assistance to our fellow refugees in the camp in the field of chinuch and the strengthening of Yiddishkeit in general.

The aforementioned central institutions, which stand under the leadership of the Rebbe, may he live, have already succeeded, with the help of Hashem, in accomplishing great and wondrous things in the U.S. and Canada by establishing kosher chinuch institutions and publishing textbooks and reading material in the spirit of Torah in Lashon HaKodesh, Yiddish, and the language of the country. We have great confidence that in your camp, too, we will be able, with the help of Hashem, to accomplish much in this regard.

Regarding the head of the camp, Mr. Smart, whom we have approached officially to permit our visit as soon as possible—he is surely occupied with various matters. It would be appropriate for you to approach him on your end, in the name of all the refugees, to urge him to expedite the permit for our visit to the camp, so that we may speak with you at length regarding all the aforementioned matters that concern the welfare of our fellow refugees.

We hope to hear from you soon. Best regards to you and to all our fellow Jews in the camp, with feelings of blessing and affection,

Rabbi Menachem Schneerson
Chairman of the Executive Committee of
Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch and Machne Israel

## 168

[10 Elul, 5704]

I enjoyed reading your essay, and seeing that you are a thinker regarding such profound matters, and that you take an interest in them with such strong intellectual focus.

My thanks to you for presenting your ideas to me and for your request that I share my thoughts on the matter.

Due to the many pressures in connection with the preparations of the *Mahlach* for the upcoming school term, the response has been delayed until now, and I ask for your forgiveness.

The matters are extremely deep, and human language is poor in its ability to exhaust these concepts precisely and in their entirety; therefore, several details remain unclear and leave room for doubt.

Without intervening in the specific details of the matters in your essay, I will address the general lines within it, and after I present the conclusion of your essay, I will offer what appears to me on the subject.

Your idea: Every single thing exists initially in the state of a "seed." Afterward, it develops and grows until it reaches its perfection—according to its current level. But this perfection of its level is also nothing more than a "seed" (perhaps "prelude and preparation" is better than "seed") in relation to the level to which it must ascend. Thus, it rises from level to level, unites with other bodies (which have also passed through the *Seder Hishtalshelus* in a similar way), and the participation of all of them is a "seed" (form) from which a complex body grows. This complex body serves as a "seed" for a more developed body, and so on. At a certain level of development—within this order of levels—we find man. And here we find a new power: spirituality and free will to walk and conduct oneself according to the laws of "growth" or to rebel against them.

There are several details that remain unclear in your presentation:

a) Since free will exists only in man, and in all "the rest," everything occurs according to natural laws of development and growth, it follows that the free will in man makes him deficient in relation to all other existing things: for they all move only forward, whereas man sometimes regresses due to his power of choice.

b) You did not clarify what the "thing" is that develops and grows. For example: one plants a seed; this seed itself grows and becomes a tree bearing sour or small apples. But from the seed of this tree grows a tree whose apples are sweeter or larger.

Now, it is impossible to say that the first seed developed into a better seed, since the first seed is no longer in existence. It is impossible to say that the "species" of apple trees developed, since a "species" does not exist in reality; it is merely a conceptual construct that our intellect uses in its perception of the creation around it, to facilitate comprehension and understanding by organizing all "phenomena" into categories and species according to various signs and rules. Is it your intention to say that "nature" developed—that previously there was no reality other than a tree whose fruits were sour, and through perfection, "nature" grows a tree whose fruits are sweet?

c) You did not clarify what the criterion is by which we know what is development and what is regression.

For example: sweet apples versus sour apples—this is not an absolute development, but development solely from the point of view of human needs.

The sense of sight is sharp; it is not a sign of development except for a living creature that dwells in a place of light and needs to use its eyes. Conversely, for a creature that does not live in such conditions, it is actually a deficiency and a regression, since it provides no benefit and the eye requires "sustenance," attention, energy, etc.

It is known that for horses living underground in coal mines in England for many years, their sense of sight weakens from year to year. Likewise, many species of fish found in rivers and lakes beneath the earth in caves where light does not penetrate at all—they have almost no sight, or they have no eyes at all.

More generally: a more developed and complex body—according to our intellect—is a higher level on the ladder of perfection compared to a very simple body (a monkey versus a worm). But from the point of view of the body itself and its struggle for existence, it is the opposite: a simple body (a worm) can withstand cold and heat; when you cut it into pieces, each part lives on its own; its food and needs are simple and very common; there is almost no room for illness, etc.—none of which is true for a highly complex body.

d) According to your scheme, simple bodies develop at first each on its own, then they combine together, and this is an ascent in the perfection of each of them, and they become one (complex) body. And here the question arises: what or who is the great coordinator that "leads" each of the bodies at the beginning along their path of development in such a way that they will later complement one another?

It is fine regarding everything that belongs to this body itself; one could say that everything is included, as there is some connection between this and that in their essence. But it must be that this coordinating "power" is something outside of the two bodies and exists above them, and therefore it rules over both and leads them in a way appropriate to a goal that also exists outside of the two bodies.

***

Some premises regarding the above are founded upon the teachings of Chassidus:

a) *Hashem* created the world and all that fills it, and in every single thing there is a Divine spark that constitutes and enlivens it at every moment; were it not for the spark, it would return to nothingness and void, even though it is present within it in concealment and not in revelation (an example: the soul of a person is not seen, and its existence is known only through the necessity of the intellect).

b) The more the Divine spark is revealed and active in a certain thing, the closer that thing is—even in revelation—to its true state; thus, through this, the thing rises, develops, and becomes more perfected.

c) *Hashem* is the source of life and His essence, and therefore, the more the vitality is revealed, the higher it is on the ladder of development. Consequently, in general, the *tzomeiach* (vegetative) is above the *domem* (inanimate), the *chai* (living) is above the *tzomeiach*, and the *medaber* (speaking/human) is above the *chai*.

d) The Creator is completely free, for He is the One who created not only reality but also the laws of nature. Something akin to this—freedom and free will—we find only in man, and this is one of the proofs that man is higher in development than all other created beings.

e) If man, through his actions of free will, does the opposite of what is desired and appropriate—as mentioned above, meaning that through his actions he adds concealment to the Divine spark within him or within another thing (gluttony and drunkenness)—then not only has he not risen on the ladder of development, but he has actually regressed and brought down with him (the food and drink and the Divine spark within them); he has added destruction to the world.

But if, through his free will, he chooses the good deed, then not only has he risen in the level of development—which is found also in other created beings when they fulfill their role—but he has added from his own self, from his own good will, since he was free in his choice, a new matter of construction in creation—that is, a matter of creation. It is as if we say that the Creator gave man the power to create, as our Sages say (*Bereishis Rabbah* 98:2), "Yisrael (Yaakov) creates worlds."

***

If you have any comments regarding the above, I would be happy to hear from you, and I am ready at any time, etc.
